The author of this book, having completed for you, O sons, this book which is the entire science of medicine in its completeness, and having reached the peak of its clarity and expression, has seen fit to complete it for you with this article, which is a part of the manual work.
Because manual work is its excellence in our country and at our time, it is entirely absent, so much so that the study of its science has nearly ceased and its traces have disappeared. Only a few traces of it remain in the works of the early scholars, which have been altered by hand and whose reality is filled with errors and confusion, making its meanings obscure and its benefits distant.
I have therefore decided to revive it and compile this article, explaining, elucidating, and condensing, and presenting images of knives and other tools used in manual work, as this is an addition to the explanation and a means to convey what is needed with clarity and precision.
The reason there is no skilled manual worker in our time is that the practice of medicine is a lengthy endeavor, and one must first master the science of anatomy as described by Galen. So as to understand the benefits of the organs, their structures, temperaments, connections, separations, and knowledge of bones, nerves, muscles, their numbers, origins, arteries, tendons, and static and mobile vessels, and their locations.
Therefore, Albucasis said that many are called physicians but few truly are, especially in the art of hand surgery, of which we have mentioned some in the introduction to this book.
For he who is not knowledgeable of what we have mentioned regarding anatomy is liable to make errors that may kill people, as I have often seen many who pretended to this science without knowledge or understanding.
I have seen a physician, ignorant of anatomy, who incised a cyst on a woman's neck and damaged some neck arteries, causing the patient to bleed to death in his hands.
I have also seen another physician, who advanced to remove a stone from a man who had been stabbed in the urethra; as the stone was large, he panicked and removed it using part of the urinary bladder. The man died around three days later; I had been called to remove the stone, and upon seeing the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, this is what I estimated.
I have also seen another physician who, for a living, practiced medicine with some of our country's leaders.
I witnessed a doctor, in his ignorance, treating a young black man with a fracture near the ankle, along with a wound. The physician, without knowledge, tightly secured the fracture using ropes and casts, leaving no room for the wound to breathe.
Then he released him to his desires, and left him for several days, commanding him not to loosen the bandage until his leg and foot had swelled, and he was on the verge of death.
Then I was called to him, and I quickly loosened the bandage, so he found rest and recovered from his pains; yet the corruption had taken hold in the member, and I could not stop its progress.
The corruption continued to spread in the member until he died.
I also saw another physician who treated a malignant tumor; it developed an ulcer after several days, and the condition of the patient grew worse. This is because, if cancer is pure black humoral, it should not be treated with iron at all.
Unless it is in a member where the whole of it can be surgically removed.
For this, my son, you must know that manual work divides into two parts: the work that brings safety and the work that causes harm in most cases.
I have warned everywhere in this book about the work that involves risk and fear; you must be cautious and avoid it.
So that the ignorant do not find a way to speak or argue, take hold of yourselves with discipline and caution, and of your patients with gentleness and stability.
And employ the best path leading to safety and a favorable outcome, avoiding dangerous and difficult diseases.
Purify yourselves from what you fear may cast doubt upon your religion and world, for it is the stay of your honor and the elevation in both this world and the hereafter of your destinies.
As Galen said in one of his precepts: 'Do not treat a bad disease, lest you become bad doctors.'
This article is divided into three doors.
First Door.
In this door, we discuss the use of fire and sharp drugs, arranged from the ancient to the modern, with illustrations of the tools and instruments used for cauterization, and all that is needed for handwork.
Second Door.
In incisions, abscesses, phlebotomies, cupping, surgery, extraction of arrows, and similar procedures, all are categorised and arranged with illustrations of instruments.
**Chapter III**
In fractures, dislocations, treatment of internal injuries, and similar cases, all are categorised and arranged from the most recent to ancient times with illustrations of instruments.
**Chapter I**
**On burning**
Before discussing the procedure, it is necessary to mention the benefits and risks, and the temperament for which it is indicated.
I say that discussing the benefits and risks of burning is a lengthy and precise matter, a hidden secret, and many wise men have spoken about it.
They have differing opinions on this matter, and I have summarized their brief statements to avoid excessive elaboration.
I say that burning is beneficial in general for any bad temperament, whether with or without a material cause, except for two temperaments: the hot temperament without a material cause and the dry temperament without a material cause.
On the dry humours with matter, there is disagreement. Some say cauterization is beneficial, while others disagree, stating that cauterization is not suitable for a condition marked by heat and dryness, as fire's nature is heat and dryness.
It is impossible to cure a hot and dry disease with a hot and dry remedy, and those who disagree argue that fire cauterization may be beneficial for a hot and dry condition in the human body. Because when you add the human body and its moisture to the fire's humour, it makes the human body cold.
And I adhere to his saying, for experience has revealed this to me numerous times; however, one should not envision this unless they have been thoroughly trained and schooled in the art of cauterization. And he understood the diversity of human humours, the conditions of diseases within themselves, their causes, symptoms, and duration.
As for the other humours, there is no cause for concern, especially for cold and wet conditions. All physicians agree on this, and they are unanimous in their opinion that cauterization is beneficial for these conditions.
Know, my sons, that the secret of treating with fire and its superiority to other cauterizing agents lies in fire's single essence; it only affects the member that is cauterized, without harming another connected part, except slightly.
The use of cauterizing drugs may extend their effect beyond the affected member and potentially cause a condition in the organ difficult to treat or even death. Fire, for its honor and noble essence, does so only when excessively applied.
This has become clear to us through experience, due to the long service and care in the practice and observation of the realities of the matter.
For this reason, I have avoided lengthy speech, and if it were not unbecoming of this book, I would have presented you with a secret mysterious fire and the manner of its action upon bodies, expelling diseases through philosophical and logical discourse that would be profound for your understanding.
Know, O my sons, that there is disagreement regarding the time most suitable for cauterization, and they have designated the season of spring as the best time.
I say that cauterization may be appropriate at any time, for the harm caused by time passes into the benefit gained through cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is from essential, strong, and urgent pains that cannot tolerate delay, for fear of what follows them, a greater harm than what has already passed from the time.
Do not be misled, my sons, by what the general public and ignorant physicians imagine: the burning (cauterization) that heals from a certain illness will never return, and they make it into a permanent condition.
It is not as they thought, for cauterization is not like a medicine that alters the humor and dries up moistures that cause pain.
Cauterization, however, is superior to medicine in terms of its rapid success, strength of effect, and intense authority.
The illness may return at some point in time, depending on the patient's humor, the strength and severity of their condition, and what gatherings of impurities occur within their body, as well as their neglect of themselves through improper diet and other causes.
Except for the illness that requires cauterization, which is a gentle illness in a body part with few impurities and moistures. Such as the cauterization of a tooth for pain, it may not return, and this could be the least.
And the statement of the general public that cauterization is the last resort of medicine is correct in its opposite, for they believe that no treatment with medicine or otherwise can be effective after cauterization has taken place.
And indeed, the cauterization is the ultimate of medicine; for when we employ various treatments for a disease and they are ineffective, then we resort to the last remedy, cauterization, which if successful, becomes the ultimate of medicine. Not as the common understanding or the ignorance of many laypeople and untrained doctors goes.
The ancients mentioned that gold cauterization is better than iron cauterization, as they stated, for the moderation and nobility of its essence. They said it does not cause an infection at the site of application, far from that.
For I have tried it and found that it acts differently on different bodies; cauterization with gold is better and more preferable than iron, as they said, except that if you heat the iron in the fire, its redness does not indicate the exact temperature you desire.
Since it cools down quickly, and if you increase its heat during fever, it melts in the fire and becomes clumpy, leaving the practitioner in a state of distraction. That is why cauterization with iron has become for us faster and more suitable to the task, God willing.
I have organized this chapter on cauterization into sections, from head to toe, so that the student can easily access what they need, God willing.
Chapter One.
The operation on the head is one whole procedure.
This procedure is effective against the humidity and cold that affect the brain, causing headaches, frequent discharge from the head to the eyes and ears, excessive sleep, toothache, and throat pain. In general, it is useful for any condition that arises from coldness, such as paralysis, seizures, aphasia, and other similar ailments.
The procedure involves first ordering the patient to induce vomiting with a purgative that is gentle on the head, for three or four nights, depending on the patient's strength, age, and habit.
Then, he is commanded to shave his head with a razor, and place himself between your hands, seated and with his hands on his chest.
Then, you place the palm of your hand on the bridge of his nose between his eyes, and where your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then, hold the oil-infused iron (as illustrated).
If the bone exposes itself to the extent of a tooth's head or a grain of coriander, raise your hand. Otherwise, if it cools, apply the same iron or another one. Until you see what was mentioned to you from the bone, take some salt and dissolve it in water, then dip a cotton in it and place it on the wound, leaving it for three days.
Then, take a cotton dipped in butter and leave it on until the inflammation from the fire subsides. Then treat it with four-ingredient ointment until it heals, God willing.
They say that the wound, the longer it remains open, the more pus it produces, which is better and more beneficial.
Some mention that the skin should be burned to the bone, holding the iron until some of the bone's thickness is burned, then removing what has burnt and treating it afterwards.
Others say that the burning should be excessive, affecting the bone strongly until it falls out like amber or a small seed, and they claim that it releases vapors from the head in that area, leaving the wound open for a long time.
Then, it should be treated until it heals. I do not, however, ever use these types of burns on any but the most necessary occasions, and I consider it better to leave it alone if it does not cause immediate harm, for when the connection between parts is broken, as we have seen in other members, the head weakens.
Above all, when the patient's head is weak by nature, the first type of cauterization is safest and best for me, and it is this that I employ, with God's will, to achieve security.
Chapter II.
Regarding the head as well.
If chronic pain occurs in the head and persists for a long time, and treatments such as poultices, applications, plasters, and bandages are used without effect, especially if the same cauterization described has been applied without success, then consider the following. If the patient's head is strong in structure by nature and does not feel weak, and it experiences severe cold, apply another cauterization slightly above the previous one. Then apply cauterization to each of the head's horns until the skin thins and reveals the desired amount of bone, as described. Apply cauterization in the back of the head, at the location known as the occipital region, and be gentle with your hand in this area, as the patient will experience severe pain distinct from the discomfort of all other head cauterizations, which I will describe in its appropriate section.
The iron used to treat the horns of the head, both front and back, should be gentler than the iron used for the middle of the head. And this is its image.
Chapter III.
In the treatment of a non-chronic fracture.
If a fracture of the head causes pain, with a headache that radiates to the eye, and the patient vomits, then the treatment should begin with purifying drugs for the head, followed by all other treatments mentioned in the classifications of diseases. If this does not work, there are two methods of application of heat (or 'kāi') in this case. Either with a sharp, burning medicine, or with iron.
As for the application of heat with a burning medicine, take one clove of garlic, peel it and cut off both ends. Then, using a wide scalpel, make an incision in the affected area on the temple, creating a pocket beneath the skin that will accommodate the garlic clove.
It should be inserted beneath the skin until it disappears, then secured tightly with bandages and left for approximately fifteen hours. After that, remove the garlic and leave the wound to heal for two or three days before dressing it.
On it, apply a cotton ball soaked in lard to the affected area until it suppures, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If you prefer, you can do this with some burning drugs as mentioned in the eighteenth article on burning drugs.
And as for burning with iron, it is done in this manner: heat the iron in the shape depicted here:
It is called the "nailed iron" because its head resembles a nail with some ridges and a small protrusion in the center.
Then place it on the affected area and hold your hand while slowly rotating the iron; burn until half of the thickness of the skin is charred. Lift your hand to avoid burning the artery below, which could cause bleeding. Then soak a cotton ball in salt water and apply it to the area; leave it for three days. Then apply the cotton ball soaked in lard, followed by ointment until it heals, God willing.
If you prefer, cauterize this wound with the sharp end of the iron and be careful not to cut the artery in this non-chronic wound.
Chapter Four.
In chronic hernias.
If the treatment for hernia, as described above and in the classifications of diseases, is ineffective, and you observe a condition that cannot be remedied by the initial caustic application or by fire burning, then... Then the surgical knife should be heated until it becomes white, after marking the affected area with a line of half a finger or similar length, and once in place, tighten your grip to sever the artery and reach towards the bone.
However, you must be cautious not to burn the jaw joint that moves during chewing, which would damage the muscle or nerve controlling it, leading to spasms.
And be wary, for if the artery is cut, blood will flow, and cutting it entails risks. Especially for those who do not know what they are doing, and for whom this is an untried path, it is best to cease their efforts.
The treatment for bleeding from an artery, as it occurs on its face or in any other part, will be mentioned in detail in this book, God willing. If you observe a condition that cannot be remedied by this caustic application, then...
And I observed that the sick body was amenable to a cauterization in the middle of the head as we described, and I treated the wound until it healed, God willing.
If you wish, you may use the cauterization that we mentioned in the chapter on artery slaking with a two-bladed iron rod, for it is a better and more effective cauterization.
Chapter Five.
On the pain of the ears.
If a pain in the ear occurs due to cold and is treated with laxatives and other remedies mentioned in the divisions, but the pain persists, apply the iron rod, which is called the point, as illustrated here.
Then, after heating it, apply the iron rod around the ear (or both ears if the pain is in both) as it rotates. Move the cauterization (iron rod) slightly away from the origin of the ear after having marked the location with ink, and make the cautery about ten points in each ear or similar. Then treat the wounds until they heal, God willing.
Chapter Six.
In the strength of knowledge.
The strength that is treated with cautery is of the type that occurs from phlegm, as I mentioned in the classifications of diseases.
And one should avoid cautery of the type that occurs from dryness and nerve spasms.
When treatment with irrigations, snuffing, and gargling does not take effect, the patient should be cauterized three times. One at the base of the ear, the second slightly lower on the cheek, and the third at the junction of the lips.
And make your cautery from the opposite side of the affected area, because relaxation occurs only on the side that appears healthy.
The image of the cautery is to make a single cautery opposite the top corner of the ear, slightly below the temple, and another in the cheek, with a length equal to that of the index finger.
Lower your hand with the cautery to burn through half the thickness of the skin. This is an image of the iron, which is a type of knife as described previously, but slightly gentler. The knife should have a slight excess of thickness, then the site should be treated as mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter VII.
In the case of chronic apoplexy.
If the apoplexy becomes chronic and treated as mentioned does not improve, and there is no fever in the patient, apply four cups (as a treatment). Apply one cup on each side of his head, as mentioned, and one in the back of the head, following the previously described method.
The application of hot cups (mukai) should be as mentioned, and a cup can also be applied to the stomach opening, which is even better. Then, treat the site as previously described.
Chapter VIII.
On forgetfulness.
For one affected by phlegm, the patient should first be given warm drinks and grains that purify the brain.
Then he shaves all his head, and a poultice of mustard, as described in the article on poultices, is carried on his back, applying it repeatedly; for this is a form of cauterization.
Do the same as described there, and if he recovers, otherwise apply three cauterizations on his head, aligned from the top to the bottom of the neck.
Make a space between each cauterization, as thick as a finger, then treat the cautery with what has been mentioned previously. If you wish to increase it and the patient can tolerate it, apply another cautery in the middle.
If you wish to increase it, apply another cautery on the horns (of the head), then treat it until he recovers; and the iron should be olive-shaped, as described previously.
Chapter IX
For treating paralysis and relaxing the whole body.
One should begin by purifying the head with cauterization and what has been mentioned, then shave the patient's head, apply a cautery in the middle of the head, a cautery on each horn (of the head), and a cautery on its back.
Three on the vertebrae of the neck, if you need for the purpose of relaxing the body to more than that and the patient is able to withstand it and the disease is strong and persistent, make four burns on the vertebrae of his back. And continue with the burning until most of the skin is burned off and then raise your hand and treat it as mentioned earlier, so that it heals, God willing. The cauterizing iron should be olive-shaped.
Chapter Ten.
On seizures.
Only the seizure victim whose seizure is caused by phlegm should be cauterized, after first purifying his brain with powerful purgatives and other treatments mentioned in the sections, if the patient is old and able to endure medications.
But if he is a boy who cannot endure medications, then use gargles and brain-purifying chewing tablets for several days before, along with improving his diet.
Then shave his head and cauterize a single burn in the middle of his head as mentioned earlier, and another burn at the back. And on each side of his head, make a burn. If the patient is strong and tolerable, remove the burns as mentioned for the person with paralysis, relaxing the body along the cervical vertebrae and the vertebral column. The iron should be olive-shaped, as described previously.
If the patient is a child, make the iron gentle in this manner.
Chapter XI.
On the treatment of melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is putrefied humours and thick phlegm, remove the burns as mentioned for the person with paralysis.
If the cause of melancholia is a tendency towards black humours and the patient's body is filled with putrefied humours, then cleanse his brain as described in the previous division.
Then shave the patient's head. Make a compact cake from linen as round as a circle and place it in the middle of his head while he sits cross-legged. Take one pound of old sheep fat.
Then heat it over a moderate fire until the finger can tolerate it when inserted. Empty it into the circle in the middle of his head and leave it until it cools. Do this for the patient once a week, along with all his other treatments, until he recovers, God willing.
If you wish to apply heat, do so with many small droplets without holding the iron with your hand, but rather through a gentle warmth. For this type of heat moderates the brain.
Then place a cotton swab soaked in butter or chicken fat on it.
Chapter Twelve.
In the treatment of water entering the eye.
If you notice the onset of water entering the eye, as described in the previous sections, act promptly to relieve the illness and protect the head from all moisture. Bathe the patient in a hot sweat bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave the patient's head and apply heat to it with a hot iron in the middle of the head, then apply two heats on the temples, if water enters both eyes, or one side if it is only from one eye.
Then cut all the veins and arteries beneath the skin with the iron, making incisions that are long and wide across the temples, taking care not to cause blood loss.
If you notice any of it, cut it off at the base with any means available, and we will discuss later the wisdom of binding and cutting arteries while preventing blood loss.
A severe burn may be made on the nape of the neck between the two bones.
Chapter XIII.
In the treatment of chronic tears.
If the tears of the eye are chronic and persistent, resulting from veins and arteries located on the surface of the head outside the skull, and you have determined that this is due to a thick cold humour in the brain, then apply the cauterization described at the onset of the descending water. Cauterization in the middle of the head, two cauterizations on the temples, and two on the nape under the bones. If necessary, add a further cauterization on each side of the eye's tail at the edge of the eyebrow with a small iron.
Chapter XIV.
In the cauterization of the nasal cavity.
If the treatment as described in the previous section does not improve the condition, then promptly remove the ill person's coccyx for three nights, then shave his head and apply a cauterization to the central wound using an olive iron. Then apply the iron (maskar) twice above the eyebrows, slightly below the hairline, and be careful not to cut the artery, for this is the image of the maskar.
Chapter XV.
In the process of relaxing the eyelid.
If the eyelid relaxes due to illness or moisture, apply a single iron (halali) to the eyelid with this iron.
And if you wish, apply them above the eyebrows a little, two irons on each side, distancing them from the temples, and each iron should be as long as the eyebrow.
Do not exaggerate with the iron, but only apply it to the extent that one-third of the skin is burned, and this is the image of the iron.
Chapter XVI.
In the ironing of the eyelid.
If the eyelids turn inward, there are two types of ironing in this case. Either cauterization with fire or with a burning drug: if cauterizing with fire, the patient must first be instructed to let his beard grow, should he have one, until it is long and even.
If you trim it while it's still young, cover the patient's eyes with a bandage so they don't move until it grows and becomes even. Once it has grown and straightened, place the patient's head in your lap, then draw a mark on the eyelid with ink in the shape of an Asian maple leaf.
The mark should begin near the lashes, then place a cotton ball soaked in egg white or poppy seed extract under the eyelid. Heat the iron to this temperature.
Then, apply the heated iron to the marked area in several thin passes until the skin surface is burned, forming a shape similar to an Asian maple leaf. A successful mark will be evident as the eyelid puckers and the hair on the eyelid stands up.
Then lift your hand and leave it for three days. After that, apply a cotton ball soaked in lard to remove the burnt skin, and treat the area with ointment until it heals, God willing. If any hair grows back after some time and the eyelid relaxes, repeat the process.
Repeat the cauterization on that area as you initially did. If the hair is on the lower eyelid, burn it away until it returns to its natural position, taking care not to affect the eye with the flame. For cauterization with a burning drug, instruct the patient to let their lashes grow until they are long and even.
Then make a paper from the bark of the mulberry tree, and take soap and slaked lime, each weighing the equivalent of a dirham, or similar, and grind them together thoroughly, doing so swiftly to prevent drying out. Then spread it out on the paper made from mulberry bark, placing it over one or both eyes, and place under the eye a cotton ball soaked in egg white, with the patient's head resting on your lap.
Place your index finger on the medicine, press slightly, and move it as the patient feels the sting of the medication, for it will feel like fire. As long as they feel the sting, do not stop moving it. If the sensation subsides, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
Look, if you see the eyelid rising as it does when struck by fire or cut, then apply more of the medicine to the area that was not affected and did not turn black, until your work is complete. Then place a cotton ball greased with butter over the burned area until the sensation of burning disappears.
Then treat it with a palm butter ointment or similar, until it heals. When working, be extremely careful not to let any of the medicine fall into the eye, for if the eyelid relaxes after several days and there is any fear of hair entering the eye, it must be carefully cleaned.
Then prepare the medication for that area where the eyelid has relaxed, particularly as you did initially, and treat it until it heals, and this is a picture of the calamine leaf.
Be aware that people's eyes may differ in size and shape, so let your binding be appropriate to that, and the path to correctness is clear to whoever has experience in this craft.
Chapter XVII
On the treatment of the abscess that appears near the eye's socket.
If you treat the abscess as we described in the classifications of diseases and it does not respond to your treatment, then it should be cauterized in this manner.
Order the patient to place his head on your lap, and have a servant hold his head between your hands, gripping it firmly but not moving it or striking it.
Then place a wet cotton ball in the white of an egg or in the saliva of a lizard on his eyes, and heat the iron in the following manner.
It should be hollow, shaped like a tube like the feather of an eagle, with one end for the heat and the other either perforated or solid, but it is best for your work if it is hollow on both ends.
Then, if the fistula is open, squeeze it and extract the purulent fluid and dry it. If it is not open, open it and extract the pus, then place the iron on it while it is still hot, and hold your hand firmly until you reach the bone.
And move your hand slightly away from the eye towards the nose to avoid any mistake or distress to the patient, so that the iron does not fall into the eye and damage it. If you reach the bone with the first application of heat, otherwise, reheat the iron if necessary.
Leave it for three days, then apply a cotton ball with butter and treat it with a drying ointment until it heals. If it does not heal after forty days, apply a sharp, corrosive medicine to expose the bone and strip it as mentioned in its respective section, God willing.
Another method for treating fistulas, mentioned by some of the earliest practitioners, involves making a small incision at the site of the fistula and inserting a thin cone-shaped object into the same opening. This is depicted below.
Pour into it an amount of melted lead weighing the equivalent of a dirham, while holding the cone firmly with your hand. The patient must not move at all to prevent the molten lead from entering their eye. It is recommended to place a cotton ball soaked in egg white or water over the patient's eye.
Lead burns the site of the fistula and heals it in a remarkable manner. If the fistula is healed through the use of heat and treatment as mentioned, otherwise there is no choice but to use the nose hole and return the fistula to its path in the nose as will be discussed in its specific place.
Chapter XVIII.
On burning the fissure of the lip.
A fissure often occurs in the lip, known as a 'hairline', especially in boys' lips. If treating this fissure with the methods mentioned above does not work, then take a small, sharp iron (as illustrated) and apply it to the affected area.
The cavity should be as thin as the knife blade, then place a wheel over it to protect (it) and insert it into the fissure until the heat reaches its depth. Then treat it with a poultice until it heals, God willing.
Chapter XIX.
On burning the fistula occurring in the mouth.
If a tumor appears in the gum tissue, or on the soft palate, or at the base of the teeth, and dries up, bursts, and becomes a fistula producing pus, and treating it does not work, then you should heat an iron (tool) to fit inside the fistula.
Then, insert a protector into the fistula hole and hold your hand until the protected iron reaches the bottom and ends of it, and do this once or twice, then treat it as we mentioned until it heals, God willing.
If the treatment stops and it heals, otherwise, there is a need to examine the place and remove the rotten bone as mentioned in its section, God willing.
Chapter XX
In the treatment of teeth and relaxed gums.
If the gums relax due to moisture, the teeth move, and treatment with medication is ineffective, place the patient's head in your lap, then apply the heated iron, as depicted below, after placing a tube on the tooth.
Insert the heated iron with a wheel protector, hold your hand slightly until the patient feels the heat of the fire reach the tooth's root, then raise your hand and repeat the process as desired, after which the patient should rinse their mouth with salt water.
He should hold it for an hour and then spit it out; the loose teeth will be firmly set, relaxed gums will tighten, and corrupt moisture will dry up.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In cases of toothache.
If the toothache is caused by cold or has a worm and does not respond to drug treatment, there are two options: either apply heat with sesame oil or use fire.
The method with sesame oil involves taking melted cow sesame oil, boiling it in a cast-iron pan or shell, then taking a cotton wick, wrapping it around one end of a rod, immersing it in the hot oil, and applying it to the aching tooth with a turning motion until it cools.
Then repeat this process multiple times until the heat from the fire reaches the root of the tooth. Alternatively, you can dip a woolen thread or cotton in cold sesame oil and apply it to the aching tooth, placing a heated iron on top to ensure the heat reaches the base of the tooth.
For the method using fire, take a copper or iron tube, slightly thickened to prevent the heat from reaching the patient's mouth, and apply a heated iron to the aching tooth, holding it until the iron cools, repeating this process multiple times.
The pain may disappear either that same day or the next. After applying the heat treatment, the patient should fill their mouth with sweet sesame oil, hold it for a while, and then spit it out. This is the image of the iron.
Burn with any side you choose, according to what is possible. This is the shape of the tube.
Chapter XXII.
On the pig's carcase.
If the pig's carcase is affected by phlegm and cold humours, and does not respond to medicinal treatments for maturation, then quickly roast the hollow iron rod, as illustrated here, on both sides.
Let the smoke escape from the other end when roasting; place it protected over the same tumour multiple times, as needed, until you reach the depth of the tumour. If the tumour is small, adjust the size of the iron rod accordingly.
Then, leave it for three days and apply a cotton ball soaked in lard to remove any remaining burnt areas. Treat the wound with ointment and bandages until it heals, God willing.
Chapter XXIII.
On the roasting of those suffering from hoarseness and shortness of breath.
If moistures dominate the bronchial tubes, especially if accompanied by a cold temperament, then the patient should first be purged with laxatives. Afterwards, roast the affected area with an iron rod placed at the throat's opening, near the base of the neck.
Beware that the cautery does not reach the pharynx, and burn only half of the skin, then apply another cautery at the joint of the neck on the last bead, powerful, and let the iron be nail-like in the manner previously described.
Then treat it as mentioned above until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Fourth.

Chapter XXVII
Cold cauterization of the liver.
If a pain in the liver is due to cold, dampness, or putrid wind that exceeds its natural disposition and is treated as described in the previous sections without improvement, then other remedies should be considered. Chapter Twenty-Eight.
In the case of a tumor in the liver, use heat.
If a pus sac appears in the liver, to determine whether the tumor is in the liver tissue or in its capsule, it will cause the patient a sense of weight and pain, but not sharp pain.

This type of cauterization should only be used by those who have been well-trained in the art of medicine and have repeatedly experienced these diseases through trial. If they proceed with such an act, it would be better to leave it alone.
Chapter XXIX
On cauterization with a hot iron.
The earliest mentions of cauterization with a hot iron describe the use of the roots of dried, long-stemmed rhubarb, taking one root that is longer than the thickness of a finger, immersing it in oil, and heating it over fire. Then, apply a single cauterization between the clavicle and the neck, and two small cauterizations slightly below the jugulars, tilted towards the side under the beard. Also, apply two large cauterizations above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
Apply two cauterizations between the fifth and sixth ribs, tilted slightly backward, another in the middle of the chest, and one above the stomach. Additionally, apply three cauterizations from behind, one between the shoulders, and two on the sides below the ribcage, beneath the cauterization between the arms.
One should not deeply insert the cauterizing iron into the skin, but rather use it on the outer surface, as some of the earliest authors mention that people would use a flat iron-like tool, heating it and inserting it between the ribs until it reached the affected area.
And the condition will resolve, as we have mentioned, in the case of a liver tumor, and in this operation there is danger; either the patient dies immediately, or an incurable fistula develops at the site.

On the Spleen.

Chapter Thirty-Six.
On cauterizing the fistula that occurs in the seat (anal region) and its surroundings.

Beware of burning a nerve, if it exists, or a large vessel, if the fistula leads to the bladder or to the intestine.
Beware of these places, but only act upon them if the fistula is in a fatty area and you judge that it is not permeable. Then treat the site until it heals, for if the site closes up and no longer leaks and remains so for a period of time, then know that it has healed completely.
And if it no longer leaks, know that it is permeable or that there is a dead bone or something similar within it, as will be mentioned in its place.

Chapter Forty-One.
In the heat for sciatica.

A single burn can be made in the center of the hip, which would be four burns in total. Be cautious in all incisions to ensure that you do not cut through a nerve or major artery, as this may cause severe harm or complications to the patient.

Many times, then treat it with a ointment until it heals. And as for cauterization with burning drugs, it is to make two half-circular vessels into which the door of the firebox descends, made of copper or iron.
It has a height of about two fingers or similar, and it is open at the bottom and top, with some pieces held together in this manner.
Then lower them onto the right hip of the patient, who is lying on their healthy side, firmly gripping the hand. Pour water between the circles, cooled slightly over a small fire for about an hour, and the patient endures the stinging and burning sensation, finding it akin to fire.
Until the stinging subsides, then raise your hand with the instrument and wipe the sharp water off the hip entirely with sweet water. Leave it for three days, applying butter to it until the burn's blackness disappears, and let it heal for some days; this is most beneficial.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain spreads to the thigh or leg, make a mold for it to hold water in the same way as was done for the hip, as described in the article on preparing medicinal solutions.
Take from saltpetre and unquenched lime each a part, grind them, and put them in a new cauldron with a small hole at the bottom, large enough to admit the rod, and place under the base of the cauldron another cauldron sealed with clay.
Add to the saltpetre and lime water from fresh sources, immersing them with your finger after tightly gripping them together. Leave the cauldron until the strong water has drained to the bottom of the sealed cauldron, then gather all the water and pour it over fresh saltpetre and lime. Repeat this process.
It becomes then extremely sharp, useful in many medical procedures and for treating various members, as it acts like fire itself. Among the remedies are those that cauterize the hip, such as thyme, honey from plantain, and lime mixed with soap.

Tighten and leave for three hours or until the patient feels the burning has subsided. Then, immerse them in a bath, and if their body is moist, place them in a tub. The pain will cease and heal with God's permission. If it does not heal, repeat the dressing after ten days.

Chapter Forty-Five.
On the heat for hernias.

Then, the iron of this shape is heated.
Until a white cloth is applied, which scatters sparks, then the intestines or entrails are returned to their cavity, and a servant places his hand over the area to prevent the intestines from protruding, having separated them between the sick person's legs, and a cushion is placed under him, and another servant ties his legs.
And another servant holds his hands, then the iron is lowered onto the same mark, and your hand with the iron stands straight up, holding it until it reaches the bone or returns it if it did not reach the first one to the bone.
Be careful not to allow the intestines to protrude while applying heat, otherwise you may burn them, leading to the patient's death or great harm; know that the iron should be applied gently for children according to their size and firmly for adults according to theirs.

Chapter Fifty-Third.
On the Use of Reversed and Unreversed Nails.
This ailment often occurs in the soles of the feet, presenting as a rough, painful condition. It can be treated with heat, either by fire or hot water.

It is left for three days to form pus, then dressed with crushed herbage mixed with salt. The bandage is left for a night, after which the nail is removed from its root and the wound treated with a meat-promoting ointment until healed, if the nails are not reversed.
It often occurs on the surface of the body, particularly in the hands and feet. One should use a tube of copper or iron or a falcon's feather, applying it to the nail or verruca and then pouring a small amount of hot water into the tube.
Grip the hand and rotate it, slightly squeezing with your other hand to affect the margin of the tube at the base of the nail. Allow the sharp water to penetrate to the base of the nail, and let the patient bear the stinging sensation of the sharp water for a moment.
Then leave it, for the nail will come out at its roots. You should do this to each one until you have treated all of them in the body, then attend to their wounds after they have come out, using ointments that promote tissue regeneration.

And on his abdomen, make a burn with the olive iron, for the poultice will calm down and expedite the maturation of the cold illness.
Chapter Fifty-Five.
On the poultice occurring on the body.
When ugly boils appear in the body, caused by cold, dense, and corrupt materials, it is advisable, upon their first emergence, to burn each boil gently with a stick of ash whose end has been ignited, or with the root of long turmeric, or with a lentil-shaped iron.
Boils, upon their first appearance, should be treated in this manner so that they do not increase or spread, and the patient recovers completely, except that this should follow a let-down (phlebitis) procedure.
Chapter LXVI.
On burning (the method used) for stopping bleeding caused by cutting an artery.
Bleeding from an artery that has been severed often occurs due to a wound on the outside, or when a tumor is incised, or when a member (limb) is burned, and so on, making it difficult to cut off.
If such a thing happens to anyone, quickly move your hand to the mouth of the artery, place your index finger over it and apply pressure until the blood is contained beneath your finger and does not escape, then take several small and large burnishers (hot instruments), heat them in the fire until they are very hot, and blow on them.
Then take one, either small or large, according to the wound and the place where the artery has burst, and press the iron on the same vein after removing your finger with the wheel and gripping the iron until the blood stops.
If, when removing your finger from the artery's opening, the blood flows out and the iron cools down, take another iron from the pile prepared over the fire using the wheel, and continue to apply one after another until the bleeding stops.
Be careful not to burn a nerve, as this may cause further harm to the patient. Know that if an artery bleeds, it is not possible to cut it, especially if it is a large artery, unless one of four methods is used: either by burning as we have mentioned, or by amputation if it has not already severed. If the artery is amputated, both ends will contract and the blood will stop. It can be secured with tight stitches or treated with medications designed to halt the flow of blood, and then supported with strips of fabric.
Those who attempt to tie, tighten with cloth, or apply burning substances or similar methods will not succeed in cutting it off entirely, except rarely. If such a situation arises without the presence of a doctor or medicine, they should immediately place their index finger over the wound's opening as described.
He is gripped so tightly that the blood collects and flows from the wound, over the artery and onto the finger. It does not leave until it is exposed to extremely cold water, until the blood freezes, thickens and cuts off. During this time, he should examine what needs cauterization or treatment.
The Second Chapter
In incisions, punctures, lances and wounds, etc.

I will follow the same approach in this chapter to make it easier for students to achieve their goals.
Before I begin, you should know, O children, that this chapter contains even more risks than the first one on cauterization.
Therefore, a strong warning is necessary as the work in this chapter often involves excessive loss of blood, which supports life. This can occur when opening a vein, making an incision on a tumor, draining pus, treating a wound, extracting an arrow, or performing surgery on stones, among other things, all of which carry risks and fear, and often result in death.
And I advise you to avoid things that are doubtful, for in this profession various types of people will come to you with different ailments; some may be weary from their illness and indifferent to death.
Due to the intensity of their suffering, the duration of their illness, and the obvious signs of death from the disease, some will offer you their wealth in exchange for health, while others will despair and see their illness as a battle, so you must not seek help from those who have such characteristics.
Let your caution be greater than your desire and careful consideration, and do not proceed with any treatment without certain knowledge that confirms the favorable outcome, utilizing the advance of knowledge and warning about the results of safety in all the treatments for your patients.
For in this, you will find assistance in acquiring praise, glory, and gratitude; may God guide you, my sons, to wisdom and success, for that is in His hand, there is no god but Him.
I have organized this chapter into sections, as previously discussed in the chapter on cauterization from the tip to the root, so that it will be easy for you to find what you need from it, God willing.
Chapter One.
In the treatment of water accumulation in the heads of children.
This condition often afflicts children at birth when the midwife applies excessive force during delivery, and it may also arise from an underlying hidden illness that is unknown.
I have not encountered this condition in any other gender, and all those I have observed from whom it arises, meet a swift death; therefore, I have abandoned treatment for it, having seen a child whose head became filled with water, and the head increased in size each day. Even the child could not sit due to the enlargement of his head and the increasing moisture, until he perished.
This moisture either collects between the skin and bone or beneath the bone on the peritoneum; in that case, one should make a single incision across the middle of the head for a small tumor, about two fingers long, to drain the moisture, and this is an image of the scalpel.
If the moisture is excessive and the tumor greater, make them two intersecting incisions as per this image:
If the moisture is beneath the bone and its sign is that you see the head stitches open on all sides, and the water decreases when pressed with your hand, clearly visible to you; in this case, one should make three incisions across the middle of the head as per this image.
After the incision, all moisture is expelled, and then the wounds are bound with bandages and dressings. On the fifth day, the bindings are loosened and the wound is treated with twists and ointments, without over-tightening the head dressing. The patient should be given dry food with little moisture.
Until the member strengthens and heals, another description of the incision: observe where the bone of the tumor is visible and the accumulation of fluid, as it may be more in the back of the head or at the front, or on the right or left side.
You intend the incision where the tumor and water accumulation are visible; make it as best you can, taking care not to cut an artery, lest bleeding occur, leading to the patient's death from such loss of blood, along with vomiting up the moisture.
Chapter Two
Cutting the arteries behind the ears, known as the sensitve ones.
When a sudden attack affects the eyes or chest, and persists despite attempts to treat it with medications, the most effective remedy is to cut these two arteries.
If you wish to cut them, you should first shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a coarse cloth to expose the artery. Next, tighten the patient's neck with the help of a garment, and observe where the pulse is strongest; these are the two weakest spots behind the ears, visible in most people.
Then learn their positions with ink, and cut them with the scalpel transverse to the bone, making the incision as wide as the head of a finger, or you may introduce the scalpel from beneath the artery and cut it up towards the top. The length of the cut should be about two fingers' width when joined together.
If the sweat gland is cut off, the blood will flow pulsatingly, leaping forward and receding repeatedly; if the artery cannot be felt, measure from the ear with three fingers' length, then mark it with ink and make an incision to the bone.
The blood sends six pulses on average, or perhaps more or less, depending on the patient's strength and the fullness of their arteries. Then examine the wound; if any part of the peritoneum remains attached to the bone, cut it away.
To prevent a hot swelling, then tighten the wound with a thread of cotton cloth and treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter III
On the arteries in the temple.
If a person suffers from a chronic condition such as persistent inflammation or severe pain in the temple muscles, or chronic headaches, and has been treated with various medical remedies without success, we have found that cutting (or cauterizing) the arteries in the temples can be effective for these conditions.
The method of operation on the skull involves shaving the hair from the temples, targeting the visible artery in the temple. Through this, you will discern its pulse, and rarely does it remain hidden except from those with exceptional knowledge.
If, in the chill of cold, it remains hidden from you, let the patient tighten his neck with his garment, then you can rub the area with a cloth or apply hot water to make the artery clearly visible. Then take the scalpel, whose image this is. Then, using the scalpel, carefully peel away the skin until you reach the artery. Next, insert a hook and pull it upwards to extract it from beneath the skin, freeing it from all attachments on either side. If the artery is thin, twist it with the tip of the hook. Then, cut a portion from it, as much as the distance between its ends allows, without causing bleeding. If it is not amputated or severed, the blood will not flow at all. Then, drain the blood using six to three openings.
If the artery is large, it should be tied at two points with a strong, doubled thread. The thread can be either flax or string from a lute, to prevent putrefaction from reaching it before the wound heals, which could cause bleeding.
Then, cut the surplus between the two ligatures. You can do this at that moment or later, and if you wish to cauterize it with a hot iron in the form of a thin knife, it will sever its ends, thus serving the same purpose or even better as we have said.
If the patient suffers from fever or a disturbed mood, cauterization is beneficial in expelling humors, making it even more effective. After releasing the ligatures, the wound should be filled with old cotton and covered with tight bandages. Subsequently, it should be treated with dry medicinal herbs to promote tissue regeneration and twisting until healing occurs, God willing.
If, while working, bleeding occurs from an artery, immediately cut it either with a hot iron or by filling the wound with ash and pressing hard until the blood stops. If nothing is at hand, put your finger in the wound to prevent further bleeding until help arrives.
Apply ice-cold water to the wound until the pain subsides and then tightly bandage it as necessary. Removing an artery is easier and simpler than you might think; cauterize the sweat glands with this two-bladed hot iron after marking the positions with dye.
Apply a hot iron with great care until it reaches the bone, severing the sweat gland at two points to separate its ends. It will not heal with this cauterization. This is an illustration of the iron.
The blades of the scissors are sharp, resembling two small pads, but they are much less sharp than a knife. For if they are as sharp as a knife, the cold would come to them more quickly and the meat would not be cut smoothly. If there is any roughness in them, they will grip the hot flesh.
Cutting the meat quickly is the best and most effortless action, and it should be done with a gap between the scissors' blades as wide as a finger's thickness.

Chapter VI.
In the treatment of what falls into the ear.
All that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic stone or semi-stone such as iron and glass, or a plant seed like lentils and nuts, or something liquid such as water, vinegar, and the like.
And if it is an animal, when a stone falls into the ear, or of the type of stone, not too large to remain in the ear, and it is received by the ear towards the sun, then pour some violet or camphor oil into it, and try to remove it with head movements or sniffing with a blowpipe. And plug the nostrils when sneezing comes, after placing a bandage around the ear with cloth or wool and pulling the ear up towards the jaw. This treatment often removes the stone. If it does not come out, then try to remove it gently using an instrument of this type.

The incision should be in the shape of a crescent, reaching down to the stone. Then, using suitable instruments, remove the stone and quickly sew up the cut, treating it until it heals.

As we have mentioned, it is easy to remove them. For water that has entered the ear, the patient should first use a sniffing treatment with a sponge or something similar, while lying on the affected ear, using some old cotton wool if necessary, and then check if the water has come out.
Or, take many small, smooth stones and warm them slightly over fire. Then, the patient should insert one stone into the ear hole while leaning on one leg and striking with another stone, creating a rhythm on the stone inside the ear.
He should continue this process with each stone until all water is removed. It can also be done by using a reed or feather, placing one end inside the ear and igniting the other end to create steam, which will help expel the remaining water.
Do this several times until all water has exited or draw it out with a pipe, as mentioned regarding the stone. As for removing the animal that has entered it, if it is small-bodied like a tick and the like, treat it as mentioned in the classification.

Chapter IX.
In the treatment of cold affecting the eyelids.
Often, something resembling cold exhibits itself in the eyelids with great intensity and hardness.
And therefore it is called cold, being the accumulation of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids. The treatment involves examining it; if the cold is prominent on the surface of the eyelid and moves to all sides, then its case is simple, and you should make a vertical cut through it.
Then, peel it gently from all sides until you are free, then hang it on a hook and cut it. If it is not possible to cut it except after the eyelid has been completely cut, this will not harm the patient. If the incision is large, sew it together and treat it until it heals.
If it is small, there is no need to worry, as the ointment will heal and seal it. If the eyelid is inclined towards the inside surface, turn it upside down and hang the edge of the eyelid on a hook without the need for an incision, then cut it from all sides.
If the incision penetrates the eyelid, it will not harm the patient. Then, after cutting off the edge of the eyelid, wash the eye with salt water and treat the area to seal it until the patient heals.
Chapter X.
In the treatment of the ulcer that appears on the eyelid.
The ulcer (or "sharnak") is a fleshy growth that occurs on the upper layers of the eyelid, most commonly affecting children. It can weigh on their eyes and cause discharge at night, making it difficult for them to see in sunlight. Due to the rapid flow of tears towards them.
Therefore, they rest and sleep on their faces, with their eyelids wet under their eyebrows, inflated and the hernia visible. When you press the area with your fingers, you can feel the hernia between them. The proper procedure is for the patient to lie their head in your lap.
Then take a cotton cloth, prepare a thread from it, and make a circle with that thread around the hernia from all sides. Place it on top and press from all sides to gather the hernia in the center of the circle.
Then, using a scalpel, make a vertical cut through the center of this moisture, not larger than the cut made during phlebotomy. Deeply, you should cut through all the skin to reach the hernia.
In most cases, the hernia emerges from the cut as a white fat piece, which you can draw out with a cloth wrapped between your fingers, rotating your hand right and left until it is free. Be careful not to increase the cut, otherwise, the scalpel may slip and injure the eye.
If the hernia does not appear at first in the cut, you should gently increase the depth of the cut slightly until it emerges. Then, draw it out as we have said, and dip a cloth in vinegar and water, place it on the area, and secure it with bandages. Some people prefer to apply salt.
He places it within the wound to dissolve what remains of that moisture, then treats it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs at the site, he treats it with soothing remedies until it heals.

Chapter XII.
Raising the detached hair from the eye with a needle.
If it is a single hair or two, the work involves taking a fine needle and inserting a thin, smooth silk thread into it. Then gather the ends together and tie them with a very delicate knot. Let the length of the thread be about an inch.
Then, compose another thin thread in the bandage, shorter than the previous one, and tie its ends as desired. Then, have the patient lie down on your lap, close to the sun to clearly see the work, as it is a delicate task. Then, insert the needle with the bandage into the root of the excess hair.
Perform this operation on the eyelid with haste until you extract it above the natural hair, then pull the thread over the ligature and insert it into that ligature, whether one, two, or three hairs at most.
Then, pull with your hand until the hair comes out with the ligature in the eyelid with the natural hair; if you pull the ligature but the hair does not come out with it, pull the ligature down with the thread that you inserted into it until the ligature comes out of the upper hole.
Then, put the hair back in and pull both until it rises in the upper hole and you see it with your own eye. At that point, release the ligature and thread and remove them, then close the eye and leave it tight for two or three days until the hair takes its place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, either add to it a longer hair from the natural hair so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer after some time, then raise it.
Chapter XIII.
In the treatment of the gap (or separation) that occurs in the upper eyelid of the eye.
The condition to which this gap is exposed is called a palpebral gap, and this gap can either be natural or accidental. The accidental gap occurs due to the healing of a wound, a cut, a burn, or something similar.
In performing this operation, incise the scab, spread apart its edges, and insert a thread of linen between them, then bind it until healing occurs, without using drying or constricting agents for treatment.
If you do this, the wound will heal more severely than before, and instead of drying agents, use things that relax it, such as fennel seeds soaked in water with cumin and flaxseed, and a paste of diachylon mixed with fat, apply it to the thread and treat the wound with it.
The key to treating it is to strive by all means for the wound not to heal in the same shape as before, and this operation that we mentioned is only part of repairing the wound, not to restore the area to its original state.
Chapter XIV
In treating wounds on the lower eyelid.
The wound that occurs on the lower eyelid, which is truly called a "stra" (wound), is natural and occurs randomly, whether from a cut, tear, or burn, etc.
The procedure involves taking an needle with a doubled thread and sewing it into the flesh, passing it through from the left corner of the eye to the right, until the thread is in both corners of the flesh, then lifting the flesh above with the needle and cutting it with a broad scalpel.
If the shape of the eyelid returns to what is necessary, otherwise take a rod and place it on the site of the cut and turn the eyelid with it, making two cuts on the inner side of the eyelid, and the ends of the cuts will be from the angles of the cut that was made until they meet, forming an angle. Until they meet, it will form a shape similar to this: .
It is a Greek letter lambda, then remove that flesh as much as the sharp side of it is below the eyelid, and gather the scattered parts with two stitches, stitching it with woolen thread, and treat it with the relaxing drugs and twisting as we mentioned until it heals.
If the injury was due to a cut, stitching, or burning, then one should make a simple cut without affecting the hairs of the eyelids, as mentioned before, and separate the eyelids with twisting as we described.
In conclusion, when treating an injury to the eyelid, whether it is from above or below, one should perform the procedure according to what presents itself as the nature of the injury, as it can vary greatly in appearance; the surgeon must adapt their method as best they can.
Until the shape returns to its natural state or approximates it, and the same disease indicates what is suitable for it in terms of treatment and tools in most cases.
Chapter XV.
In the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may prevent many people from moving their eyelid freely, obstructing its natural functions, and therefore a thin rod should be inserted under the lid.
