Chapter XVI.
In the removal of the tendon and healing of the flesh of the regions.
The tendon is of two kinds: either nerve-bound, resembling a thin, firm membrane; or non-nerve-bound, resembling a hard, white substance that, when touched by metal or caught with a hook, readily breaks off.
And if a hook does not adhere to it, both types begin from the larger eye and gradually become more firmly attached until they cover the sight and prevent light and movement of the eye; in the process of cutting them, the patient should rest their head on your lap.
Then open the patient's eyes, raise the eyelid with your hand, grab the tendon with a slightly flexible hook, and pull it upwards. Then take a needle and insert a horse or cow hair and a strong thread into it, bend the tip of the needle slightly, and sew it in the middle of the tendon.
And pass the needle through, tying the tendon with the thread, pulling it upwards, and scraping the side of the tendon next to the pupil with the hair, as if spreading it out with the hair to its end. Then cut the rest at the base of the eye with a delicate scalpel or small scissors, leaving the natural flesh of the eye.
To prevent permanent tear-staining, the tendon may be pulled with a hook alone or with the thread as mentioned, then scraped with the smooth-ended scalpel, as illustrated below:
Be cautious that the scalpel does not touch the cornea, as it may cause a rupture leading to pupil dilation. After cutting, apply some powdered salt or zingar (a type of spice) to the eye and leave it until the next day, then treat it as necessary until it heals.
If the eyelid is non-nervous and neither an needle nor a hook can be inserted into it, this type of eyelid often heals on its own with medication, as described in the previous section. If you decide to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a delicate, smooth scalpel as shown in the image. Carefully remove the eyelid from above with a delicate scalpel. If you notice that it is loose, brittle, and separates easily, apply some zingar (a type of spice) or red dye or powdered salt to the eye and leave it for another day.
Then, continue the process until all of it is removed, unless a hot, swollen lump appears in the eye. In this case, leave it and treat the swelling until it heals, then resume the process of removing the eyelid.
If the tent (presumably a growth or mass) severely irritates the eye, attach it with a hook and cut off some of it, but do not excessive cutting to prevent excessive tearing. Then, apply red dye or zingar (a type of spice) to the eyes to heal.
Chapter XVII.
In the amputation of the legs and the removal of excessive flesh growing around the eye.
In some people, a red flesh may grow around the eye, overlapping or nearly covering the pupil, and sometimes overflowing onto the eyelids, turning them inside out and resembling the rose of Jinnar.
The procedure involves the patient lying on their back, and then opening their eyes while using suitable pincers to extract all the red flesh.
Either use a scalpel, as described for cutting the nail, or small scissors to remove it, ensuring not to harm the eye during the process. as the pincers used to capture the splinters, as illustrated in the following door (referring to an image or diagram).
After removing the excess flesh, fill the eye with ground salt or drop in some red dust (a drying agent) and apply a clean cotton ball soaked in egg white from outside to prevent excessive swelling.
If there remains something from the operation and blood overcomes you and you fear a hot tumor, then leave the eye and treat it with what soothes the tumor, then return to work on it until it heals, and do the same with any excess tissue that appears in the eye of this type.
However, you should avoid treating every weak eye through natural means or by performing any operation with iron, and do not put your hand into any of these treatments unless you see that the organ is suitable for such work.
Chapter XVIII
On the procedure for removing foreign bodies from the eye.
The vessels, red and woven across the eye, obstruct vision and weaken the eye over time. Therefore, you should first examine it. If the eye with the foreign body is strong and has no other illness besides the foreign body,
Then, the procedure (for removing the foreign body) is as follows: You should instruct the patient to place his head in your lap, then use a hook to attach these vessels, either one or two, depending on your skill. The hooks should be flexible and curved like this:
or use two hooks attached to a single body, curved like this:
Then, using a delicate scissors, gently snip the vessels and wipe away the blood from time to time until the eye perceives that the vessels have vanished and the blood has drained. Be careful with the eye, do not hurt it with the scissors' tips, and work during the middle of the day facing the sun.
And be diligent in your work so as not to cut any vessels other than the ones intended, and upon finishing, apply eye drops of green or red vitreous to consume the remaining vessels with its sharpness; if you cannot remove them all at that time, dress the eye with a remedy to soothe its pain.
Leave it for several days until the pain subsides and the swelling disappears, then resume work on it with the same care until it heals completely.
Leave it [with the patient's head resting on the student's lap, one drying the tear ducts and ensuring clear vision in the front and back of the eye, while you] focus on opening the eye with fixed or triangular keys held by a student.
Be precise, with the patient's head resting on the student's lap, one drying the tear ducts and ensuring clear vision in the front and back of the eye, while you have the option to begin snipping from either above or below the eye; ideally, dry two threads on the upper side, one at the end of the eyelid and the other at its beginning.
Grip it between the middle and index fingers, then attach pincers underneath. Hold it again between the ring and little fingers, and gently lift your hand to see if the tendons have risen. Cut the lateral tendon with scissors or a delicate scalpel.
The needle should be inserted through the tendon, and you should examine it until it rises on the flesh without cutting the tendons, as blood and serum may spill onto you.
Then, cut with scissors what exceeds the tendon both above and below, so that nothing remains attached except the cornea. If possible, lift it from the cornea using the needle, as this is best, and in some people, it may come off easily.
If you cut it, the wound will heal after several days, and the hot swelling will subside. Then, you can resume working on it.
Chapter XIX.
In the reattachment of the feather to the nose.
The surgeons call the feather a 'nostrum' (a term for a medical remedy). If it does not heal from burning or sharp, pungent medicine as described previously, there is no other recourse but to incise into the tumor when it matures and extract all the moisture or pus within.
Until the bone is exposed, if it reveals corruption or blackness upon exposure, then remove it with this instrument, which is shaped as follows:
The instrument called "al-khashna al-ras" is made of Indian iron, with a rounded head shaped like a seed or a knife edge, finely engraved like a scalpel or a file. Place it on the affected area of the bone and rotate it between your fingers while slightly squeezing your hand to ensure that the corruption has been removed.
Repeat this process several times, then treat the wound with drying and constricting medications. If the wound heals, new flesh grows, and no further issues arise for 40 days, it is considered successfully treated.
Know that it has healed, otherwise there is no other remedy but to return the nostril fistula to its original hole. To do this, expose the bone again either with iron or a sharp medicine, and if the bone becomes exposed, take a splint shaped as follows:
The end of the iron should be triangular, and its core should be a reversed cone, as you can see. Place it on the bone itself, close to the eye and slightly away from it, then rotate your hand with the splint until the bone penetrates and is felt by the patient.
By having the patient press their hand against their nose, if they feel air coming out, then treat the wound with constricting and drying medications as previously instructed. If new flesh grows and solidifies, the discharge that used to come out will return to the nose, which is lighter for the patient, God willing.
Chapter XX.
In the repair of the eye's net (or retina).
If the entire retina is detached and no harm or loss to vision occurs, one should ease the patient then fix it in a specific position, then place a plug behind the retina without a set rule, and suck gently.
Then place on the eye a poultice made of mulberry, chamomile, frankincense, and myrrh, then secure the eye with bandages using many strips, where each strip is folded in half four times to make many layers.
Then layer them as needed for the eye, securing each layer with a firm cloth, and continue to suck gently with the plug without a set rule. Remove the plug and leave the eye secured for one day and night.
If the bandage loosens during this time, it should be tightened, and then after one day and night, remove the bandage as mentioned. If you see that the eye has returned to its place, otherwise, repeat the bandaging, applying pressure, and using the plug until it returns.
Chapter XXI.
In amputation of the limb.
If a hernia appears in the fascial layer, then it becomes retracted and protrudes outside the skin like a grape, deforming the human figure, and you wish to amputate it, then proceed as I describe.
Insert a needle into the base of the hernia from below upwards, then insert another needle with a doubled thread from the opposite side and withdraw it, leaving the first needle in place.
Then, cut the point of curvature of the doubled thread and tie a portion of the hernia around the needle, tightening it well, then remove the needle and apply a wet plaster of egg white to the wound and leave it until the threads fall out with the hernia, then treat the wound to strengthen it.
Until the wound heals, the hernia may be tightened from all sides with a circular bandage to enhance the recovery of the eye, then cut open with a thin scalpel reaching the depths of the eye, allowing the egg white fluid to drain, and the eye will recede, droop, and eventually heal.
Chapter Twenty-Second.
On the Treatment of Fistulas.
This condition, known as the coma, is a period in which the eye accumulates fluid similar to descending water, but without it. The method of treatment involves having the patient sit on an upright chair and then holding their head with both hands from the sides.
And you should move it until you see the period flow downward with your eye, then become stationary and do not disappear, allowing light to pass and enabling the patient to see objects as they normally would. If the period does not flow downward, we know that it is not water; if it does not prepare for the descent as mentioned.
If this does not work, then place the patient between your hands and take a thin scalpel, and make a small cut above the cornea at the junction of the sclera and the cornea, allowing the material to exit.
If it exits, rinse the eye with hot water mixed with honey or water boiled with fennel and honey, then apply all other treatments until healing occurs.
Chapter 23.
As we have mentioned the types of water in the classification, and which one is suitable for the cup with an expanded explanation, take it from there for the health. At that time, you should have the patient sit between your hands, squatting, facing the light near the sun.
Secure and fix the healthy eye, then raise the eyelid of the affected eye with your left hand if it is the left eye with the water, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then place the tip of the instrument near the acropolis, at a right angle to the inner corner of the eye, and push the instrument strongly while rotating it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel the instrument reach something hollow.
It should go as deep as the distance from the cornea to the farthest point of darkness (the limbus), for you will see the copper in the same way by the viewer as the eye due to the clarity of the corneal membrane, then continue the instrument above to the position where the water is.
Then press it down repeatedly; if the water flows out, the patient will see what their vision allows from that moment on, and the instrument will be in their eye. Then pause for a little while. If the water rises again, lower it a second time without removing the instrument.
If it stabilizes and does not rise, carefully remove the instrument while twisting your hand slightly, then dissolve a little pure salt in water and wash the eye from inside out. Apply a damp compress or cloth over the entire eye externally.
Moisturize the eye with rose oil and white of eggs, and tie it with a healthy eye. As for us and our contemporaries, we place crushed cumin with the white of eggs on it. If the point (of light) does not enter the eye due to its hardness, as some people have eyes that are very hard. One should take the surgical knife, called the 'bried', whose image is shown here.
Drill through only the conjunctiva with this knife, without going deep. Lightly tap the point (of the knife) into position, then insert the point as we have described. Once your work is complete, prepare the patient to lie down comfortably on their back in a dark room.
Abstain from all movements, coughing, and make the patient's food soft to suit their condition. Do not move the patient's head to the right or left at all. Leave the bandage in place until the third day, then remove it in that dark room and test their sight.
Show him objects, then re-bandage until the seventh day. Do not do this during treatment or after probing with your instrument, but rather avoid it beforehand, as the water can quickly rise due to intense looking.
If a hot tumor appears, unbandage the eye before the seventh day and treat it with something to soothe the tumor until it subsides. Once it has subsided, release the patient's sight, place a veil over their face for several days in that dark room to help them train their eyes.
The treatment progresses gradually and addresses its causes. Know that in this art, the learner cannot do without observation; he must see it done several times before attempting the procedure. I have been told by some Iraqis that he described a special instrument from Iraq that sucks up water.
I have never seen anyone in our country make it, nor have I read about it in any of the early books, and this might be a recent development. Here is an illustration of various types of instruments to help one understand them.
They are made especially of copper, with an end that is equally delicate and shaped like a triangle with a sharp point.
Chapter XXIV
In the treatment of flesh that has grown in the nose.
It may grow flesh of various types in the nose, some resembling a mass of many-legged spiders, others a hard, tumor-like cancerous flesh, and some a soft, non-cancerous flesh.
If it is a soft flesh that is neither malignant nor cancerous, the patient should sit facing the sun with your hands on his face, opening his nostrils and inserting a hook into the flesh. Then pull it out gently and cut off what you have reached using a delicate, sharp scalpel from one side.
Until you know that all the flesh is gone, if anything remains, you will not be able to cut it off. Remove it with one of the delicate tools gently so that nothing remains, unless overcome by blood or a hot tumor.
If it faces a malignant tumor, be prompt to cut it off so that the blood stops and all flesh is removed. Then, after the cut, apply a vacuum or liquid to the nose, for if the nose opens and moisture passes through to the throat.
Know that he has healed, if moisture does not pass as it should, know that there is flesh grown inside the bones, detached, and the instrument did not reach and cut it. In this case, you should take a thread of linen slightly thick.
And tie many knots in it, placing each knot about the thickness of a finger or less. The patient then inserts one end of the thread into his nose using a rod or what he can make similar to a plug, and pulls on it until it reaches the nostrils.
It comes out in a loop, and often boys in school perform such actions. It is easy for whoever desires it. Then, you collect the ends of the thread: one end emerges from the mouth, and the other remains in the nose.
Then, you use the thread to cut through the meat with the knots. Do this until you know that the flesh is cut by the thread's knots. Then, remove the thread and, after wiping away the blood with a cotton ball soaked in Egyptian ointment, repeat this process for three days or more.
Until the ointment consumes all that remains of the flesh, and then it becomes a lead pipe in the nose for several days until it heals, if treatment is needed, drying should be used.
And this is an image of the nasal dropper through which oils and medicines are dripped into the nose:
A silver or copper object resembling a small jellyfish with an open container and a similar passage is placed there.
And if you prefer, the pipe can be made closed like a tube, and the container of the nasal dropper flat.
It has a handle at the end, as you can see, to hold it if you heat oil or any extract or flowing substance in it.
Chapter 25
On the warts that grow at the tip of the nose.
A wart often grows at the tip of the nose, increasing in size over time until it becomes unsightly.
Therefore, it should be cut off as soon as it appears and all of it removed. Then, either cauterization or a burning medicine that acts like cauterization should be applied to the site. If it cannot be cut off until it grows large, examine it; if it has become hardened and colorless with little sensation, then...
Do not approach it with iron, for it is a cancerous tumor, and I have often seen great harm result from cutting into such a tumor. Even if the tumor is soft to the touch and not discolored, I believe it can be cut entirely. So induce vomiting in the patient and cut it without caution or delay, and treat the wound with astringents to dry and contract it until healing occurs.
Chapter XXVI.
In sewing the nose, lip, and ear. If the connection breaks away from a wound or similar injury, know that once the attachment of any of these cartilages is severed, little benefit will be gained from treatment in most cases, except for certain individuals.
If any of this is encountered, one should examine if the wound is still fresh and filled with blood. Gather the edges of the wound together with stitches, then treat it until healing occurs. If the connection has completely separated, revealing two distinct parts, one should gently peel each separate edge, exposing as much of its skin as possible, until it bleeds.
Then gather the edges together with stitches, tighten them, and apply a mixture of frankincense and myrrh, powdered, to the wound, followed by a layer of sealant from an ointment made from palm oil or any other adhesive plaster. Leave it intact for two or three days before removing the stitches.
Change the dressing and leave it until the stitches dissolve on their own, then treat the wound with a salve until it heals. The method of stitching involves bringing together what is separated, either with needles as described in the treatment of abdominal wounds, or with thread as mentioned previously.
Chapter XXVII
In removing knots that appear on the lips.
Many people may present with small, hard tumors inside their lips, some resembling sebaceous cysts and others smaller or larger. You should turn the lip inside out, make a cut on each knot, and use a hook to remove them.
Cut them from all sides, then fill the cut with powdered alum to stop the bleeding, and afterward rinse with vinegar and salt. Treat the areas with astringents until the wounds heal.
Chapter XXVIII
On cutting excess flesh from the gums.
Often, excess flesh grows on the gums, which the ancients call 'abuls.' You should use a hook or a scalpel to grasp and cut it at its base, allowing the blood to flow, then apply powdered alum to the area.
If a corner of the flesh, after treatment, returns to its former state, cut it again and cauterize it, for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
In the inspection of teeth with iron.
It may happen that rough, unpleasant scales gather on the inner and outer surfaces of the teeth and between the gums, becoming blackened, yellowed, or green, leading to damage to the gums and ugliness to the teeth.
You should have the patient sit between your hands and head on your lap, and remove the tooth or the one where scales or something resembling sand is present, until nothing remains of it, as well as with blackness, greenness, yellowing, and so on, in order to purify them.
If what is in it disappears from the first inspection, otherwise repeat the inspection on the following days, second, third, and so on, until you reach your goal. Know that a tooth may require different types of instruments with various shapes according to what presents itself for your work.
For the instrument used to remove a tooth from the inside is different from that used to remove it from the outside, and also differs from that used between the teeth, each with its own shape. These are several instruments, all of which should be prepared for you.
Chapter Thirty
In the removal of teeth.
One should treat the toothache with all means possible and not hesitate to extract it, for it is a noble substance, even if there is no other choice but to remove it.
If the patient decides to extract it, it should be done carefully until the aching tooth is healed. Often, the patient, due to pain, mistakes a healthy tooth for the painful one and extracts it, but the pain does not disappear until the diseased tooth is removed, something we have witnessed repeatedly by chirurgians.
If the aching tooth is correctly identified, one should cut around it with a scalpel that has some force, in order to loosen the gum from all sides, then move it slightly with fingers or delicate tweezers until it becomes loose.
Then, firmly grip the tooth with both forceps, placing the patient's head between your knees for stability, and pull it straight without breaking it. If it does not come out, take one of the instruments and insert it under the tooth on each side, gently pushing and moving it as you initially did.
If a tooth is hollow or decayed, the hole should be filled with a piece of cloth and sealed tightly with a thin rod to prevent it from breaking off while you are pulling it with clamps. The gum around the tooth should be thoroughly examined from all sides.
Be careful not to break it, leaving some inside, which could cause greater harm to the patient than their initial pain. Do not do what the ignorant healers do, pulling teeth without using the methods we have described.
Often, they cause people great harm; the least of which is a broken tooth leaving its roots or some of them intact. Either it pulls out part of the jawbone, as we have seen many times, and then rinse it with a drink or vinegar and salt; if there is bleeding from the site, which often happens, then crush some glass and stuff it into the wound.
If the glass does not help, use a fine-toothed clamp, initially moving the tooth. It should be long with a short handle, thick so it doesn't bend when you grip the tooth. This is the image of the large clamps:
The handles should be thick, as you see, so that when you grip them, they do not give way or bend. They should be short in length, and made of strong iron or steel, with pointed ends that fit together tightly for a secure grip.
And the limbs may be made to resemble shears, being also strongly adjustable.

Chapter XXXII.
On the eruption of teeth into one another.
If teeth erupt in an abnormal position, it distorts the natural order, especially in women and slaves, and one should examine it; if a tooth has erupted behind another.
If it cannot be moved nor pulled back, and it is attached to another tooth, cut it with this instrument, whose shape is as follows:
It resembles a small beak, and it should be made of sharp, fine Indian steel. Use it for multiple cuts over several days to ensure the tooth is removed without affecting others. If it is firmly attached and easily removable, use a dental file from India, whose shape is as follows.
It should be entirely of Indian steel, with a very fine-toothed file similar to that used for making needles. File the tooth gently over several days, avoiding any sudden movements to prevent its displacement; then smooth it and remove it using pliers.
If a tooth has broken off and affects the tongue when speaking, it should also be filed gently until the roughness of the break disappears, ensuring it does not harm the tongue or disrupt speech.
Chapter XXXIII.
In the interweaving of mobile teeth with silver or golden threads.
If mobile teeth become shaky and move due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot chew anything edible without risking further loss of teeth, after trying and failing with constricting medications.
The trick is to tie a gold or silver thread, with gold being preferable as silver tarnishes and corrodes over time, while gold remains unchanged. The thread should be of medium softness and thickness to accommodate the space between the teeth.
And the method of interweaving is to take the thread and insert its bend between the healthy teeth, then weave both ends of the thread among the mobile teeth, one or more, until the weaving reaches the healthy tooth on the other side.
Then, weave it back to the starting point, gently and skillfully, ensuring the thread does not move, and tighten it at the bases of the teeth to prevent slippage. Cut off any excess thread with scissors, collect it, twist it tightly, and hide it between the healthy tooth and the mobile tooth.
In order that the tongue may not be wounded, it should then be left in this manner stretched (tension) as long as it lasts. If it slackens or breaks, tighten it with another thread so that it may enjoy this state throughout all time. This is a representation of the teeth and their interlocking condition in two healthy teeth and two mobile teeth, as you see:.
A tooth, or both teeth, may return to its original position after falling out and interlock as described, remaining in place. This is only possible with the skill of a skilled craftsman who carves bone from a cow's skull to create a tooth-like structure for placement in the missing tooth's spot. It is tightened as we have said, remaining in place and enjoying this state.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
In cutting the ligament exposed under the tongue, it prevents speech.
This ligament exposed under the tongue may either be a natural feature that humans are born with or it may result from an injury that has healed.
The procedure involves opening the patient's mouth and placing their head in your lap, then lifting their tongue and cutting the nerve ligament running across it horizontally until the tongue is freed from its attachment. If there is some firmness or complexity due to scar tissue from a previous injury, insert a hook and make a horizontal cut through it to sever the ligament and release the tension.
Beware that the incision does not penetrate too deeply into the flesh, for it may sever an artery and cause bleeding. After cutting, rinse with rosewater or a mixture of vinegar and cold water. Then place under the tongue a piece of cotton that the patient holds each night.
If the wound closes before healing, and bleeding occurs, apply powdered glass to the area. If blood persists, use a lens-shaped iron to extract it. Then treat it with all other remedies until it heals.
Chapter 35.
In the removal of the frog that forms under the tongue.
A tumor may form under the tongue, resembling a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural movement and possibly even blocking the mouth, filling it with swelling. The treatment involves opening the patient's mouth facing the sun and examining the tumor.
If you see it as a dark, black, solid mass that the patient cannot feel, do not interfere, for it is cancer. However, if it leans towards white and has moisture, insert a hook in it and cut it with a delicate scalpel from all sides to remove it.
If blood prevails while you are working, apply powdered glass until the blood stops, then return to your work to completely remove it, and afterward rinse with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with all appropriate remedies until it heals.
Chapter XXXVI.
In the treatment of tonsillar swelling.
And any growth in the throat, other than tumors, may present itself as glands inside the throat resembling those that appear outside, called tonsils. If you treat them with what has been mentioned in the previous sections and they do not heal.
Examine it: if the tumor is solid, of a dark color, and not very sensitive, do not treat it with iron. If it is red in color and its origin is thick, do not either, for fear of bleeding, but leave it until it ripens; either it will discharge on its own or burst from within.
If it is white in color, round, and its origin is thin, this is what should be cut off. The procedure involves looking at it before acting to see if its hot tumor has completely subsided or has decreased in severity.
When ready, seat the patient facing the sun with their head in your lap, open their mouth, and take a servant in both hands to press their tongue down using this instrument: [image or description follows]
The instrument is made of silver or copper, thin as a knife. When pressing it against the tongue to visualize the swelling, insert a hook into the tonsil and pull it outward, taking care not to tug on any adjacent tissues.
Then, cut it with an instrument resembling scissors, except both ends are bent towards each other and extremely sharp. It is crafted from Indian iron or Damascus steel: [image or description follows]
If you do not have this instrument, cut it with a scalpel: [image or description follows]
Sharp on one side and blunt on the other, after cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner. Then, rinse the patient's mouth with cold water or a mixture of vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, rinse with boiling water mixed with pomegranate peel or assafoetida or similar astringents until the bleeding stops. Then, treat as mentioned for tonsil infections. Similarly, any other growths in the throat, such as additional tonsil-like swellings, should be cut away.
A woman was treated for a tumor that had grown inside her throat, extending to the epigastrium, almost completely blocking it. The woman's breathing was restricted and she could not eat or drink.
And she was close to death if she had not been treated within a day or two, as the tumor had sent out two branches that emerged from her nostrils. So she acted quickly, inserted a hook into one of them and pulled, and a viable piece separated from it.
Then she cut it off at the point where it entered the nostril, and she did the same for the other nostril. She then opened her mouth, pressed her tongue down, inserted the hook into the tumor again, and cut off a piece, leaving only a small amount of blood.
The woman's throat opened, and she immediately began to drink water and eat food. For a long time, they continued to cut off pieces of the tumor, which grew back each time until it had affected both her and me.
I tried and burned the tumor inside the throat with a special tool, which stopped its growth. Then I left the region, not knowing what God had done to her afterward.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
In the excision of the tongue's tumor.
Which is called a grape if it descends to the tongue and swells, becoming white, rectangular, it is called a column; and if it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called a grape. If treatment with what we mentioned in the classification does not succeed.
And I saw that the hot tumor had subsided and it was thin, so it should be cut; and if it is rounded and compact, and has no length, and is purplish or of a black color or has no feeling, then cutting it should be avoided.
So there is a risk for the patient; therefore, when you see it with the mentioned qualities, such as its whiteness and length, you should have the patient sit facing the sun, press his tongue with the instrument described, then insert the hook into the grape.
And pull it down and cut it with one of the instruments mentioned in cutting the tonsils; however, you should only cut what exceeds the natural state without adding more, because if you cut too much, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then, after cutting, you use what we described in the procedure for cutting tonsils and treat it until healing occurs; if the patient refuses to have it cut, then you should use a trick to cauterize it without fear or caution, and the method of cauterization is with a sharp drug, by placing the patient's head in your lap.
Then press his tongue with the instrument mentioned, and take from the sharp water (mentioned) in the chapter on cauterization, and knead it with unquenched clay, making it neither too thick nor too thin, and fill the hollows of this instrument with it. And this is its form:
The end of the instrument, which holds the medication, has a hollow shape like a spoon, and you place the instrument with the medication on the tonsil itself, while the patient lies on their side, allowing saliva to flow from their mouth into their throat, preventing any foreign objects from entering and causing harm.
Then grasp the medication in your hand and squeeze it onto the tonsil for half an hour until it turns black and the sting of the medication subsides. If you wish, take a cotton ball, twist it around the instrument, soak it in the medication, and apply it to the tonsil.
Insert the instrument with the cotton ball into the tube from above, pressing it onto the tonsil. Repeat this process as needed until you achieve the desired cauterization. Then leave it; within three or four days, it will wither and fall off. If required, you can apply the medication again.
After cauterization, wipe around the tonsil with a cotton ball soaked in lard and dry the surrounding area. Then rinse with cold water and treat externally with compresses and internally with gargles until healing occurs. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and eliminates fear.
The pharyx may also be treated with a more delicate method than cauterization and incision, using fumes. The ingredients include fennel, thyme, sage, mugwort, chamomile, sweet marjoram, and similar herbs. They are mixed in a vessel, all together or some of them, and immersed in vinegar.
The mixture is boiled in a pot lined with wax, and in the lid of the pot there should be a hole through which the hollow instrument is placed, made of silver or brass.
The end of the instrument, containing the gum, is inserted into the patient's mouth, allowing the vapor to reach the pharyx through the tube until the pharyx is satisfied and relaxed. This process should be repeated several times until the swelling subsides, but avoid attempting this treatment at the onset of the tumor.
If the swelling increases, it is important to be patient and wait until the hot tumor subsides. If you do not have the hollow instrument, take a tube and attach a slice of egg shell to its end to avoid burning the patient's mouth.
Because the egg shell prevents the hot vapor from burning the mouth, this is a good treatment that ensures safety.
Chapter Thirty-Eight
In the removal of thorns and other objects lodged in the throat.
Often, a bone, fish hook, or other object will become lodged in the throat. One should remove whatever is visible after pressing the tongue with an instrument at the base of the tongue.
To determine what is in the throat, and if something is hidden and not visible, one should induce vomiting before the patient's food is digested in their stomach. Perhaps the lodged object will be expelled through vomiting or the patient might swallow a piece of lint or a small piece of fabric.
Or, they might swallow a dry cracker or take a piece of soft seaweed, tie it to a thread, and make the patient swallow it. When it reaches the location of the hook, quickly tug on the thread, doing this several times.
Often, the hook or bone becomes stuck and can be removed by these methods. If it does not come out as mentioned above, use an instrument made of lead in the shape depicted here.
It should be slightly thicker than a rod and have a curved end that the patient inserts into their throat gently while lifting their head, taking care not to touch their throat to avoid coughing. The doctor can also use their hand to insert it.
The insertion of the sick person is better for his knowledge of the location of the embedded object, and it drives it downwards or pulls his hand with the instrument upwards, all of this to the extent that it becomes possible until it comes out.

Chapter Forty.
On the discussion of the speech regarding the formation of tumors and their incision.
The types of tumors are numerous and varied, as mentioned one by one in this book. They differ in their shell and incision from two aspects: one is the type of tumor itself and what it contains of moistures.
The second type differs by the locations where they occur in the body, for a tumor occurring in the head is not the same as one occurring in the hip, and a tumor occurring in a hot spot is not the same as one occurring in a joint, each having its own rules regarding treatment.
Some tumors should not be incised until the pus within them has matured and reached its fullness, while others should be incised immediately, such as those close to joints that have not yet fully formed.
Because when a tumor occurs near a joint and its condition persists until it putrefies what is around it, it may damage the ligaments or nerve of that joint, causing paralysis of that member or when the tumor is close to a vital organ.
If you delay the incision until the pus matures, it will harm that vital member or, if located near the hip, it will cause a fistula when incised after maturation, leading to putrefaction and spreading inside the hip. Therefore, one must not delay incising a tumor that has not yet fully matured, even if located near the hip, for fear of it becoming a fistula.
In some cases, the condition may not heal completely, and one must know when the tumors have reached their full maturity, which occurs when the pain subsides, fever disappears, redness and pulsation diminish, the tumor's head becomes firm, and other signs disappear.
If possible, the ulcer should be placed at the lowest point of the tumor to facilitate the flow of discharge downward, or in the thinnest part of the tumor with the most distinct contour, and if the tumors are located near the hands and feet, the ulcer should extend along the length of the body.
The ulcer should also be placed in the positions of muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries, and generally in all flat areas with no bends. If the tumor is located in bony areas, it is better to leave the ulcer intact.
So that it may fully mature as we have mentioned, if you ulcerate it before, the discharge of pus will be prolonged and cause much harm and dirtiness, and sometimes the ulcer's edges may harden and its depths may become clogged. Some tumors may be ulcerated across the width of the body if necessary or according to what the organ requires.
For small tumors, a single ulcer should be used, while for large tumors, a wide ulcer or multiple cracks should be made according to the tumor's size. Some tumors may require the skin to be cut and peeled if they have become like a cloth.
And it became, in a manner, as if it were dead, as is often the case in many abscesses and pustules, some of which are incised with three angles, and others have a portion cut from them resembling the shape of a laurel leaf.
And some use a round incision, a crescent-shaped incision, and other cuts, and if the tumor has no head, like a smooth, flat mass, it should be incised simply, and if the tumor is large and has accumulated pus for a long time, one should not hurry to remove all the pus at once.
Instead, remove some of it and then compress the tumor until another day, then remove some more pus, doing this gradually until all of it is removed.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly person, for often the animal spirit dissolves with the discharge of pus, and the patient may die without you realizing it, so be extremely careful in this matter.
After incising these tumors, you should clean the wound and examine it. If the incision of the tumor is small or the cut simple, use old linen or cotton thread. If the tumor is large and the incisions numerous, ...
Then, you should insert a thread in each cut until they connect, and if the tumor has detached some skin or formed a cavity, it should be filled with old cotton or linen beard, without moisture, and secured until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar after soaking a cotton cloth in them and applying it to the bleeding area several times.
If the bleeding persists, you should employ the measures, treatments, and applications described in many places within this book and according to its divisions. If it is winter and the area of the tumor has many nerves, then soak the bandages in a hot drink and oil.
Place it on the area, and if it is summer and the conditions are hot, then soak the bandages in cold water and oil or a cold drink and oil. As mentioned earlier, you should leave it for three days and then remove the tumor and clean it.
Use what resembles it for treatment until it heals. This is all the knowledge needed to treat tumors in general, and as for the detailed method, each tumor has been described with a summary of its treatment.
Chapter Forty-One.
On the incision of tumors that occur on the scalp skin.
There are small tumors on the scalp skin, which belong to a category of growths enclosed in membranes and having specific conditions similar to a hen's egg. There are many types, some are fatty, and others contain moisture like mud.
Some (tissues) contain moisture similar to cotton or soup, and others are hardened and solid. All of these pose no danger in terms of incising and extracting them, unless a vessel (i.e., a blood vessel) is encountered during the incision.
The procedure for incising (tissue) involves first probing it with the instrument depicted below, which is called a 'meds', to determine what it contains. If it contains moisture, make a longitudinal incision along this line, a simple cut as shown.
Begin the incision from line ~B~ to line ~C~. If the moisture evacuates, peel and completely remove the sac that contained it, leaving no part of it untouched, for it often returns if any remains.
Then, soak a cotton ball in pure ointment if available, or in saline water if not, and fill the wound with it. Leave it until the next day, as it will consume what remains of the sac. Reapply the cotton ball with ointment to the wound a second and third time if necessary.
Until you are certain that nothing remains of the sac, continue treating the wound with ointments until it heals. If the tumor contains a fatty substance, incise it as shown.
Make a cross-cut and break the stitching across the wound edges, then peel it from all sides. Strive to remove the pericarp that contains it, for if a vessel blocks your way, proceed as described, and do the same with the tumor if it is hardened from the cut.
The treatment is the same as mentioned, and the cut on a hardened tumor is easier because it contains less blood and moisture, and some of these tumors in the head may have no moisture at all, as I once cut a tumor in...
On the head of an old woman, I found a tumor as hard as a solid stone, rough and white, which could not be broken even if someone threw it against something, and these tumors are different from the rest that occur on the head, like those that appear in children's heads.
At the base of the ears, make a simple cut through them all and turn their flaps downward to facilitate the flow of the contents downwards, then treat them as appropriate.
Chapter Forty-Two
In the cut on pigs, tumors often occur in the neck, under the arms, and in the groin, and they can appear anywhere on the body. Some may be single, while others are numerous and some grow from one another.

Chapter 34.
In the incision of the throat, revealing a tumor within the pharynx.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the throat, but I have not witnessed anyone perform it in our country, and this is their manner of speech. As for those with strangulation, they should avoid this throat incision, as it is useless.
In order that all the veins and lung may be healed, and for those with a hot tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if there is no illness in the trachea, then the incision of the throat must be used to avoid the damage that results from suffocation.
If we wish to do this, we should make an incision in the throat beneath three circles of the trachea or four small vertical incisions between two circles, as deep as the incision is in the peritoneum, not in the cartilage. This location corresponds to the proper place for the incision.
Since it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are distant, if the healer is timid, he should stretch the throat skin with a hook and then make an incision in the skin until it reaches the trachea, avoiding the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then, he should make an incision in the peritoneum that we described, and he can determine the trachea's incision by the mucus that comes out of it, along with the coughing and keeping the wound open for a period. Once the time when suffocation was feared passes, the wound edges are closed by pulling the skin together.
He should operate alone, removing the cartilage, and then use medications to promote tissue growth until healing occurs. The author of this book explains that these words refer to when they observe a patient with a blockage in their throat due to one of the tumors mentioned.
And when the patient nears death, they should open a slit in the throat to allow him to breathe on the wound, thus regaining his breath and escaping death. They ordered that the wound remain open until the illness passes, usually within three days or so.
They then commanded that the wound be stitched and treated until it heals. I have personally witnessed a servant taking a knife and cutting into her throat, severing part of her lung, and she was brought to me for treatment; I found her gasping for breath as if she were being slaughtered. I examined the wound.
I found that the blood flowing from the wound was thin, indicating that no major vessels or nerves had been cut; air could also escape from the wound. So I cleaned and treated it until it healed, with the servant experiencing only a slight change in voice.
After several days, she recovered fully, demonstrating that opening the throat does not pose any danger.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On the incision made into the tumor that appears in the throat from the outside and is known as the pharyngeal fistula.
This tumor, known as the pharyngeal fistula, is a large growth that resembles the body's own flesh and is common in women. It comes in two types.
It is either natural or accidental. Chapter Forty-Five.
On the types of incisions on various tissues.
The tissues have many types, and all of them have been mentioned in the classification. Here, it is necessary to explain the difference between tissue and abscess, as it is a complex matter. I say that an abscess presents with heat, fever, and severe pain until the inflammation of the pus subsides. The development of the mold completes the resolution of fever and its intensity, and with it, there is no heat, no fever, nor pain. It envelops a sac-like membrane that serves as its specific container, appearing in the color of the body. Its initial form resembles a chestnut, and it becomes like a bladder, larger or smaller.
These are of two types: either fatty or containing moisture. The colors of the moisture vary as mentioned in the classification. When treating this condition, it is necessary to examine and inspect it first with a tool called a probe, as shown in the image in the door following this one.
Assess the size of the tumor, then remove the probe and observe what emerges. If it is a flow of liquid moisture, regardless of color, make a simple cut as mentioned for other tumors. If no moisture emerges after probing, know that it is fatty.
Make a cross-shaped cut on it as shown, and suspend it with pincers. Gently peel the skin from all sides, and preserve the sac if you can extract it intact along with the tumor.
If the sac ruptures during the procedure and you are unable to extract it intact, be careful, as this often happens. Remove it piece by piece until none remains, for if any part persists, whether small or large, the condition may recur.
If you are overcome and a small amount remains, tear it with some sharp, edible particles and tighten the wound, then place something to soothe the hot swelling on top and treat it with all other remedies until it heals. If the wound is large, outline its edges and treat it with what seals.
If you encounter a pulsating or non-pulsating vein and blood loss occurs, immediately tear the area with powdered ash and leave it tightened for two or three days until the wound putrefies and the blood fever subsides, then return to cutting what remains of the vein.

Chapter Forty-Nine.
In the incision of the tumor that is exposed by an artery or vein, called aneurysm.
If an artery is wounded and the skin above it heals, such a condition often leads to a tumor, and similarly, a vein may also develop a puffy, swollen lump.
The signs by which it is known whether the tumor and swelling are from an artery or a vein: if the tumor is from an artery, it is usually rectangular and located deeply in the body; when you press the tumor with your finger, you can feel a crackling sensation.
The one that is from a vein will present as a round tumor on the surface of the body; the incision on these tumors is dangerous, especially those located in the armpit, groin, and neck, and in many places on the body, and they can be very large, so it is advisable to avoid treating them with iron.
And for those located in the limbs or head, it is advisable to avoid them; if it is a swelling of an artery, make an incision along its length in the skin, then open the wound with forceps, remove the artery, and free it from adhesions.
Until it is exposed, then insert an needle under it and push it through to the other side, tieing the artery with a doubled thread at two points, as I have previously explained regarding the arteries in the neck; then use a scalpel to punch the area between the bands until all the blood flows out.
The tumor resolves, after which the treatment that produces pus is employed until the bands (or ligaments) fall off, then it is treated with appropriate ointments until healing occurs.
If the tumor is in front of a vein, you should hold the tumor with your hand along with the skin, then insert an needle below the site where you are holding and pass it through while containing a doubled thread until you exit from the other side. Then, tie the tumor securely as mentioned to you, by tying both sides of the thread to the ends of the tumor, if you are afraid that the threads will slip, then insert another needle with a new thread under the entire tumor at the point where the first needle intersects.
Pull the threads in four places, then make an incision in the middle of the tumor until its contents are expelled, cut off any excess skin and leave what was attached to it. Then apply a bandage that has been soaked in a mixture of wine and oil. Then employ the treatment involving winding and ointments until healing occurs.
Chapter Fifty.
On the tumor that is exposed due to nerve twisting.
As the tumor appears in the artery and vein, so it appears in the nerve if it is struck or occurs due to excessive fatigue or similar, and most often in the wrists and ankles.
In every location where the joints move, there is a firm tumor that resembles the color of the rest of the body. Most often, it does not cause pain, and if pressed firmly, the patient feels a sensation similar to numbness. The tumor is not localized deep within the body.
Instead, it is located under the skin and moves in all directions, neither advancing nor retreating. As for what is found in the joints, it should not be treated with iron, as it may cause damage. And as for what is found in the head or forehead, it should not be treated in the same manner.
Cut the skin with a scalpel. If the tumor is small, use a pincers to remove it from the root; if large, hang it on a hook and peel it off, then remove it entirely and close the wound with stitches until it heals.
Chapter fifty.
On the excision of warts appearing on the abdomen.
They may appear frequently on some people's abdomens and other parts of their bodies, known as warts (fungi) due to their resemblance to mushrooms. Their root is thin, while their head is thick. Sometimes their edges turn inward, and they can vary in size, from very small to extremely large.
And I have seen a man with two polyps in his abdomen, similar to mushrooms, without any difference; their white, delicate bases had twisted and split apart, and moisture was always seeping from them. So I cut them off, and weighed one at about eighteen ounces and the other at approximately six ounces.
And the procedure for cutting them is to observe: if the patient is moist and the polyp is white, wet, and of delicate origin, then cut it with a wide scalpel. Be prepared with hot iron, as cutting them often causes a great deal of bleeding.
If you are overcome by blood, then cauterize it; if the patient is timid and afraid of having iron cut into them, take a tight leaden thread and bind it around the polyp, leaving it for two days, then increasing the tension on the lead daily until the thread is tightly bound.
As often as it affects the polyp until it detaches and falls out on its own, without any assistance; if the polyp is thick in origin, cutting it does carry some fear and risk, especially when it is inside the abdomen. However, it may be possible to cut off half or part of it.
Then cauterize it so that it does not return; then treat the wound until it heals. Be careful not to cut a polyp that is pale in color, lacking sensation, and unappealing in appearance, as it may be a cancerous tumor, which we will mention later.
Chapter LXXV.
In the treatment of navel hernia.
A navel hernia may result from various causes, either a tear in the peritoneum that allows the omentum or intestines to protrude, as is the case with other hernias, or from blood originating from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned, or from trapped gas.
If the hernia is caused by a tear in the peritoneum allowing the omentum to protrude, it will appear as a tumor-like mass similar in color to the body's flesh, soft to the touch, without pain, and easily movable. If the hernia is caused by the protrusion of the intestines, its appearance will be significantly different from what we described earlier. Pressing it with your fingers may cause it to disappear then reappear, and it may emit gurgling sounds. It often becomes more pronounced during bowel movements and intense exertion. If it is due to fluid accumulation, it will be soft.
It will not disappear when pressed with your hand, nor will its size increase or decrease. If caused by blood, the tumor will appear black, and if due to a growing tissue, it will be spongy, solid, and maintain a consistent size.
If the cause is gas, it will be soft to the touch. In this case, you should refrain from treating it, as there is risk and uncertainty, as explained in the chapter on tumors caused by arteries and veins.
If the navel hernia protrudes from the intestines or fat, you should order the patient to hold himself and stand upright, then trace around the navel with a pen. Then, you should order the patient to lie down on his back between your hands, and with a wide knife, cut around the navel at the marked point. Then, using a large forceps, pull the middle of the hernia upward, and tightly tie off the incision with a strong thread or silk thread.
The knot should be a simple loop. Then, open the hernia wall above the knot and insert your index finger. If you find intestines, relax the knot and push them back into the abdomen. If you find fat, use the forceps to stretch it.
Cut off any excess, and if you encounter an artery or vein, tightly tie it off. Return to your task, take two needles, insert them into the cut at right angles, and pull, tightening the hernia in four places around the needles.
