If the hernia affects all the vessels, one ovary should be removed along with the vessels, otherwise the egg will lack nourishment. By cutting the vessels, they wither and become useless.
Chapter LVI
In the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a tear in the peritoneum extending across the abdomen towards the hips, causing the intestine to protrude through this hernial opening into one of the ovaries.
This hernia can either be a tear or an extension of the peritoneum, and these types are caused by numerous factors such as a blow, a jump, a shout, or lifting a heavy object, etc. Its sign is that if it's due to an extension of the peritoneum, it occurs gradually over a long period of time.
It does not occur suddenly, and the swelling is level towards the depth as the peritoneum compresses the intestine. Its sign is that if it's due to a tear in the peritoneum, it causes severe sudden pain, and the swelling is visibly distinct beneath the skin near the site.
And thus, when the intestine protrudes and exits the peritoneum, it may also bring forth waste or thrubs, which are then called intestinal or thrub-like adhesions. It can also contain feces that become trapped there, leading to the patient's demise.
Because it causes intense pain and rumbling, especially if pressed, treating this condition with heat is dangerous and one should be cautious. The treatment involves instructing the patient to gently push the intestine back into its cavity if possible.
Then the patient lies on their back between your hands, with their legs raised. You extend the skin covering the testicles and make a long cut along the scrotum. You then insert hooks into the edges of the incision to facilitate its opening as needed.
The incision should be deep enough to allow the testicle to be extracted. Then, you remove the layers of tissue beneath the scrotal skin until the white, tough peritoneum is exposed on all sides. At this point, insert your index finger into the space behind the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum beneath the testicle and the second peritoneal layer, you loosen the adhesions behind the testicle. Then, using your right hand, you gently fold it back into the scrotal skin while, with your left hand, you lift the white peritoneum and testicle towards the incision.
And command the servant to extend the egg above, and you release the adhesion from behind with a complete release, and inspect with your finger to see if there is any part of the twisted intestine in the firm white peritoneum; if you find something, push it down into the abdomen.
Then take a needle with a thick thread twisted from ten threads, and insert it at the end of the peritoneum under the scrotal skin next to the incision, then cut the ends of the bent thread so that there are four threads. Then arrange them one on top of the other in a cross-like manner and tie the peritoneum, which is under the scrotal skin, with a strong ligature from both sides; then also twist the ends of the threads and tie them tightly so that nothing can reach the vessels they nourish. So that nothing of a hot tumor appears, and also make a second ligature outside the first ligature, away from it by less than two fingers; after these two ligatures, leave some peritoneum under the scrotal skin as thick as a finger.
So that nothing of a hot tumor appears, and also make a second ligature outside the first ligature, away from it by less than two fingers; after these two ligatures, leave some peritoneum under the scrotal skin as thick as a finger. Cut off the rest in a circular manner, and remove the egg with it; then make an incision below the scrotal skin from which blood flows, as we described earlier, then use wool soaked in oil and place it in the wound, and apply the ligature as described.
And if the white peritoneum, which we mentioned after cutting it with caution from bleeding, is often encountered, leave the bandage until it falls on its own. If its fall is slow, then pour hot water over it and carry what causes it to sweat until it falls.
Then treat the wound with all the treatments for wounds until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Six
In this spongy tissue, I have seen no one dare to treat it with iron. The ancients mentioned that one should proceed as we described for the tissue attached to a bone, by binding the vessels after carefully cutting them below.
Then make a cut in the middle and treat the cyst by opening it until the vessels fall, and treat the wound as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Six
In hernias that occur in the groin.
The hernia in the groin may expose itself as we mentioned, but nothing from the intestines is exposed to the female side; if it were to descend slightly, it would return on its own at all times.
But if time passes, the tear in the peritoneum increases until the intestines and testes descend into the scrotum, and this is due to the extension of the peritoneum that occurs in the groin as we have mentioned. This is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes and wrinkles the area, and its treatment involves cauterization as we have previously described.
It may also be treated with a iron on this manner: the patient lies on their back between your hands, then you make a vertical cut in the protruding hernia to a depth of three fingers, and then you separate the peritoneum layers beneath the skin.
Until the white peritoneum beneath it is exposed, you take a strip of material, place it over the area where the peritoneum has separated, and press it deep into the abdomen. Then, you sew the two edges together, forming a continuous seam to close the hernia.
Then, you take the edge of the material and do not cut the peritoneum or touch the testicle or any other structure, as we taught you for treating intestinal hernias. Then, treat it with surgical methods.
If the stitches tear, clean the wound and bandage it until it heals; then the peritoneum will contract and the hernia protrusion will not increase.
The best course of action in this matter is preferable because it leads to greater safety.
Chapter LXVI
In the relaxation of the scrotal skin.
The scrotal skin often relaxes in certain individuals, without the muscles within becoming slack, and this can make it unattractive. Therefore, for those who wish to treat this condition, they should put the patient to sleep on their back.
He should cut away all the relaxed skin from the thick layer beneath, then gather and close the edges with stitches, either three or four in total. The stitches should be secure, after which the excess relaxed skin is cut between the stitches.
Then treat it like any other wound, until it heals and the stitches fall out.
Chapter LXVII
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
Therefore, I should not mention it in this book, but I have only mentioned it for two reasons: one is so that the doctor, when asked about it, will know the treatment for whoever encounters it; and the other is that we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
As in the case of lambs, goats, cats, and similar animals, I say that castration is done in two ways: either by pressure or by incision and cutting. In the case of pressure, we make the animal sit in hot water until its female organs relax, soften, and hang down.
Then, you press it with your hand until it dissolves and is no longer noticeable upon touch. As for castration by incision and cutting, you should hold the animal and squeeze the scrotum with your left hand, then tie the testicles and make a single cut on each testicle.
Until the testicles are exposed, cut them after removing the skin, leaving only the thin membrane covering the vessels. This method of castration is better than the one involving pressure.
Because pressure might leave some of the female organs intact, making the animal desire copulation; then you treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy
In the treatment of hermaphroditism.
In men, hermaphroditism presents in two types: one where it appears below the scrotum or within the scrotal sac, taking the form of a vagina-like structure with hair, and urine may discharge from the part located within the scrotum.
In women, there is one type, where it appears above the vagina on the pubis, resembling a small penis emerging externally, and both resemble females; treatment for all three types.
For both types of men and the single type of woman, one should remove the excess flesh until its trace is eliminated, then treat it like any other surgical wound until healing occurs. However, for the second type of man from whom urine discharges from the part within the scrotum, there is no intervention, nor will it ever heal.
Chapter Seventy-One.
On the removal of the clitoris and flesh growing from the vagina in women.
The clitoris may grow beyond the normal size, becoming prominent and aesthetically displeasing, and in some women, it can enlarge to the extent that it resembles a penis, enabling vaginal intercourse.
It is necessary to grasp the clitoris with your hand or a forceps and cut it, but do not be excessive in cutting, especially at the base, lest there be bleeding.
Then treat it with surgical treatment until it heals. As for the flesh that grows, it is flesh that grows in the cervix until it fills it.
It may protrude outward, similar to a carbuncle. Therefore, some early scholars call it the "carbuncle disease," and you should cut it as you would cut the clitoris, treating it until it heals.
Chapter 72.
On the treatment of epityphic stone (urinary tract stones).
Epityphic refers to a condition where a woman's vagina is either unperforated or has a small opening, either naturally occurring since birth or developed later due to various ailments. The latter, acquired conditions, may be caused by excessive growth of flesh.
It can be a thin or thick membrane, located deep within the uterus, or along its sides, or at the top or bottom, preventing intercourse, conception, and childbirth. It may also cause amenorrhea (absence of menstruation). This condition is identified by skilled midwives through examination.
If the blockage is visible, examine it; if not, use your fingers or a speculum. If the blockage is made of thin perineum tissue and is near the labia, quickly make an incision by creating a fold in the labia and placing both thumbs.
For a woman lying on her back with legs apart, forcefully spread the labia to puncture the thin perineum tissue, and then take a piece of cloth, soak it in oil, apply it to the area, and have sexual intercourse with the woman daily to prevent the tissue from fusing again.
If the perineum tissue is thick and dense, use a wide scalpel similar to a sheet of paper to make an incision. If the blockage is made of grown flesh, hang it with pincers and cut it. Have on hand remedies to stop bleeding without stinging, such as alum, chalk, and myrrh mixed with white egg whites.
Then, use a wide lead pipe to prevent the wound from healing quickly. Hold it for several days and apply a dry cotton string as a dressing, treating it with all manner of scar treatments until it heals. Sometimes, a new flesh growth may occur in the womb, which should be cut off in the same way.
Unless it is a benign tumor, a cancerous growth in the womb should not be treated with iron under any circumstances.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
In the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red pustules that occur in the female genital area.
As for hemorrhoids, it is an inflammation of the veins' openings such that they spill out a significant and continuous amount of blood. If hemorrhoids are chronic, they transform into warts, and I have mentioned in the previous section their types, symptoms, and signs, which we will recall here.
As for what is curable and what is not, I say that hemorrhoids and warts, if they are deep within the uterus and do not manifest externally, then they are not to be treated with iron. Rather, those located at the uterine opening, which can be felt, are the ones that should be treated.
A woman should enter a cold house, then use a scalpel or coarse cloth to remove the warts and cut them back to their roots. Then, she should apply pressure over the bleeding area without causing further injury.
She should apply materials such as castor oil, rhubarb, and gum olibanum, then raise her legs against a wall for a while. After that, she should lower them into cold water. If the bleeding persists, she should sit in a bath of sumac, pomegranate seeds, and gallnut, and then mix flour from barley with honey and vinegar.
If the blood stops, apply a compress to the back. If not, fasten the splint firmly to the ribs and breasts without condition. Then take a woolen cloth, dip it in a mixture of traethith extract, camel's tongue extract, or olive branch extract with sumac drink, and secure it to the site.
If the tumor subsides, treat the area with ointment until it heals. As for the red pimple, it resembles the heads of hair, rough to the touch, so you should cut what is visible of it, as you would with warts, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Seven
In the case of an abscess in the womb.
There may be various types of tumors in the womb, such as cancer, sarcoma, ulcers, fistulas, hemorrhoids, leeches, and warts, among others. All these conditions and their symptoms have been detailed in the preceding sections.
In this article, we should also mention the hot tumor that appears in the womb if it is among the tumors with a prolonged course. You should examine it; if the pain of the tumor is acute at its onset, with palpitations, inflammation, and fever.
If the skin turns red due to sensation, do not hurry to dress it. Treat it with what aids maturation. Once these symptoms subside, the woman should sit on a chair with two seats and lean back.
She should gather her legs towards the lower abdomen, keeping her thighs apart, and place her arms under her knees with a bandage to secure them.
Therefore, the midwife should sit on the right side and use the instrument to open the cervix, whose image is depicted later. When using this instrument, one must measure the depth of the woman's uterus to ensure that what enters through the instrument does not exceed the depth of the uterus.
If the instrument causes harm due to its depth, one should place pads on the cervix's edge to prevent it from going too deep into the uterus. The thread running through the instrument should be from the upper side.
While you hold the instrument, the assistant turns the thread until the cervix opens. If the discharge appears and feels soft when touched with the hand, it should be incised at its base with a broad blade once the period is complete.
A soft thread soaked in rose fat or green oil, with some contraction, should be placed in the wound. The thread should extend from the incision in the cervix to outside, where it is wrapped around the uterus and groin area with clean wool soaked in boiled baker's yeast water.
Then, it is treated after two days with the ointment suitable for that purpose until it heals.
The uterus and wound may be washed by injecting water and honey, then water in which the root of fennel or long radish has boiled, and finally return to the ointment, if the abscess is hidden internally within the uterus.
One should refrain from treating it with iron, but rather treat it as we have mentioned in the classification.
Chapter LXXV.
In teaching midwives how to treat living fetuses that emerge outside the normal form.
The midwife should first know the signs of normal childbirth, which include when the woman feels a need to move lower and longs for fresh air, and when she finds relief from the pains she is experiencing.
And if the baby comes out quickly, know that this is a normal delivery, and the baby should come out headfirst, with the placenta either attached or hanging from the umbilical cord. If you observe these signs, you should squeeze the woman's abdomen to help the baby deliver more swiftly.
If the foetus descends with the placenta, it is a normal and desirable delivery. However, if the foetus emerges with its legs first, or its hands before its head and legs, it is an abnormal and undesirable birth.
If one hand or leg emerges first, or the head and hand, or if it curls up and possibly turns over on its back, these are undesirable positions. The midwife must be skilled and careful in handling such situations to avoid mistakes.
I will explain each of these positions and how to handle them so that you can recognize and manage them. If the foetus emerges with its head first in a normal position, but the woman feels intense pain and her strength weakens, seat her on a chair and have other women support her.
Moisturize the womb with cooked fennel and wet oils. Then, the midwife inserts a small scalpel or uses her nail to make a cut in the placenta, allowing the fluids to drain. Finally, she presses on the woman's abdomen to deliver the foetus.
If the foetus does not descend, the woman should be given a mixture of fennel saliva and alum (شيرج) oil. After this treatment, she should be instructed to sneeze and sniff with a cold compress while holding her mouth and nose closed. This will often prompt the foetus to come down immediately, and if one hand or leg emerges first, gently guide it back inside, little by little.
If the baby cannot be delivered, place the woman on a bed and raise her legs above, then shake the bed on the ground while she grips tightly to prevent falling. If the hands do not deliver the baby and it dies, cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
If delivery is difficult, tie the woman's hands with a cloth and gently pull them, and it will come out. If the baby is delivered on its feet, you should push them up and then gradually turn the baby until it is in a normal position. Then take one of its legs and slowly stretch it.
If both legs are delivered, order her to cough and blow her nose with a sponge, and it will come out. If it does not come out as described, gradually turn the baby until it is in its natural position; then it will come out smoothly. If all else fails, try again.
Take some of the saliva from a snake, fennel seed, and brahmi fat, mix them well in a mortar, then apply it to the woman's vagina and lower abdomen, and sit her in warm water until the contractions begin.
If you see that her contractions are weak, prepare a warm compress and apply it. Once she grasps the compress for an hour, sit her on a chair, then make her cough while covering her mouth and nose, and gently press her lower abdomen. The baby will come out at this point, coming out on its knees and hands.
If the patient is in this position, gradually insert your hands and then have the woman lie on her stomach with her legs hanging down over the side of the bed while she holds her hands. Then gently press down on her abdomen little by little. Then remove the pillows from under her legs so they are pointing downwards. If the baby does not come out, take both of her legs and move them vigorously. Then press down on the perineum little by little until the baby ascends, and the midwife can then insert a hand to help position the baby.
Order the woman to squat until the baby is born, with the baby's hand extended and reaching for one of her hands. Gradually withdraw your hand if she cannot grasp it. If she cannot stand, have her lie down on her side.
Have her lie on her side on the bed and shake her legs vigorously. Then use the birthing chair (latuq) as described, applying lubrication. If she experiences strong contractions, sit her up on a chair and assist in returning her hand to its natural position while positioning the baby correctly. Encourage her to squat until the baby is born.
When the baby is born upside down, stretching its hands with its face towards its mother's back, the midwife should take hold of the baby's hand and gently turn it, positioning it correctly. Once positioned, apply the lubricants as described previously.
Then make her move a little bit by bit. If she does, sit her on the chair and order her to lean to the right side, then instruct her to twist and sneeze, and the foetus will come out upright on its side.
If it descends into the womb in this manner with the placenta, cut the placenta with your fingernail until it is free from moisture. If it is attached to the face or neck of the foetus, cut it from the navel so that it does not suffocate and die. Then push the foetus back inside until it is in the correct position.
Instruct the woman to twist several times, and it will come out easily, the birth of twins or many fetuses. Know that twins often come into the world in threes or fours and survive, except rarely. Five is something beyond nature; they never survive.
They are surrounded by a single placenta, and they are separated by a membrane that connects them at the navel of each one. They can come out naturally and easily, or they can come out abnormally as we have described.
If some of them are difficult at birth and turn to the forms we have described, treat them as we have described. The midwife should be gentle and act with caution, taking time and finding a way around each shape using her skill for the safety of the baby.
Description of the fetuses envisioned in the womb that fall, it may be one or two or three or four or five or six or seven or more than ten, and it has been confirmed for me that a woman miscarried seven, and another had fifteen, all of them having envisioned those fetuses.
By the grace of the wise Creator, all this should be known by the midwife, for she too may one day face such a situation and respond with what is appropriate.
Chapter LXVII
In the removal of the dead fetus.
If, after applying the treatments mentioned in the previous sections, the dead fetus does not come out and you need to use your hand, you should examine her. If the woman is strong and free from any symptoms that could cause harm, she should lie on her back on a bed.
Her head should be inclined downward, her legs elevated, and she should be supported from both sides by an assistant or tied to the bed to prevent her body from shifting when extending the fetus.
Then, the cervix should be lubricated with moist oils, including salvia, fennel seed, and flaxseed meal. The midwife should then take her wet hand and insert it into the womb gently, seeking the place where the forceps can be placed to deliver the fetus.
If the fetus is in a position where it is about to descend by its head, then hooks should be inserted into its eyes, back, mouth, throat, under the mustache or jaw, or in the areas near the ribs or under the ribcage.
If, however, it descends by its legs, then in the groin and middle ribs. One should hold the hook with the right hand and make its fold between the left hand's fingers, and gently insert the hand and hook into some of these mentioned positions.
Until it reaches something hollow, then insert another hook in front of it, or a third if necessary, to ensure an equal pull and prevent leaning towards one side. Then extend it evenly, not just straight but moving to each side as well.
To facilitate its exit, as is done with a tooth when it is removed, and in between, the extension should be relaxed. If any part becomes stuck, the midwife should lubricate some fingers with grease and insert them to turn what is stuck. If some of the fetus emerges, then move the hooks to other positions.
It should be raised slightly so that the fetus comes out completely. If a hand comes out before others and cannot be pushed back due to compression, it should be wrapped with a cloth and then pulled until it fully emerges, after which it can be cut off.
Either at the wrist or the forearm, and so you should do for the other hand and feet, if the fetus' head is large and there is pressure during delivery, or if water has collected in its head, then you should insert a sharp knife between the fingers.
And cut through it with this instrument, which is called a forceps, that comes after the description of all other instruments in the following chapter.
Similarly, if the fetus' head is large, you should naturally cut through or reshape the skull with forceps as we mentioned, then extract the bones using pincers, and if the head emerges but becomes compressed at the collarbone, cut until the moisture in the chest drains.
If the chest then collapses, but it does not open and the fetus cannot be delivered, cut as best you can. If it is located below the abdomen or has a hernia, remove it so that the moisture drains from it.
If the fetus is in a position where its feet are facing down, it can be easily drawn and positioned towards the cervix. If there is compression in the abdomen or chest, use a cloth wrapped around your hand to grasp it, then cut through the abdomen or chest until the contents drain out.
If all other organs have been removed and the head is retracted but the cervix is open, insert the left hand and, if the cervix is dilated, grasp the head with your fingers and pull it towards the cervix using a hook or hooks inserted into the vagina.
If the cervix has adhered to a hot tumour, it should not be forcibly separated, but rather moist, fatty substances should be applied, and drugs used, and one should sit in water that relaxes and moistens.
If the foetus is on its side and can be straightened, the same methods mentioned for a live foetus should be used; if this is not possible, it should be cut and removed, ensuring no part of the placenta remains inside. Once the treatment is complete, the same remedies for hot tumours in the womb should be employed.
If bleeding occurs, one should immerse themselves in contracting water and use all other remedies; I have seen a woman who had become pregnant and the foetus died in her womb, then she became pregnant again with that same foetus.
Then the other foetus also died, and after a long time, a tumour appeared in her womb, swelling until it opened and discharged pus. I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, but the wound did not heal, so I applied some strong ointments that draw out the infection.
From the wound, a bone emerged, and then another, days later. I was amazed, as the abdomen is not usually a place for bones; I suspected that these were bones from the dead foetus, so I examined the wound and removed many bones from the head of the foetus.
I continued to follow this course until I removed many bones and the woman was in the best condition, having lived thus for a considerable time, during which a small amount of pus discharged from the wound. And here I present this rare case.
Because it contains knowledge and aid for the skilled physician in their handiwork of healing.
Chapter Seventy-Seven

And both spirals should be directed towards the finger, with a length of one and a half palm-spans, and in the middle of the handles, two guides made of the same wood are attached, each with a length of half a palm-span or slightly more, and a width of two fingers or slightly more. These guides are crucial.
These are the two instruments that enter the cervix to open it when you rotate the spirals. Another similar instrument, softer and lighter, is also depicted.
Surgeons make these from ebony or boxwood in the shape of hooks, each with two prongs as you can see, one shent-long and two fingers wide. To open the womb, use these.

Chapter Seventy-Eight
In removing the placenta.
If the placenta becomes stuck after childbirth, the patient should be instructed to grasp herself, then cough forcefully while you press on her mouth and nostrils with your hand. If it passes out this way, otherwise take a pair of tweezers.
Drill a hole in the cover and insert open herbs such as fenugreek, sage, mint, chamomile, parsley, plantain, and artichoke root; soak them in water and boil them. Then place a silver pipe over the hole in the cover, with the other end inserted into the cervix.
Hold it until the vapor reaches the bottom of the uterus, then sneeze as mentioned, for the placenta will come out swiftly. If it remains after this treatment and does not exit, then the midwife should dip her left hand in syringe oil or elephant's spittle.
Then, insert your hand in the vagina and feel for the placenta. If you find it, grasp it and slowly stretch it out to remove it. If it is attached deeply within the uterus, insert the hand as described until you locate the placenta, then gently pull it out with caution.
To prevent the uterus from tearing with strong pulling, it should be gently moved to the sides, right and left, then the amount of pulling increased, which will cause it to respond and release the adhesion. If the cervix is closed, the treatment has been described through sneezing, the use of a cup, and herbs.
If it does not come out with all the described methods, avoid using force on it during subsequent attempts. Instead, tie what has come out to the woman's thigh and apply a four-ingredient ointment, as it will soften it after a few days.
It will dissolve and come out, unless it becomes gangrenous, in which case a foul odor will spread to the stomach and head, causing harm to the patient. In such cases, appropriate smoke should be used.
Therefore, some of the pioneers tried smoke from herbs and dried fig, an image of the machine that evaporates a woman during menstruation, retention of the placenta, and similar conditions.
It is made of lead resembling a funnel, or it can be made of copper, where the thin end is placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire, with incense placed on the coals. It should be held until that incense is consumed and replaced repeatedly.
Chapter LXXIX
In the treatment of an unpierced hemorrhoid.
A midwife may encounter a case where many boys are born with unpierced hemorrhoids, blocked by a thin membrane. She should puncture this membrane with her finger, or if necessary, use a sharp scalpel, being careful not to touch the muscle.
Then, she should place a woollen pad soaked in wine and oil on it and treat the area with ointments until it heals. If there is a concern that it might become blocked again, she can insert a small lead pipe into the opening for several days, removing it when the child needs to defecate.
A blocked hemorrhoid may also be due to a clot or tumor. In such cases, the clot should be broken, and then treated as mentioned, using wine, oil, ointments, and possibly a lead pipe for temporary relief.
Chapter Eighty
In the treatment of hemorrhoids that occur in the lower region.
Hemorrhoids that occur in the lower region are a complication and thickening that takes place near the anus, either externally or within the surrounding space, and hemorrhoid may be one or more in number.
If this complication persists, it will open and release a white watery discharge or thin pus. Some hemorrhoids may extend to the anus and colon, while others that are not extending may be recognized by what is discharged with feces and flatulence during defecation.
Sometimes worms may emerge from them, and they can be hemorrhoids if in the surrounding space they extend to the bladder or the urethra, or to the joint of the thigh and the coccyx, and what is known as a hemorrhoid that extends to the anus but is not extending.
Insert your index finger into the anus and insert a thin needle made of copper or iron into the hemorrhoid if it is not curved. If it is curved, insert a thinner lead needle or a horsehair into it.
Until you feel the needle or hair in your finger, if you don't feel anything and nothing comes out of the opening in the form of feces, flatulence, or worms as we mentioned, then know that it is not extending, so act quickly to treat it.
If the fistula opens into the bladder or urethra, its indication is the passage of urine from it and the inability of the wound to heal with medication. But if it opens into the hip joint or the perineum, its sign is the arrival of the probe there.
If there is no curvature of the bone, pain in a specific direction, constant discharge of pus, and no treatment or ointment works, then these fistulas that open in this way will never heal.
Treating them is a futile effort for those who attempt it without knowledge, as for those that are not fistulas or are not chronic, healing is possible with iron, as I will describe and what I have experienced: place the patient on their back with their hands under their buttocks.
He should raise his legs and tilt his hips towards his stomach. Then, insert the copper or lead probe into the fistula if it has a curvature, until you feel where the probe ends. If the patient feels it towards the anus, you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel the probe has penetrated on its own, exposing itself without you feeling any membrane or flesh between your finger and it, then be certain that it is a fistula that cannot be cured as we said earlier.
They have said that it heals in some people rarely, and among the treatments expected to be beneficial is to apply a thin iron over the size of the fistula as mentioned before, and insert a protector inside the fistula until it reaches near the anus, then return it twice or three times.
Until you know that all of that excessive, accumulated flesh similar to a bird's feather tube has burned, then treat it with a wet twist in butter, until that burnt flesh comes out, and then treat it with sealing ointments; if it heals, then the treatment is complete.
If the probe does not penetrate to your finger in the anus and there is a thick barrier of flesh or membrane between them, and you see the fistula behind the skin surface, then make an incision at the beginning of the fistula. As you pass the probe through the fissure, following its end inside the fistula until you reach where the probe's tip stops, then remove it and discard it, then clean away that excessive flesh similar to a bird's feather tube, leaving nothing behind.
Then treat it with sealing ointments until it heals. If blood interferes with your cutting of that excess flesh, the best course of action is to cauterize it with fire or a sharp medicine, as burning it with fire combines two beneficial conditions: it burns away the excessive flesh.
Cutting the blood and drying the humors, then treating it with a wet twist in butter or powdered sulfur mixed with oil until the site pus, and those burnt meats come out in the pus, then treat the wound at that time with pastes that promote the growth of solid flesh.
These are the drugs that contract and dry; if they heal, they will not relapse. If the fistula has extended into the depths of the seat (anus) and is distant from the surface of the body, insert your finger into the seat and explore it.
If you feel the probe and between it and you is a membrane or flesh, and it is close to perforation, then the treatment is not but in the way of hope and desire; that is, you use one of three methods: either you cauterize it as we have said.
Or you open it until you reach its bottom to be able to insert the thread and treat from close by, but do not overextend the cut lest you sever the muscle surrounding the anus, causing the patient to pass stool involuntarily. Then treat it as we have mentioned; perhaps it will heal as we said.
Or you penetrate that membrane and perforate it after cutting it near the anus, as we said, either with a probe or any other sharp instrument; then clean all the hardened flesh in the cut as much as possible.
Then, the surgeon should remove the entire wound along with the fissure and the upper opening until it heals, leaving the fistula open inside the seat, which will be lighter for the patient, and the fistula may remain in this state.
If, when introducing the probe into the fistula, it is on one side of the seat towards the surface of the body with the skin and ring of the seat, then take a probe with a pointed end like a shoemaker's needle in this way:

If the fistula opening is not perforated, perforate it as best you can, but do not make it too deep to avoid damaging the muscle. Then, proceed with treatment as we mentioned until it heals.

Chapter LXXVIII.
In the treatment of hemorrhoids from which blood flows, cutting them, and healing fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: those inside the rectum, resembling red blowers or, like grapes, small and large, from which blood always flows.
They can be solitary or numerous, and when outside the rectum, they are usually less moist, releasing yellow water or a slight amount of blood, and they are of the body's natural color.
For treatment of internal hemorrhoids, order the patient to defecate and clean themselves until the anus opens, revealing the hemorrhoids, then quickly grasp them with tweezers or your fingernail and cut them at their base; if they do not stay in place due to moisture and relaxation, you can use a ligature.
Be careful to cauterize them with a coarse cloth or piece of bread soaked in vinegar, or pull them out with your fingers and cut them, then sprinkle some sharp fine particles (as described in the burning section) to serve as a substitute for burning, or use an instrument to remove them as mentioned previously in the burning chapter.
Then treat it with all other treatments until it heals. If the seat does not allow for expulsion, inject the patient with a solution that causes a slight burn to wash out what is in the seat and allow for quick expulsion when the patient moves. The treatment of warts that protrude from the seat is easy and simple.
If one prefers to remove it with their fingernail or hook it onto a fishing line and cut it off, they should then treat it as we have mentioned until it heals. If one dislikes cutting with iron, they should use drilling instead, taking a twisted thread and inserting it into a needle.
Then pull the wart upwards and insert the needle into its base on the other side, winding the thread around both sides of the needle and crossing them below, tightly pulling the wart, then tie the thread and remove the needle, doing this to all warts, leaving one unpierced.
To drain any excess blood, then place a cloth soaked in rose fat or pitch on the seat and leave it there, instructing the patient to remain still, until it falls off. Once the warts have fallen, treat them with ointments and all other remedies until they heal.
As for the treatment of cracks, it often arises from dry scales and a stagnant nature. If it persists and no remedy works, one should use a scalpel or their fingernail to remove the top layer of dried scales, allowing the moisture beneath to heal and the crack to close.
Then treat it until it heals as it should. If it does not heal, repeat the treatment with greater force than before, causing bleeding and flaking. Then treat it again, for it will cure.
Chapter LXXVIII
In the treatment of reverse nails, unreversed nails, warts, and dry moles.
A nail is a round knot that resembles a screw head, present throughout the body, especially on the soles of feet and toes, causing pain while walking.
One should make an incision around the nail, using a knife or hook to extract it from its root, then treat it as mentioned previously. As for warts, each one is a small, rough, rounded protuberance above the surface of the body.
It often appears on boys' hands. Its treatment involves extending the wart and cutting or drilling it with silk thread or a hair, until it falls off. You may also burn it or treat it with a sharp medicine as previously mentioned.
Warts, both reversed and non-reversed, can be removed with the instrument mentioned in the treatment of moles. As for moles, they are small, rough, thick growths on the body's surface, extending deeply and, if affected by cold, causing a sensation similar to that of a bee sting.
And it should be done all over the body, most often in the hands. Its treatment is to take a tube of goose feather or strong eagle feather and place it around the ant until the circle of the tube surrounds it from all sides, then rotate your hand with the tube to cut through what is around the ant.
It should go deep into the flesh, and the ant will be removed from its origin. If you wish, you can do this with a copper or iron tube in this manner:
The top of the tube should be rigid and slightly curved to facilitate its grip and rotation by the fingers, as previously described for cauterizing warts in the chapter on cauterization.

Chapter Eighty-Four
In the treatment of surgical wounds.
As I mentioned in the division regarding the treatment of burns, here I recall the treatment of surgical wounds both by manual methods and medicaments, so let me say that surgical procedures vary according to the nature of the wound and the location of the incision.
There are numerous things that can cause surgical wounds, such as a stone stab, sword slash, knife cut, spear thrust, or arrowhead wound, among many other potential causes.
As for surgical wounds based on their location on the body, they include injuries to the head, neck, chest, abdomen, or liver, among other vital organs.
In describing the treatment of certain wounds to serve as a model and rule for all other wounds, I begin with simple head injuries, especially since those that are complex will be mentioned in the first section of the third chapter of this book.
I say that if a simple wound occurs on the head, and it is not a fracture, but rather a stab from a stone or similar object, and only the skin is pierced, and the wound is large and I fear the development of a hot swelling for the patient, then promptly cut it with a knife at the right place. Do not delay this, and drain the blood from him to the extent of his strength. If there is no obstacle to cutting, especially if the blood that comes out of the wound is slight, let the incision be made on the opposite side of the wounded area.
Warn the patient against eating, drinking, and heavy foods. If there is an obstacle to cutting, let him reduce his food intake and avoid meat and drinks. Apply a cotton ball soaked in rose fat or, alternatively, with a drink that has a constricting effect, to the wound if a hot swelling occurs.
If you are confident that a hot swelling will not occur, apply pressure to the wound if it is still fresh with its blood and has not been changed by the dry air I will describe below. However, if it has been affected by the air, use some ointment on it.
Until the pus drains, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large, from a sword or similar, and its edges do not meet, sew it together as I have described in the section on stitching abdominal wounds.
If the skin reveals the bone, is hanging loose and can only be secured with a thin splint, cut it away, then treat the wound with remedies to harden the flesh in its place, and order the patient to eat foods that are firm, such as heads, shoulders, and similar cuts.
If there is decay in the skin, not adhering to the bone, cut off the entire decayed part and discard it. Then treat the wound. If there is a blood vessel or nerve still bleeding from these wounds, locate the vessel.
If it does not heal, amputate it with a scalpel and tie it off. If necessary, if what we mentioned does not help, cut away the affected part until the blood stops. For small, simple wounds, their treatment is easy and straightforward; all that is needed is to apply these remedies while the wound still has its blood.
Before it is affected by the air, the recipe for the poultice is to take a part of resin, two parts of camphor, and three parts of slaked lime or unslaked lime, crush them all, sift them, and fill the wound with this mixture, tightening it well until a strong adhesive is formed on top.
It forms a clot, then it is tightened from above with bandages and left without loosening until it does not swell, for the treatment will not leave it until healing occurs, and it may heal on its own with just lime if resin and pitch are not available.
This process can also be effective in many major surgeries if the technique is mastered, and the bandages are tight, especially when the wounds are still fresh, unless they have been affected by air, which may cause some changes, particularly during the summer season.
Placing these bandages alone is not sufficient; they should be accompanied by specific treatments such as applying warmed ointments or a porridge made from barley flour, mixed with water and honey, to promote the discharge of pus. Then, other treatments can be applied until healing occurs.
If a fracture occurs with the wound, especially if it is a simple break in a bone, it should be immobilized with splints (gajf), as mentioned in the treatment of skull fractures. If the wound is in the neck, there is no distinction in treatment from a head wound of similar severity.
If a nerve in the neck is cut, there is no remedy other than to avoid tightening it excessively with items like ropes or bands, as they can damage and spasm the nerve.
Never apply cold to a wound, for the nerve's essence is cold and its connection to the brain, the most noble of organs, requires that the treatments applied be gentle, such as نوره (a medicinal compound) washed with sweet water, repeated, moistened with oil or rose fat.
If you also wash it with sweet water and other mineral stones in the same manner, and moistened pastes, but if the wound is large, use stitching or bind it tightly with bandages, then treat it until it heals.
If a wound has a deep cavity or an accumulation of pus at the bottom, drain it into the lowest point; if a blood vessel is severed and its blood is flowing, amputate it or tie it off or plug it if necessary.
If the wound has cut some or all of the pharyngeal mucosa and the jugular veins are intact, sew together the edges of the skin over the pharynx, without touching the pharynx itself, then smooth it and restore it to its natural shape, and tightly tighten it, leaving it for several days.
Then treat it with pastes that have a constricting and drying effect until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, explore it; if there are fragments of bone, extract them entirely or as many as possible; if you cannot, leave it until the wound putrefies and heals, making it easier to remove.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle over it the powder we have described. If the wound becomes gangrenous and forms an ulcer, treat it as mentioned in its section, under chest and shoulder surgery.
If the wound is from a spear or knife and you see it has penetrated deeply, observe if air escapes from it. If the patient breathes and air comes out, know that it is a fatal wound. If there is no deep penetration and the wound is fresh with blood, do not immediately sprinkle powder on it and do not tighten it, lest the blood become trapped in its depths.
It then turns to the heart and kills the patient, but apply a soothing ointment and mix it with water twice a day. If you do not have an ointment, place a old cotton wick in the wound to absorb the discharge and let the patient sleep with the wound exposed.
To wash out any accumulation, and if the wound has healed after three days or more without spasms, palpitations, shortness of breath or other adverse symptoms, know that the wound is healing well and treat it with bandaging and other remedies until it is completely cured.
If it is not possible to see the base of the discharge and it continues constantly, know that it has become a chronic ulcer. Treat it as mentioned in its section, washing out the wound and applying bandages. If the wound is on the chest or back, treat it with stitching as previously described.
Depending on the size, if it is large or small, and if it has affected the bone and cut off fragments from it, examine the wound and immediately remove those fragments. If they are clean, leave them; if not, remove them.
If the wound becomes gangrenous, it will be easier to remove the fragments; as for other injuries occurring in other parts of the body, their treatment is similar to what we have mentioned.
Chapter LXXVIII
On abdominal wounds, the emergence of the intestines, and their stitching.
The abdominal wound may be large or small or medium-sized. A large one might expose the intestines or several inches of them, making their reinsertion and stitching more difficult.
And a small wound can also be difficult in a different way, requiring the intestines to be carefully and promptly returned to their place; if not, they may bloat and thicken, making reinsertion even more challenging. Thus, the medium-sized wound is preferable, as it does not present the same difficulties with returning the intestines as the larger ones do.
Know that the stitching of the abdomen has four aspects; two general stitches suitable for stitching the abdomen and other body wounds, and two specific stitches that are used exclusively for abdominal wounds.
And I will describe them to you one by one, explaining and clarifying: If the wound is small and something from the intestine protrudes and it is difficult to push it back, this is due to one of two reasons: either because the tear is small as we said, or because the intestine has been inflated by the cold air.
If this is the case, then you should warm it by immersing a sponge or cloth in lukewarm water alone, or water in which dried dates, saffron, and fenugreek have been cooked, until the intestine relaxes and the inflation disappears.
This can also be achieved with a drink that contains astringents, which is better than water alone in relaxing the inflation. If you observe the inflation has subsided, moisten the intestine with water in which fennel or aniseed have been cooked, as this will facilitate its entry.
If, after this treatment, it still cannot be pushed back, make a small tear in the opening with the instrument used for cutting away hernias, and this initial treatment is preferable if successful. However, one may only resort to making a cut after necessity arises and all other methods have failed.
One side of the instrument is curved and limited, while the other side is unrestricted. The thin end is not as sharp as the scalpel, but slightly blunted, and it resembles a small spear. If the wound widens and the intestines are exposed, it should be returned to its natural shape.
Its specific position is preferable if possible, but if the wound is wide and located in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on their back with their legs higher than their head. If it's in the upper abdomen, the patient should be positioned with their head and chest higher than the rest of their body.
Similarly, if the wound is on one side of the abdomen, always aim to position the surgical site higher than the other side. This should be applied in both major and medium surgeries.
In minor surgeries, position it as best you can. Then, have an assistant gently hold the wound open with both hands, revealing a portion at a time to the surgeon.
This is the general method of stitching: take a needle or several needles suitable for the size of the wound, leave a gap the thickness of a thumb on one side of the wound, and sew with a single needle without threading it with a suture through both edges of the skin. With the edges of the peritoneum beneath the skin, from within to penetrate them from that side, and having gathered the edges of the skin and the peritoneum, it becomes four openings, then tie with a doubled thread around the needle on both sides multiple times until the edges of the wound meet firmly.
Then, leave a space the thickness of a finger and insert another needle, then tie it with thread as you did with the first needle, continuing to do so with as many needles as needed until the entire wound is filled, and let the needles be placed between the thick and thin parts.
Because very thin needles quickly cut through flesh, and thick ones are difficult to insert into the skin, therefore they should be placed at a medium point between thin and thick, and the needle should not be inserted near the edge of the skin.
To prevent the flesh from tearing and the wound from opening before healing, and also to avoid excessive stitching that might hinder healing, then cut the ends of the needles to avoid harming the patient during sleep, and use pieces of cotton cloth as handles to secure the needle ends.
And leave it until you are sure that the wound has healed, and this type of stitching with needles is most successful in minor surgeries, as it may be sufficient to stitch them with one, two, or a few needles.
As for the technique of stitching the second layer of the general type, it is to gather all four margins - that is, both skin margins and both peritoneal margins - in one operation using a needle with a moderate amount of spun thread, neither too fine nor too thick. Then, after these four margins have been stitched together by the needle, the needle is returned from the direction it started.
The thread should be woven across the top of the wound, forming stitches similar to those used for sealing a bladder, which tightens the contents and leaves a space between each stitch, approximately the thickness of the small finger. This type of stitching is well-known to everyone.
By using this stitching, a surgeon treated a man who had been cut with a knife. The abdominal wound was over an inch in diameter, with more than two inches of the middle intestine protruding. The tear was located in the center of the abdomen.
After 24 hours, the intestine was returned to its original position within the wound. The wound healed in approximately 15 days, and he was treated until he fully recovered and lived for many years, engaging in all his usual activities. The doctors had judged that he would never recover.
It is surprising that I did not treat it with a salve, as I was in a place without any medicinal supplies. Instead, I applied old cotton wool to the wound twice daily and kept it clean with honey water until it healed.
And as for one of the two types of sewing, I mention it based on the words of Galen: to begin sewing with the skin and insert the needle from outside to inside, then, when the needle has penetrated through the skin and muscle in a straight line across the entire length of the abdomen.
I leave the edge of the peritoneum on the side where the needle was inserted, and I insert the needle into its other edge from inside to outside in the other layer of fascia; then, having passed through it, I insert it again into this same edge of the fascia from outside to inside.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side, and insert the needle into its other edge from inside to outside, then, as you pass it through, also pass it through the layer of fascia in his direction until it penetrates entirely.
Then, begin again from this same side, following the edge that is against the peritoneum on the other side, and bring the needle out through the skin next to it, then insert it again into that skin, and return it along the edge of the peritoneum on the other side, in accordance with this edge of fascia.
Bring the needle out through the skin on his side, and repeat this process several times until you have sewn the entire wound in a manner similar to how a furrier sews leather. The overall nature of this sewing is like stitching fur with leather.
By sewing the peritoneum once on one side with the edge of the skin and leaving it open on the other side until it is emptied, and as for the second type of stitching, which Galen also mentioned, this is his words verbatim.
And it is that you sew it as the healers do, by gathering each part to its natural counterpart, joining the edge of the peritoneum to the other and the edges of the drains to the other, all as I have described.
The needle should be pierced in the edge of the drain close to you from outside and pushed through alone into the inside, leaving the edges of the peritoneum intact. Then bring the needle back out from outside to inside through both edges of the peritoneum, and also bring it back.
Push the needle from inside to outside through the edge of the drain on the opposite side, and this type of stitching is better than the common, simple stitching that is mentioned, as Galen said verbatim. Some have even suggested that a stomach stitch can be done with just two needles. And this is done by threading one needle and beginning to insert the needle from your side, pushing it through to the other side, and then inserting the other needle from the opposite side, pulling it through according to the stitching of the ascites, just as with any other stitch.
Know that if the wound is in the middle of the belly, sewing it will be more difficult than other places on the belly. As for treating the wound, its treatment is like other wounds, for if you succeed in reaching the wound while it is fresh with its blood before the air changes it and return the intestines to their place and sew them up tightly, then apply hot iron needles.
If the wound has been changed by air, apply some ointments to it that you prepare, until it suppures and the stitches fall out, and the peritoneum and omentum heal, then treat it as you would any other wound until it heals.
If you do not have any remedies, apply cotton wool that has been steeped in pus and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it heals. In most cases, if the wounds are simple, you will not need further treatment. However, if you fear that the wound might affect vital organs and cause pain, be cautious.
You should dip soft lint in moderately warm oil or rose fat and apply it around the areas between the ribs and armpits. If there is any pain or foul odor from the intestines, this often indicates a problem, so inject a warm, black, constricting drink.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestines and it becomes an open wound inside the body, know that what was thick in the intestines heals more easily, while what was thin is harder to heal.
And as for the intestine, known as the colon, it does not heal from surgery at all, due to the abundance of its vessels, their greatness, and the delicacy of its substance, and its nearness to nerve nature. If, however, what emerged from the wound is still fresh, return it to its place, as with the intestine.
If it has passed some time and it has turned green or black, you should tighten it with a thread over the place that turned black to prevent bleeding, for in the wound there are vessels and arteries, then cut what is below that bond.
You should make both ends of the thread attached from below the surgery, extending it outside to facilitate its removal when the wound discharge and the wound suppuration, mentioning the wound that occurs in the intestine. If there is a tear in the intestine, small in size.
It may happen that in some people, as I saw a man who had been pierced in the abdomen with a spear, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, I delayed treating it, becoming a fistula from which feces and gas emerged, so I treated him as if I did not expect a cure. I continued to soothe it until it healed and the wound was closed. When I saw that the wound had healed, I feared for the patient, lest something bad happen to him in his body, but nothing bad happened to him from that, and he remained in the best condition. Correctly, he should eat, drink, have sexual relations, and take a bath while being served in his condition. Some experienced people have stated that when there is a small wound in the intestine, it should be sutured accordingly.
And it is to take the larger ants, open their mouths, and place them on both lips of the wound. If they grasp it and tighten their jaws, their head will be cut off, but their body remains stuck and does not come loose, then another ant is placed next to the first one.
And this should be done with several ants, in accordance with the size of the wound, then it should be covered and sutured. These ant heads remain stuck to the intestine until it heals completely, without causing any harm to the patient, and even thin thread can be used to suture the intestine.
Which is to take a thread from the adherent intestinal tissue, insert it into an needle, and when pulled, it will come out smoothly. Then, a fine cotton thread is tied at its end, and this thread is inserted back into the needle. And the thread of the intestine is used to suture it, then it is returned to its place in the body. This method of suturing with ants and intestines is done for the sake of greed and hope.
However, if the wound is large and wide, especially in one of the thin intestines, there is no remedy or cure at all.
Chapter LXXVIII.
In the treatment of the common cold and nasal polyps.
Know that every wound or lump, if it persists and becomes a chronic ulcer that does not heal and continuously produces pus, is generally called a fistula in any part of the body, and we call it a cold (common cold) and a nasal polyp.
It is, in truth, a complex, solid, white lump with no pain, having a hollow space like the feathers of a bird.
Some have therefore named it a feather, and it can sometimes be moist, continuously producing pus, and at times the moisture may cease. It can be abundant or scarce, thick or thin.
In the classification when discussing wounds, it has been stated that every ulcer that does not heal and does not allow tissue regeneration is due to one of nine reasons: either a lack of blood in the body, or its poor quality, or because there is hard flesh inside or on the edges that prevents healthy tissue from growing. Either because the ulcer is severe and filled with filth, or because it is itself putrid and the material it produces is of poor quality, or because the treatment is inappropriate, or due to a corruption in the town from a plague-like illness, or because of a special condition in the town.
Either because the ulcer is severe and filled with filth, or because it is itself putrid and the material it produces is of poor quality, or because the treatment is inappropriate, or due to a corruption in the town from a plague-like illness, or because of a special condition in the town. As demonstrated in the city of Saragossa, where the progression of diseases is slow and the healing of tumors is difficult, either due to the presence of one or several bones, I have explained all these causes, their symptoms, and treatments with drugs in the preceding section.
Here, I should introduce you to the treatment of fistulas and rhinitis that is done by manual manipulation. Know that fistulas can occur in all parts of the body; some terminate in large veins and arteries or in a nerve.
or to the peritoneum, or the colon, or the bladder, or a rib, or a vertebra of the spine, or a joint of a composite joint such as the hand or foot, and some terminate near a major organ, among other places.
As for these fistulas with such characteristics, they are among the most difficult diseases to cure and require a great deal of effort and knowledge. If there are any fistulas that have not reached the places mentioned, where treatment is hoped for, then there is still potential for healing.
And in order to understand this, take a probe of copper or iron, if the fistula runs straight, use it to examine it. If there is a curve in the fistula, use a thin lead probe, as lead softens its form.
When the intestine enters and turns towards a bend, if the fistula has multiple openings and you cannot locate them with a probe, inject one of its openings with moisture, as the moisture will flow towards the other openings and discharge.
Then, conduct a thorough examination to determine if there is any bone or nerve, or if the fistula's depth is far or near, or if it is a single fistula with multiple openings. Assess all this within your ability of inquiry and by observing the patient's discomfort when you touch the area.
And similarly, consider all the signs and causes of the tumor. If you have accurately determined these factors, then proceed with treatment confidently, observing: if it is...
If the fistula appears close to or in a healthy place away from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or any of the places mentioned earlier, cut the fistula as described above and remove the debris and decaying flesh.
And treat what grows in its opening, whether flesh or a small lump, until it heals. If the fistula is deep and straight, cut it deeply as much as possible and clean it of all decaying flesh.
Then use cotton swabs soaked in strong medications and insert them into the fistula's depth, which you have reached with the iron. Do this repeatedly until the sharp medication has consumed all the decay remaining at the fistula's bottom.
Then, force it with ointments that promote the growth of flesh until it heals. If it does not heal thereby, cut it as described previously, if the cause of the fistula is bone and this is confirmed by you, examine it and make an incision as I have told you, unless something prevents you from doing so.
If it is a sweat gland, nerve, or vital organ as we have mentioned, and the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackening, remove it until the corruption disappears, then treat it with what binds and heals it until it recovers, if it does not heal and pus continues as before.
Be aware that you may not have removed all of the corrupt matter, so expose it again and thoroughly investigate its removal and purification with all your effort, then treat it. If it heals, otherwise, know that this corruption is deep and far from the surface, so expose it again.
Thoroughly investigate its removal and purification with all your effort. If the corrupt bone is a small bone that can be pulled, use delicate claws suitable for this purpose.
If there are many bones, thoroughly investigate the removal of all of them without leaving anything behind. If something prevents you from doing so, such as a ligament, and cutting it poses no danger, then cut these ligaments and flesh, and save the bones.
If you are unable to extract the bones, then apply something to the wound that will preserve it and leave it for days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones rot. Keep the wound clean so that the gap you made during your work does not heal.
If you are concerned about this, apply a cotton ball soaked in powdered sulfur with oil or a cotton ball soaked in Egyptian paste or one of the green pastes; if these ligaments rot and the bones become soft, it will be easy to remove and pull them out.
If it is a single large bone, such as the shinbone or thighbone, and only its outer surface has deteriorated, carefully remove the decayed portion until the blackening and corruption disappear, then close the wound. However, if a significant part has deteriorated and the corruption has reached the interior of the bone, it must be split open.
Cut it all the way to where the decay ends, and then treat it until it heals. I will tell you about a case that occurred to a man in his leg to serve as an example and help you with your treatment; this man was young.
Around thirty years old, he experienced pain in his leg due to something moving inside the body, causing inflammation and a large swelling that was not due to anything external. The passage of time, combined with the mistakes of doctors, led to the opening of the abscess.
Many complications arose from his condition and he was mismanaged, leading to gangrene in the leg with multiple abscesses discharging pus and bodily fluids. It was treated by a group of physicians for approximately two years, but none of them were skilled in manual surgery.
I then examined his leg, where a great deal of pus was flowing from these abscesses, and the man's body had wasted away, his skin having turned yellow. I inserted a probe into one of the abscesses, which led to the bone, so I examined all of them.
I found that they opened into each other on all sides of the leg, so I incised one of the abscesses to expose some of the bone, which was rotten, having been eaten away and turned black and putrid, with a hole drilled through to the marrow, which I cleaned.
I extracted the rotten bone, and I believe that was all the decay in the bone, which I cut out and spread open. Then I treated the wound with healing medications to hold it together for a longer time, but it did not heal. So I opened it again to examine the bone once more.
I found that the decay was connected to the bone, so I spread it open again and exposed more of the rotten parts. I tried to force it together, but it did not heal or fuse. I then reopened it and continued to cut away at the bone, piece by piece, attempting to repair it, until I had removed approximately a palm's length of bone.
He removed the foreign body with his tongue, then bound it with drugs, and it healed quickly. This repetition was necessary in his treatment due to the patient's weakness, lack of resilience, and the risk of death, as he suffered from a weak consciousness due to excessive vomiting.
And as the flesh grew in the place of the bone, it became strong and its condition improved in his body, and his strength returned, and his condition changed for the better, without any further harm from walking, especially if a bone had fractured in that location.
It should be done in this manner, taking a bandage and tightening it around the newly formed bone, then commanding someone to stretch it upwards, creating another bandage made of thicker wool than the first one.
Then, bind it to the flesh beneath the bone, extending its tip to pull the flesh downward, while exposing the area you intend to cut, ensuring not to harm the flesh with the saw. Place a wooden block or board beneath the bone for a secure support.
Because if you do this, the saw will not hesitate to cut through the rotten bone. The cut should be slightly above the area of rot to prevent further damage to the bone's interior, ensuring that no hidden rot remains unseen and requiring another round of treatment.
If the bone is rotten and not separated, but connected in the middle or in some parts, then remove the flesh from all sides, and place a wooden splint from below, and spread it from the first side where the rot is.
After you have spread it from one side, spread it from the other side, a little away from the rot, as we mentioned. If the rot is in a joint, then cut off the rotten joint itself and spread the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the rot is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy but to remove it. If the rot is in the hand or foot phalanges, the treatment is very difficult, but you should peel off the rot as it appears to you and strip it and clean it in any way possible.
By what means can you make it straight when a vessel or nerve does not prevent you? Note that there are many saws and chisels to cut these bones, according to their position, placement, thickness, thinness, hardness, porosity, size, and sharpness. Therefore, you should prepare for each type of work a tool suitable for that work.
Know that the work itself may indicate the type of tool you need, if you have a long-standing experience and knowledge of these arts. For he who is skilled in this craft and has witnessed various forms of illness, will know what tools to use. One may deduce for themselves similar instruments for every disease, and I have illustrated several instruments at the end of this door.
