Chapter 78
In amputation procedures and the reduction of fractured bones.
The extremities may become gangrenous either from a cause external or internal. If this corruption is treated with drugs but does not improve and you see the corruption spreading throughout the member, nothing will stop it.
It is necessary to cut off the member as far as the corruption has reached, so that the patient may be saved from death or from a great calamity greater than the loss of the member. A sign that this condition has occurred is when the member turns black as if it had been burned by fire.
After the blackening, gangrene may set in and spread to other parts of the body, so hurry to cut it off. Similarly, if the cause of the corruption is the bite of certain creatures such as a sea scorpion, snake, or spider, etc.
If the corruption or bite is in the tip of a finger, cut off the finger and do not allow the corruption to spread to the hand. Similarly, if it has affected the hand, cut it at the wrist and do not allow the corruption to spread further until it reaches the forearm.
Similarly, if it affects the arm, cut off the arm at the elbow joint itself. If the corruption persists and you see it spreading towards the shoulder, do not cut off the shoulder, as this would lead to the patient's death. Instead, use other treatments within your ability.
Similarly, if the corruption affects a toe, cut off the toe at one of the joints. If it spreads to the sole of the foot, cut off the entire foot, but if it reaches the knee, cut off the leg at the knee joint.
If the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no remedy but to leave it and hand over the patient to death; the treatment for amputation or dismemberment is to tie a ligature below the desired point of amputation and another ligature above it.
The server ties one ligature below and another above, and you use a wide knife to remove the flesh between them, exposing it entirely. Then, it is either cut or divided, and cloth should be placed on all sides.
To avoid the saw affecting the correct area, causing additional pain and a hot swelling for the patient; if bleeding occurs during your work, quickly apply pressure to the site or place some sharp objects to stop the bleeding, then return to your treatment until you are finished.
Then, tie the injured member with a suitable ligature and treat it until it heals; I will inform you of an example that occurred to a man in his foot, this very condition that I describe, where a blackening accompanied by burning, similar to fire, occurred in his foot.
That corruption began in his finger, spreading to the entire foot; driven by the pain and burning he was experiencing, the man himself decided to amputate it at the joint, and it healed.
After a long time had passed, the same corruption appeared in his ring finger, so he sought my help. I tried to prevent it with the medications I applied to the hand after cleaning his body, but the corruption did not desist and spread to the other finger.
He then asked for his hand to be amputated, but I refused, pleading for mercy and fearing for his life upon cutting off his limb, as his strength relied on movement. When he despaired of my help, he returned to his hometown.
I heard that he had his hand amputated at the wrist, and it healed. I relate this story to serve as a help in cases of this disease and as a guide for treatment.
Chapter LXXVIII.
In the treatment of abscesses and how to inject them with medication.
If a tumor forms in any fleshy organ and it persists for a prolonged period, eventually rupturing or drying up and expelling all its contents, leaving the site empty as if it were a vessel.
The skin covering it is like a cloth, soft and not yet completely corrupted; it does not affect the bone, nerve, or ligament. Hence, it is aptly called an abscess, not a fistula, unless its duration extends to the point where corruption influences any of these members.
When this occurs, it is called a fistula or an ulcer, and its treatment is to cut off all that skin as it circles around, especially if it has become thin and papery, and you are certain that it does not adhere to the wound due to its decomposition.
If the skin adheres and it has not yet reached the point of decomposition, but there is still thickness of flesh, treat it with a suppuration, which means to observe the abscess. If it is large and the pus that extends from it has an unpleasant odour, then inject it with Egyptian medicine.
Which is a mixture of vinegar, oil, honey, and ginger in equal parts, cooked over fire until the medicine thickens like honey, then take as much as needed, dilute it with water and honey, and inject it into the abscess, seal its mouth, and leave the medicine for about two hours.
Then, remove it with a press, repeating this process for several days until the abscess is cleansed and the foul odour disappears. If this medicine is not available, you can use water mixed with ash from date palm or oak wood.
Then strain it and inject it until you are certain that the abscess has been cleansed. If there is no improvement with these medicines, then inject a mixture of water and honey with some powdered ginger.
Or inject honey and a mixture of drinks, for honey purifies and washes, while the drink adheres to the wound, especially if it contains residual contraction and drying. If you do this repeatedly and ensure that the wound's corruption has gone, then inject what promotes the growth of flesh.
For example, you might take a mixture of myrrh ointment with rose fat and a contracting drink, or inject it with other drugs and ointments whose properties we have established in the article on ointments; if the wound's opening is narrow, it may not accommodate the syringe.
If the opening is too narrow, expand it slightly with iron, or insert a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment or syrion ointment to widen it. If the opening is also wide, sew its edges together and leave enough space for the syringe to fit without excess.
Similarly, if the opening from which pus flows is elevated, make a cut in the lowest part to allow the pus to drain downward, as pus trapped within the wound's depths can prevent the growth of flesh.
If you cannot make a cut in the wound to allow pus to drain downward as desired, then use a device that facilitates the easy drainage of pus, ensuring nothing remains within the wound.
And as for the healing drugs that are applied to the wound from outside, and how they should be applied, it is to take one of the cohesive plasters suitable for what you want and extend it over a piece of linen cloth, so that the cloth covers the entire wound. Then, using scissors, make a hole in front of the wound, slightly wider than the opening of the wound, and apply soft plasters to another piece of linen cloth, according to the size of the hole, and place them on top, keeping them distant from the wound's opening to facilitate the discharge of pus. Remove the large cloth only after a while.
For several days, it is the plasters over the wound's opening that you remove at regular intervals to allow the pus to drain and to observe the discharge of purulent fluid, whether it is abundant or scarce, mature or immature.
Additionally, examine the site of the wound to see if the patient feels any pain, and if there is swelling or none. If you continue treatment in this manner and observe a small discharge from the wound's opening, the material should be examined for signs of healing.
Know that if the wound has adhered or nearly adhered, then dissolve it on the following day or the third day. When you clean the wound, replace the cloth with a new one and apply fresh plaster. If after some time a thin pus is discharged, do not despair; the wound will eventually heal.
Its healing is swift because some people's flesh heals slowly from their wounds, while others heal quickly. If after many days a non-matured pus drains out, know that the wound has not healed, for its closure was delayed.
If the condition persists, increase the use of dry drugs while maintaining a balanced moisture level, similar to the consistency of nashli (almond) paste if it is enriched with an additional portion of qulqatar (a traditional medicine). The most effective treatment is to use the nashli paste.
Apply rose oil, sprinkle it with a moderate amount of ancient drink (a traditional remedy), mix well and use it. If you don't have nashli (almond) paste, you can use honey if boiled until thick, or mix powdered myrrh, sapper (gargarism), and galangal, crush them all, and sprinkle the mixture over honey while heating it. Then, apply it to a cloth, secure it over the wound, or coat it with thickened honey, sprinkle drugs on top, cover it with a cloth, and secure it tightly.
In such cases, you can use long ziraun (a medicinal plant), the root of iris (susu'n al-asmanji), fennel flour, or hellebore, either alone or mixed, crush and sieve them, and sprinkle this mixture over the honey described earlier, then apply it. If the wound persists after this treatment, know that there is corruption within it.
And if it affects the bones or other hard nerves or ligaments, its treatment is similar to that of fistulas, as mentioned previously.
Chapter Eighty-Nine
In the treatment of corns, softened nails, amputation of a surplus toe, and incision of fused fingers.
A corn is a thick piece of flesh that grows under the nail of the thumb or any other finger, and it may also grow on other parts of the fingers. If left untreated for a long time, it becomes swollen and forms a hot lump.
It corrupts and softens until it consumes the nail root, and it may even destroy the entire nail. The corruption may reach the bone, emitting a foul odor, and the finger tip becomes wide and black, and if you treat it as mentioned in the previous sections without success.
You should cut off all the protruding parts of the nail with iron, and then cauterize the wound afterwards, for cauterization is very beneficial in this case. If the bone is intact and so is the nail,
And the corner of the nail, which buries itself into the flesh, should be treated with a thin rod placed under the corner to raise it and cut off the embedded flesh gently.
Apply burning, corroding drugs to the remaining flesh until it is all gone, then treat with ointments until it heals. If the corruption has affected the bone, it should be cut and removed; for it will never heal as long as any rotten bone remains.
If you see decay and corruption spreading in a finger, cut it off at one of the phalanges as mentioned previously, then treat it until it heals. If the nail is injured or crushed and causes severe pain, first drain the blood from the wound.
Then, using a sharp scalpel, make an oblique cut in the nail from top to bottom, taking care not to reach the flesh beneath. This will cause severe pain for the patient and may lead to excessive tissue growth at the site. Treat the area with something to alleviate the pain.
As for the excess finger that some people are born with, it may be entirely flesh or have bones in it, and sometimes there is a nail, and some of them grow from the base of a joint between fingers.
It may be that the growth originates in some of the phalanges and does not move, or it moves at the joint where it grows from a finger. If it is purely flesh, cut it off at its base with a wide scalpel.
And as for the growths that originate within the joint, their treatment is difficult, so one should avoid cutting them. As for those that grow on the finger at one of the phalanges, they should be cut first by removing their flesh in a round manner towards the bone, then the bone should be split using one of those saws suitable for this purpose.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the fusion that occurs between fingers, it often happens and is usually a condition present from birth, or it may occur due to wound healing or a burn or something similar, and in this case, one should break up that fusion.
Until the fingers return to their natural state, then place between them a thread or a small hole soaked in rose oil to prevent quick fusion and separation, or use a thin lead sheet between them until the wound heals as it should.
And likewise, if fusion occurs between some fingers and the palm, break up that fusion according to what is suitable and corrects the form of the member.
Chapter Ninety.
On the cutting of veins.
The veins are twisted, thick vessels filled with excess black blood and occur most frequently in the body's extremities, especially in the legs, particularly at the joints and where weight is borne. Therefore, one should first use vigorous friction to remove the black crusts from the skin.
Then, the surgeon should incise the abscess and, for its treatment with iron, there are two methods: one is to puncture and drain the black blood, and the other is to let the sweat flow out completely. The incision should be made in this manner so that the leg first absorbs the hot water nicely.
Until the thick, cloudy blood dissolves, then tighten the patient's leg from above the thigh to below the knee with a bandage, and puncture the sweat gland in one, two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then, with your hand, drain the black blood from below the leg upwards and from above downwards until you extract what you consider sufficient or as much as the patient's strength allows. Then bind it and instruct them to avoid foods that produce black bile, and repeat vomiting and fomentation.
When the veins are filled and this causes harm to the patient, then pouring sweat can be achieved by shaving the patient's leg if it has a lot of hair, then immersing it in hot water or subjecting it to intense physical exercise until it turns red and sweats profusely.
If a bath is not available to heat the member, then puncture the skin next to the sweat gland with a knife, either lengthwise at the knee or lower down at the ankle, and open the skin with pliers, peeling away the sweat gland from all sides until it can be felt.
When it appears, you will see it red and pulsating; if you separate it from the skin, it looks like a string. Then, insert a knife beneath it until it rises and detaches from the skin. Hang it on a smooth, blind hook, then make another cut nearby, approximately the length of three fingers.
Then, remove the skin from the sweat gland, exposing it. Lift it with a knife as before and hang it on another hook. Then make additional cuts or multiple cuts if needed.
Then, cut and remove it at the end of the cut near the heel. Pull and lift it until it comes out of the second cut, then pull it towards the cut above, doing this until you have pulled it from the third cut, which is the highest among all the cuts. Once it has been completely removed, cut it.
If it does not come out when pulled and lifted, insert a needle with a strong, doubled thread, tie it, and pull it. Then, place the knife beneath it and twist your hand to the sides until it comes out. Be careful not to let it break, as it will be difficult to remove if it does.
It is then inserted into the affected area, and if all of it is removed, apply a poultice dipped in a drink or oil and rose fat to the wounds until healing occurs. If the growth is twisted and has folds with bends in different directions.
And if it is not straight, as we have said, you should make incisions along each bend and in the places where it twists, then attach it with hooks until you have grasped all of it, and be careful when making incisions to avoid cutting the vein or injuring it, for it will be difficult to extract, so take care.

Chapter 91.
In the extraction of the superficial vein.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot countries such as Hijaz and Arab lands, as well as in arid, infertile regions with scarce rainfall, and occasionally in other parts of the body, not just the legs, due to putrefaction occurring beneath the skin.
As in living bodies, worms and insects may occur within the skin and flesh, it is necessary to probe and remove the worm generated between the skin and muscle. The onset of this condition is marked by a severe inflammation in the leg, followed by a discharge from the affected area, after which the sweating begins.
If a part of the root or stem of a plant or an animal appears, it should be wrapped in a small piece of lead weighing between one and two dirhams, tied tightly, and the lead left hanging from the leg.
Whenever anything protrudes from the wound, wrap it in lead and tie it tightly. If it takes a long time to heal, cut off a part of it and wrap the rest, but do not cut the entire wound from its root before it has completely healed; otherwise, it may contract and enter the flesh, causing a lump, putrefaction, and a bad ulcer.
For this reason, it should be managed and drawn out little by little until the last bit is expelled from the body, and in some people, this worm can be as long as five or ten hand spans.
I have heard that in one case, a worm measuring twenty hand spans emerged from a patient. If it breaks off during treatment, insert a rod into its hole and wrap it tightly with bandages for an extended period, allowing the body to expel all its contents; and treat the wound with medicines for several days.
Then he treats it with tumor therapy, and this sweat may have many branches, especially if it appears in the knee joint or in the leg itself, causing multiple openings, and each opening has a branch that should be treated as we mentioned in the classification and as previously stated.
Chapter Ninety-Two.
In the fissure, on the worm generated under the skin, called the cattle disease.
This disease is called in some of our countries the "Cattle Disease" because it often affects cattle, and it is a small worm that develops between the skin and flesh, moving up and down throughout the body.
It is perceived by touch when it moves from one organ to another until it breaches the skin at a certain point and exits, forming abscesses due to the putrefaction of certain humors, as occurs with worms, snakes, and swelling in the stomach.
And it is expected that its harm comes from when it moves in the body and rises to the head, reaching the eye, where it might open it up and exit, rendering the eye useless, and this occurs frequently. If one wishes to treat and remove it, it can only be done when it is moving and perceptible by touch.
It is necessary to tighten what is above and below it with a strong ligature, then make an incision on it and extract it. If it sinks into the flesh and you do not find it, carry out the cauterization of the place with fire until it burns, for the greatest danger of damaging the eye lies in this, as we have said.
If you see it has moved to the head near the eye, tighten under it on the forehead tightly then make an incision and extract it. The patient should also purify their body with drugs that facilitate the elimination of putrid humors and guard against foods that cause putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
On the incision for the condition known as 'naphur'.
The condition known as 'naufur' in our country is a pain that occurs in certain members and then transfers from one member to another, and I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was called to a sick woman in a certain desert, so I examined her arm and saw a slight swelling in the veins of the forearm. After an hour, I observed that swelling spreading along the arm towards her elbow with great speed, like mercury.
If the pain shifts from one location to another, it disappears from the first place and settles in the shoulder, after which I sat for an hour and it spread throughout the body until it reached the other arm. Then she told me that her entire body was rotating as I observed.
I was amazed at the speed with which it moved from one organ to another, and I had never before seen this disease in such a way or to such an extent, except that I had observed a group of people experiencing pain shifting from one part of the body to another, but not to the same degree as I witnessed in this woman.
I initially thought that this could only be due to the woman being from a desert region, with a dry body and visible veins, which allowed the sensation of the moving air and pain to manifest in such a way. By contrast, these symptoms would not appear in people living in comfort, with moist bodies and hidden veins.
If one wishes to treat this condition and the patient feels pain, one should immediately apply pressure above and below the affected area using a wheel, and slice into it gently until the trapped air is released. Then, dress the wound.
If you do not see the condition, treat it by purging the body and expelling winds with methods such as using aromatic herbs or similar medications.
Chapter 94.
In Removing Arrows
Arrows may vary according to their types and the positions they occupy in the body. As for their variation by type, there are large and small arrows, hollow and solid glass arrows, and those with three or four angles.
Some arrows have blades and others have shards. As for their variation according to the members they enter, they fall into two categories: either the members are vital, hollow organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidneys, intestines, bladder, etc., or they are non-vital members. If an arrow enters one of these members and the signs of death that I am describing appear, it is advisable to refrain from removing that arrow, as death usually follows in most cases. If the bad signs do not appear and the arrow does not become lodged in the organ, remove it and treat the wound. If the brain is struck by an arrow that pierces the bone and injures the peritoneum covering the brain, severe headaches, dizziness, and vertigo may occur.
If the bad signs do not appear and the arrow does not become lodged in the organ, remove it and treat the wound. If the brain is struck by an arrow that pierces the bone and injures the peritoneum covering the brain, severe headaches, dizziness, and vertigo may occur. Redness in the eyes, redness of the tongue, spasms, mental confusion, repeated vomiting, bleeding from the nose or ears, loss of speech, and discharge of white, mucous-like fluid from the wound, resembling porridge, are all signs to look out for.
If these signs appear, refrain from treating the patient and extracting the arrow if it has not been ejected. As for the sign of the arrow striking the heart, if it is close to the left breast and can be felt as if it were embedded in a solid object rather than in empty space.
If the arrow's movement resembles a pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, followed by numbness of the limbs and cold sweat, know that death is inevitable. If the arrow wounds the lung, frothy blood will exit from the wound.
If the vessels near the neck swell, and the patient's complexion changes, and they breathe heavily, seeking cold air, then the arrow has penetrated the pleura in the chest, close to the small ribs, causing severe pain and deep breathing.
The patient will sigh, and all the muscles around the ribcage will spasm. If the arrow strikes the liver, severe pain follows, and blood resembling the color of the liver exits from the wound. If the arrow hits the stomach, something undigested may exit from the wound.
The case is desperate. If the arrow strikes the abdomen, becomes lodged there, and exits through a wound or rupture in the intestine, no treatment can be expected, nor can the arrow be extracted. However, if the arrow pierces the bladder and allows urine to exit, there may be hope.
If a part protrudes from the body and the pain intensifies on the patient, know that he is doomed. As for the rest of the members, such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, back spine, scapula, thigh, leg, and similar members, they may recover in most cases.
When an arrow does not strike an artery or nerve and is not poisoned, and I tell you about some of what I have seen regarding these arrows so that you can draw conclusions for your treatment, for example, a man was hit by an arrow in the eye socket at the root of his nose.
He removed it from the other side, under the ear lobe, and he recovered without any harm to his eye. I also removed another arrow that had struck a Jew in the eyelid, below the lower lid, and the arrow had retreated, leaving only its small tip embedded in the wood.
It was a large arrow from a composite bow, made of smooth, iron-squared wood without ears, and the Jew recovered without any eye complications. I also removed another arrow from a Christian's throat; it was an Arab arrow.
It had two ears, so I cut it between the clavicles, as it had sunk into his throat. I carefully removed it, and the Christian recovered. I also extracted another arrow from a man's belly; we thought he would die from it.
When the arrow had remained embedded for about thirty days without any change in its condition, I incised around it and removed it, and he healed without any subsequent complications. I also observed a man with an arrow through his back, which had healed over without further issues.
After seven years, the arrow emerged from the hip joint. I also observed a woman with an arrow in her stomach; the wound healed over, and the arrow remained without causing any harm to her natural activities.
I also observed another man with an arrow in his face, which healed well and caused him little pain. There are many similar cases. I removed an arrow from a high-ranking official of the sultan, which had struck him in the middle of his nose, slightly deviating to the right.
The arrow had completely exited, and I was called to treat it three days after the injury. Upon examination, I found the wound around the arrow very narrow. Using a thin probe, I felt no presence of the arrow, but the patient experienced prickling and pain under his right ear where the wound was located.
I presumed that the prickling sensation was due to the arrow's tip, so I applied a compress with strong adhesive properties, hoping that the swelling would increase at the site and reveal the arrow's position. However, no incident occurred, and there were no signs that the arrow had reached the site.
I continued to apply the bandage for many days without any complications, and during this time, the wound healed, leaving the patient desperate to have it removed for a prolonged period. Until one day, he felt an arrow inside his nose, so I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to the wound for several days.
Until it opened and I could see inside, feeling the fine tip of the arrow attached to the wood. Then, I continued to open the wound with that sharp medicine until the arrow's end became visible, and it took me several months, around four, to remove it.
Then, when the wound widened and I was able to insert hooks into it, I pulled and manipulated them, but they wouldn't come out. So, I continued to manipulate and use various tools until one day, I managed to grab them with firm hooks, as shown in the final section of this book.
And the doctors used to judge that his nose cartilage would not heal, but I managed to mend it, and the wound healed completely, leaving no further issues. And I will tell you how to remove some arrows, which can serve as a reference for what I have not mentioned.
For the parts of this craft and its details cannot be described in words nor fully understood by any book. Instead, the skilled craftsman measures little by much, relates what is present to what is absent, and innovates new work and tools during unforeseen circumstances.
If one departs from this art, I say that arrows leave the body from the members in which they have become lodged in two ways: either by being drawn away from the place where they entered, or from the opposite side. They exit as they entered.
If the arrow is protruding in a inflamed area, it should be drawn out; if it does not come out easily, one should leave it for several days until the flesh around it rots, making it easier to draw and remove, especially if it has become lodged in a bone.
If it does not come out after a few days, one should also leave it for some time and then try to draw and move it daily; it will eventually come out. If it still does not leave after several days, one should drill around the arrow in the same bone with a delicate awl on all sides to expand the space for the arrow, and then draw and remove it.
If the arrow that is lodged in the skull has penetrated deeply into one of the brain's cavities and symptoms from the condition previously mentioned appear, do not try to draw out the arrow; instead, leave it until later, allowing the patient time to recover.

If, however, the arrow has withdrawn into a part of the body. If sensation is absent, examine it with a probe. If you feel it, draw it out with suitable instruments for drawing it. If you cannot do this because the wound is narrow and the arrow is deeply embedded, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way, consider other methods.
Make a cut until the wound widens and you can grasp the arrow, then extract it. If it has ears (i.e., is embedded in tissue), free the flesh adhering to it from all sides using any means possible. If you cannot do this without breaking the ears, break them and remove the arrow.
If you attempt to extract the arrow from any part, use your hand with pliers in all directions until you free it. Handle it gently to avoid breaking the arrow, as this would make extraction more difficult. If you cannot do this within your time frame.
Leave it for several days until the flesh around it decays, then treat it again. If bleeding occurs, use the treatments mentioned in its section. Be careful not to cut any veins, nerves, or tendons, and use whatever means necessary.
By any means, you can extract the arrow, doing so gently and with caution, as described to you, and when pulling out the arrow, you should put the patient in a position similar to that when the arrow entered, for this is more beneficial.

Make a cut on it, as much as the claws will allow, then pull it out, for it will be easier to leave. If it sticks to a bone, twist your hand in a circle until the arrow affects the bone and makes space for itself, then pull it out.

If the arrow is hollow, push it with a tool that fits inside it; this will make the arrow come out more easily. If the arrow is poisoned, you should cut away the flesh affected by the poison if possible, then treat the wound accordingly.
If the arrow has penetrated the chest, or abdomen, or bladder, or ribcage and is close to what can be felt with a probe, then make an incision and be careful not to cut any blood vessels or nerves as you remove it.

Chapter Sixty-Nine
On Cupping and Its Application.
The cups can be made of various materials: horn, wood, copper, or glass. There are two types of cupping: one for drawing blood (with a suction effect) and the other for simply applying the cup without drawing blood.
And this cupping which is without condition takes place on two faces.
And the cupping of the jawbones, both sides of the neck, the cupping of the chin below the lower jaw from the mouth, the cupping of the shoulders, the cupping of the coccyx on the back side of the pelvis, the cupping of the arms in the middle, the cupping of the legs, and the cupping of the ankle bones.
Cupping draws blood from the delicate veins spread throughout the flesh, for this reason, the force should not be as strong as lancing, and cupping should not be used, whether with or without fire, in cases of swelling, so that the whole body is emptied.
If cupping is required due to illness or habit, it should be used at any time of the month, first, last, or in the middle, and at any time of day.
For some people, when blood accumulates to the point where it needs to be drawn out by cupping, they experience a sensation of heaviness and headaches. Others may notice swelling, redness on their face, head, and neck, or itching on their face, sides, and dark circles under their eyes. Some even experience pain around the area of cupping.
And among them, some laugh a lot, and some find the taste of blood in their mouths, their gums swell, and they spit out blood; some sleep much, and others see blood, redness, slain bodies, and wounds in their dreams, and suchlike. But when did we ever see any of these things?
And particularly if it is in the middle third of the month, we command cupping after two or three hours have passed in the day; and the benefit of cupping a small wound is that it helps with the heaviness in the head and the flow of fluid to the eyes.
But it should be after emptying the body entirely; this cupping can replace the phlebotomy. It is dangerous for those with a cold-natured constitution or those suffering from a fever, as it can cause great harm to them.
Therefore, the elderly and those with cold-related illnesses should avoid it, as long-term use can lead to memory loss.
Therefore, the cupper should lower their hand with the cup slightly to prevent memory loss; and the cupping on the back replaces phlebotomy on the eye socket and the basilic vein.
Therefore, it is beneficial for asthma, shortness of breath, headaches, coughing, and congestion; cupping on the back should be slightly raised because if it is done too low, it can cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
And as for the cupping of the temples, it is beneficial for pains occurring in the head, eye strain, migraines, throat pain, and aching at the roots of the teeth, serving as a substitute for phlebotomy. One should instruct the cupper not to insert his hand too deeply, lest he cut an artery and cause bleeding.
And as for cupping under the chin, it is beneficial for ulcers in the mouth, gum decay, and similar diseases of the mouth, serving as a substitute for phlebotomy in the gums and lips. Cupping on the shoulders is effective against palpitations caused by fullness and heat.
And as for cupping on the inner thigh, it is beneficial for what phlebotomy does to the three veins: the basilic, lateral, and temporal, as it draws blood from all those delicate veins in the flesh, attracting them from other, thinner veins into thicker ones.
One should instruct the cupper not to insert too deeply, as the area is devoid of flesh and contains nerves and arteries beneath. A single cupping on the coccyx is beneficial for hemorrhoids and lower abdominal ulcers.
One should instruct the cupper for the cups to be large and made of copper, as the area requires strong suction, and a glass cup may break easily and cause excessive wounding. The cups for the legs are insufficient in drawing blood effectively.
Because it draws blood from all parts of the body and is beneficial for chronic pain in the kidneys, wombs, and bladders, it induces menstruation and is effective against boils and abscesses, serving as a substitute for phlego- and lymphatic drainage, except that it exhausts the body greatly and causes fainting in many people. The lymph nodes of the legs derive similar benefits to those of the leg venae.
The technique of applying cupping: Firstly, place the cupping glass empty and apply a moderate suction. Do not prolong the placement of the cups, but rather apply them swiftly and remove them quickly to allow the humors to flow into the affected area evenly. Continue this process, repeating it multiple times until the area becomes red and open.
When the affected area appears red, apply more suction slowly and then observe the condition of the body. If a person has soft flesh with porous pores, it is enough to apply one cup only, otherwise, the area may ulcerate.
The cupper should expand the area of suction, deepen it slightly, and adjust the suction with gentleness and delicate movement. If there is thick blood, it is necessary to apply the cup twice: once to open the path for the smooth flow of blood and moisture, and a second time to completely remove the thick blood.
If the blood is very turbid, repeat the cupping process a third time to achieve the desired result. In general, if one wishes to remove a small amount of blood, a single cupping session is sufficient. However, for removing large amounts of blood, apply more cups, especially if the blood is thick.
It is necessary to apply a deep incision and a moderate amount of pressure, reaching only the depth of the skin, when using cauterization. Oils, water, and other substances should be avoided during the procedure, as recommended by the specialist. For those with thick, tough, dry skin, with narrow pores, special care is required.
The incision sites should be treated with opening, softening, and dissolving oils, such as myrrh, rose oil, sweet almond oil, or pumpkin seed oil, especially during the summer months.
If it is during the winter, then use oils such as narcissus, iris, chamomile, or lily, and for a thick, cold residue, consider oils like ginger, fennel, licorice, or mint.
If the patient has large pores and soft, fresh skin, it is best to avoid oil and these individuals should wash their incisions after cauterization with rosewater or cold water or grapevine water or cucumber water or mullein water, especially if they have excessive moisture in their blood.
They should wash their incisions with vinegar or water mixed with sumac, and for those with thick residues, use an old drink or water of ginger, fennel, mint, or chamomile. Be cautious about performing cauterization in baths or immediately after a bath.
It is necessary to use (the treatment) after exiting the bath, either one or two hours later, and no one should sleep after cupping, what the person who undergoes cupping and sweating should arrange before and after cupping.
If the person who undergoes cupping (has) a jaundiced condition with predominant sharpness and inflammation in their blood, they should take cooling agents such as pomegranate, dandelion, vinegar, lettuce, henbane, and similar plants, and make their diet consist of fried foods, lamb meat, sour fruits, and similar items.
For those with a cold temperament, they should drink honey water, mib or bitter henbane seed beverages, and moderate aromatic wine (between old and new), and be instructed to eat little, consuming mainly fried foods, artichokes, quail, and pigeon chicks as safecooked dishes, and the beverage on the day of cupping and sweating should be more abundant than the food.
In some cases, certain people should be given a dose of a distinguishing antidote or musk medicine or chila (a traditional remedy) before cupping or sweating, or after, to strengthen the major organs and thin the blood; however, it is not recommended for those who are heated or agitated.
The cuppings that are unconditional are those applied to the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidney area, and hip joint, because these organs do not tolerate conditions, but rather, they aim either to draw blood from one organ to another.
Placing the cup on the breasts in case of nosebleed, or using it to apply cold air to a member that has become congested, as we do with the cup placed on the abdomen and navel, which loosens the member, warms it, and alleviates pain to release that congestion.
It can also be placed on the kidneys if there is an obstruction or stones, where its suction force may open the obstruction or dislodge the stones, and similarly for the liver and spleen when gases become disturbed in them. These cups can also be used simply by sucking.
It can also be used over fire or filled with warm water for issues related to constipation. The cup should be filled, either solely with hot water or with water that has been boiled with certain herbs suitable for this purpose.
Then it is placed over the area, held, removed, and repeated as needed. This is a depiction of the cup used over fire:

The use of a cold iron depends on the age and physical build of the patient, as it is not the same for boys, slender individuals, or men with well-developed bodies. Chapter 79
On the treatment of hemorrhoids.
The needle is primarily used in most cases for organs where stitches cannot be placed, either because they are too small, such as the lip, gum, and the like, or because the organ is exposed without flesh, such as the finger, nose, and the like.
The method of using them is to take the needles that are in fresh, clean water free from impurities, and then leave them in the water for a day and night until they become hungry and empty within. Then the body should first purge itself through phlebotomy or cupping, and then the affected member should be cleansed.
Until it turns red, then the needle should be placed upon it. If it fills up, it will fall off, and if possible, suction at the site with a cupping glass is most beneficial. Otherwise, wash the site with vinegar followed by plenty of water, massage and squeeze; if the bleeding persists after the needle falls off, apply a cold water-soaked cotton cloth.
Place it on top until the discharge stops. If the bleeding is excessive, sprinkle powdered glass, or sumac, or similar astringents until it stops, or apply half of a freshly peeled and sliced calamus root to the site.
Leave it until the calamus sticks to the site; then the bleeding will stop. If, however, it is necessary to use a new needle, do not use the same one if possible. If the needle refuses to adhere, clean the site with fresh blood.
If you insert a needle into the wound to express blood, and then remove it, when it comes into contact with the wound, it adheres. If you wish to remove it, sprinkle some frankincense or salt or ash onto it, and it will come away from the wound.
Chapter III
In algebra.
This chapter also deals with the art of healing, specifically fracture reduction and bone setting.
Know, O sons, that this chapter is claimed by physicians and the common folk, and there are those who have never even heard of or read a word from the ancient texts on this subject.
For this reason, the knowledge of this art has become nonexistent in our country, and I have never encountered an expert in it. I have only gained knowledge from it through lengthy study of the works of the ancients, and my eagerness to understand them, until I extracted their science from them; then, I devoted myself to experience and training throughout my life.
I have presented to you in this chapter all that my knowledge encompasses on the subject. Having subjected it to my experience, I have brought it near to you, freeing it from the complexities of elongation, summarizing it to its utmost, explaining it in the clearest manner, and depicting for you numerous images of the instruments used in it.
It is indeed a further explanation, as I have done in the preceding chapters, and there is no power but that of the Most High, the Great.
Chapter One.
In this chapter, after presenting the general principles of bone fractures and dislocations, before detailing the specific broken and dislocated members, one should first outline essential concepts through sentences and sections. You must first understand and grasp the truth of it, for yourself and those eager to learn this noble art.
So I say, whenever one experiences a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall, you must first rush to reduce or stabilize it, or both. If there is no obstacle, such as weakness of strength, or if the affected person is a young child or an elderly person, or if the time is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then, his diet should be restricted to cold legumes, bird meat, and venison. He should avoid drinks, fatty meats, overindulgence in food, and any meal that fills the vessels with blood. Until the swelling is resolved and there is no risk of fluid accumulation at the site.
Then, the patient should return to his initial diet, which consists of foods that are rich, dense, and durable, such as porridge, rice, heads of cattle, and beef stomachs. Eggs, fresh fish, thick drinks, and similar foods are part of this regimen, which ensures that the fracture heals faster and better. Note that broken bones in strong adults or elderly people may never heal completely to their original state.
Due to the dryness and hardness of their bones, what was once flexible may heal together like the bones of young children, but nature produces a substance on the broken bone from all sides, similar to glue, which thickens and sticks, causing the pieces to adhere tightly to each other.
They stick together so firmly that the joint becomes as strong and secure as it was before, without hindering any of its functions.
For this reason, the patient's diet should include foods that are firm, viscous, and thick as we have mentioned. Know that fractures may vary in type depending on the differing parts of the body, for a fracture of the femur bone is different from a fracture of the skull bone, and a fracture of the rib cage bone differs from a fracture of the spinal bone.
Similarly, all other parts of the body differ from one another, and we will mention each type of fracture, explaining it in detail in its own section, without distinguishing between them. The type of bone fracture may also vary within itself.
Because it may be a fracture without chips, and it may be a fracture along the bone with chips and protrusions that are either healing or unhealed, and the fracture may be accompanied by wounds and breaches in the skin, and it may be a slight crack.
Each type has a specific method of treatment, which will be detailed in its own place, and one can recognize a bone fracture by its deformity, displacement, sensitivity, and the popping or crunching sound it makes when touched with the hand.
If there is no apparent deformity, popping, or sensitivity when examining the bone, and the patient does not experience severe pain, it is not a fracture but could be a sprain, a minor break, or a slight crack. Therefore, one must not attempt to move the bone by stretching or touching it.
Carry out the application of the medicines mentioned later, which are appropriate for the condition, and then tighten it gently. Know that when a bone breaks and cracks into two parts without causing shards, it is still possible for each part to detach from the other.
You should take the initiative to correct and align it as soon as you notice any deviation, before a hot swelling occurs. If a hot swelling does appear, leave it for some days until the hot swelling subsides, then straighten it as best as you can with gentleness and ingenuity.
Know that repairing and aligning it is easier than a bone with fragments, then proceed with what will be mentioned later. If the bone has fragments, it is necessary to extend the broken member from both sides, whether it is a hand or a leg.
Either use your hands if the member is small, or two strings, or a string with a hand, and place the member in a flat position on its natural shape. As soon as part of the broken bone extends, then cut off these excess parts and return them to their proper positions using all possible means of gentleness and ingenuity.
Take care to avoid causing any pain or discomfort to the patient with your actions. Strive to bring one bone back to its companion in the best way possible. At this time, you should touch and feel them with your hand, and if you find anything incorrect, correct and align it within your abilities.
Beware of excessive swelling and strong inflammation, as many ignorant people do, often causing a hot lump or a tumor in the affected area, as you have seen them do repeatedly.
Then, after adjusting and perfecting it, you should command the affected area to rest and caution the patient against moving it during wakefulness, sleep, turning over, defecation, or any other movement within their capability, ensuring that the position of the member is such that it relieves pain.
If, while in a certain position, the member feels pain or discomfort, it should be moved to a different position where there is no pain, and care should be taken to ensure that this new position is level and straight.
To prevent any deformity in the member if it is forced, and regarding the method of binding a broken member, as I have described to you; know that broken members vary in size and shape, whether small like the arm, finger, ulna, or others, or large like the leg or hip.
Therefore, the bandages should be soft and flexible for small members like the arm or finger, but broader and stiffer for larger ones such as the thigh, back, or chest, to ensure that the wide bandage supports the large member equally from all sides without any gap.
Then, after you have finished preparing, you should begin to apply the appropriate paint. In a gentle compression on the fracture site, you begin by wrapping the bandage around the fracture itself, three or four wraps as required by the member, slightly tightening your hand with the bandage, then moving it upwards from the fracture site.
With less tension than you applied to the fractured site, you then slightly separate the wrap from the fracture and relax the tension a little bit until you achieve the correct amount of pressure. Then, take another bandage and wrap it around the fractured area as well.
Wraps, then move the bandage to the lower side of the fracture. In the same manner as described for the first wrap above, adjust the tension and relaxation. Place a layer of soft padding or cloth between the wraps to equalize the curvature of the fracture.
If there is a curvature, do not add anything; otherwise, wrap another bandage over the existing ones and secure with firm splints if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the member.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, apply something to soothe the swelling and relieve the pain, leaving it for several days. Then, securely wrap with splints made from half-inch wide strips of carefully carved wood or splints fashioned from wooden sieve frames.
Gipuses made from cedar, palm fronds, juniper, or gravel, and other similar materials readily available, should be of the same size and shape as depicted here.
However, if the splint to be placed on the broken bone itself is thicker and wider than the others, its length should be made according to the size of the broken member, as depicted in the image.
Then, secure the splints with another bandage, following the same tension as the initial one. Bind it above with strong threads, emphasizing the pressure on the broken area. The tension should be less the further it is from the break.
The threads should be of medium thickness and softness, preferably made from wet flax. Using thick threads, as often done by the ignorant who make their threads from strips of untwisted flax, is a grave error.
Because tension with these thin threads falls outside the optimal range, and they do not allow for precise control of the pressure. There should be no space between the splints larger than a finger.
If the patient is troubled by the edges of the splints after proper tensioning, place soft padding underneath, such as cloth or wool, to prevent further discomfort. If there is a fracture with an associated wound and skin laceration, this will be discussed separately.
Know that not every broken member should be bound with splints from the first day. For if the member is large, splints should not be applied until after five or seven days, or more, according to your security from the occurrence of hot swelling.
This describes the bandages that were used by the ancients for fractures, wounds, and dislocations, a general dressing that sets the bone and is suitable for all conditions, especially for children and women, as it does not have heat or cold.
It is to take flour dust, the inner part of the grain that clings to the walls of the mill when it turns, and knead it as it is, without sifting it with egg white, making sure it is neither too thick nor too thin, then shaping it.
Another type of dressing for fractures, dislocations, and wounds: take hair from goats, sheep, and lambs, and add 10 drams each of mastic, resin, and frankincense, 5 drams of myrrh and galangal, and 20 drams of Armenian or Roman clay. Mix well. All should be ground and mixed with myrtle water or egg white if the patient's condition is hot. This dressing, when used, is harmonious, quickly setting broken bones and suitable for most people due to its moderate nature.
A different type of dressing is also beneficial for fractures and wounds, consisting of 10 drams each of marshmallow, licorice, and white henna, 5 drams each of sumac and myrrh, 6 drams of Armenian clay, and 20 drams of wheat. All ingredients are finely ground, sieved, mixed with water or egg whites, and applied.
A dressing for joints and bones that have moved out of place, alleviating the accompanying pain and headaches, is prepared by soaking wool in vinegar and cooked oil, then applying it to the affected area. This dressing does not possess any strong healing properties.
However, it is particularly effective in alleviating swelling and pain, especially heat-related swelling. A different dressing is prepared for a broken bone, consisting of ground mulberry leaves and wild poppy seeds mixed with moistened material. This concise dressing is used when there is a fracture of the bone.
If you wish to further reduce the swelling, prepare a mixture of equal parts of henna, chamomile, rose petals, and fennel flour, or, if the member is fresh, use parsley water instead. Apply this dressing.
A stronger dressing for reducing swelling, to be used when a solid bruise occurs with a broken bone, is prepared from equal parts of henna, flaxseed, cumin, rosemary, marjoram, rose petals, and chamomile.
All apply and mix with water or fresh water or paint, depending on the heat of the member and the quietness of its temperature; while Hippocrates did not mention in his book to place anything on the broken member when setting it.
Except for the caulk made from wax and oil, it is described as being neither too thick nor too thin, and Galen suggested placing substances with a drying effect and some heat on the broken member during its setting, such as salt, pepper, resin, and similar materials as we have mentioned.
The dressing is beneficial for weakness and pain, take a mixture of lint, sesame seeds, human hair, or bird feathers, and salt, grind them finely, mix well, and apply as a bandage. The amount of the bandage should be adjusted according to the injury.
Then, observe: if no pain, itching, or movement of the broken bone occurs, do not remove the bandage for many days. If symptoms such as intense itching, discomfort, or swelling appear at the site, then remove it.
Act swiftly and remove the bandage, do not delay. Then, take a soft cloth or sponge and soak it in lukewarm water, gently apply it to the affected area to relieve itching and pain, allowing the member to rest for an hour.
Then, place on it the softened wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose fat, and secure it with a light bandage for a night until the hot swelling is alleviated, the puffiness of the member subsides, and its pain disappears. Then return it to the gentle tension and a light dressing, not applying as much pressure as before.
Treat it gently until it heals, if you find that the swelling, redness, pain, puffiness, and all symptoms have completely disappeared and you need to apply a bandage and tension, then do so as before. If nothing of what we mentioned occurs in the member.
Do not remove the bandage unless it has been three, four, five, or seven days, and sometimes even after 20 days, depending on the condition of the member as we have said. If the fracture is healing and the tissue is nearly closed around it.
At this point, also increase the tension more than your initial bandage and also thicken the diet of the patient as mentioned before. If you find that the fracture site has dried out and become thinner than it should be, know that the food is not reaching it.
Then, every time you remove the bandage, wash it with warm water. Do this every three days and slightly reduce the tension. This action promotes blood flow to the member and speeds up healing. Avoid what the ignorant do, who break the member again due to excessive tension.
If a wound does not heal as it should and heals despite being crooked, it is a mistake due to their actions and a great risk. If it were correct, the pioneers would have mentioned it in their books and practiced it. I have seen no trace of such a thing in their work. And indeed, the correct course of action is not to follow it.
Chapter Two.
On the Occurring Fracture in the Head.
There are many types of fractures occurring in the head, with various shapes and diverse causes. Some fractures result from a sword strike, where the bone is completely broken all the way to the duramater beneath the bone. As the blow does with wood.
This type of fracture is therefore called 'cadomi' (coming-in). The sword may cut some of the bone, leaving its face intact without penetrating to the end, and this type of fracture is called a complete avulsion. These fractures can be either large or small.
There are also fractures that are shattering or crushing, caused by a blow with a stone or falling onto a stone or the like. This type of fracture can either penetrate closely to the duramater beneath the bone or affect the face of the bone.
And the wound of these fractures may be either large or small, and some fractures are subtle, hidden in the bone, like a slight split.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called "shapely" (or "hairline"), and some fractures result from a fall or a blow from a stone or similar object that enters the bone surface inward, creating a depression similar to what happens when copper is struck by a hammer, with part of it entering inward.
This is most common in the soft bones of a child's head, and all types of fractures may have loose or stuck fragments. We will discuss the treatment of each type in its proper place, and we will identify these fracture types through examination.
They are examined with probes, and corrupt flesh is removed from them. For a hairline fracture, the bone is inspected and marked with ink; if the fracture appears black, it indicates a break. In treating fractures, one must first consider the patient's symptoms.
If you observe symptoms indicating fear, such as vomiting bile, restlessness, disorientation, loss of voice, fainting, fever, red and bulging eyes, or other similar symptoms, do not approach the patient or treat them. For these symptoms strongly suggest that death is imminent.
And if you see symptoms that do not alarm you and you wish for his safety, then take charge of his treatment. This is because if the wounded person comes to you in the initial stages of wounding, especially during the winter days,
You should make every effort to remove the bone before the 14th day, regardless of the season. However, in summer days, you should expedite the removal of the bone before the 7th day, before the membrane beneath it spoils, thus exhibiting the symptoms we described.
If the fracture of the bone has reached the membrane covering the brain and is accompanied by crushing and bruising, you should then cut off the damaged part as I have described, which involves shaving the head of the patient with a wound.
And when examining the bone, uncover it in whatever way you can and according to the shape of the wound, and if you notice any bleeding or hot swelling during the examination, treat it as necessary.
This involves packing the wound with cloth soaked in a mixture of rosewater and fat, cleaning the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding stops, then proceeding to trim and extract the bone, which can be done through one of two methods.
The first method is to cut the bone using a delicate, narrow blade, and this is its image:

And use gentleness in striking the segment to avoid shaking the head and causing it harm, for if the bone is strong and hard, you should drill around it before using the segments with drills called non-immersion chisels, which they call non-penetrating chisels.
Because they do not exceed the limit of the skull bone beyond it, for the drill has a rounded tip below its sharp head, similar to a ring or small circle, which prevents it from penetrating and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
And you should take several of these chisels, each suitable for the thickness of a particular bone, so that you have for every skull a drill with a sharp tip in length and shortness according to the thickness of that skull, and this is an image of three types.
