And as for the method of drilling around a broken bone, it is to place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated, then move the drill to another location and make sure that the distance between each hole is equal to or similar to the thickness of the rod.
Then, cut with scissors between each two holes, acting with all the care possible as we have said, until you remove the bone, either by hand or with some other tool prepared for this purpose.
Like the forceps and tweezers, you must be extremely careful not to touch the drill or scissors with the peritoneum. Once you have strengthened the bone and it is free from the peritoneum, if it has been attached, you should clean it.
The roughness remaining on the bone should be smoothed with another tool similar to scissors, but finer and more delicate. If any small fragments or chips of bone remain, they should be carefully removed with the tools at your disposal.
Then, the wound should be treated with bandages and ointments as mentioned. The other aspect of this work is straightforward and free from danger, as described by Galen and highly praised, as follows: You should first expose the portion of the bone at the site where the fracture is most severe and prominent. Once you have exposed this part, place the tip of this lens-shaped scissor under it.
The posterior part of the lens is smooth and does not cut, while the sharp part on its sides extends along the length as you see, so that the posterior part rests on the peritoneum, and the face of the sharp cut is on the bone, then strike the cut from one side.
Use a small hammer to cut through the bone gently as it spins, and be secure in the knowledge that the membrane will not suffer any frightening mishap, even if the healer is the most ignorant and cowardly among people, yes, even while asleep.
If anything remains necessary for the bone from the membrane in certain places of the bone, scrape it off with the same lens cutter's tip and remove it gently, for it will depart without harm or fear.
If the bone fracture did not penetrate the membrane and what broke off from the bone is still rough with fragments remaining, it is necessary to smooth that roughness and remove those fine fragments using delicate forceps. You should have a set of different sized instruments to choose from for each situation, according to your work.
You should examine the broken bone and its shape, and first use the widest forceps in your set to remove any coarse fragments. Then, after that, use a more precise pair, continuing in this manner with increasing precision until you reach the thinnest and most delicate forceps.
And as for the other small cracks and delicate fractures, one should use a treatment for each according to what leads to its healing, which is something not hidden from those who have even the slightest experience in this craft and have studied our writings and explanations regarding major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when removing the bone, one should take a piece of cotton cloth fitting the size of the wound and dip it in rose fat, then place it over the wound. Then take another cloth, folded or cut into a triangle shape. Dip it in a drink and rose fat, then place it on the first cloth, making this as gentle as possible to avoid weighing down the peritoneum. Then use a wide bandage and tighten it only enough to hold the wound in place.
Leave it with this treatment for one or two days until the hot swelling subsides, then remove the bandage and use medications that have a drying effect, such as roots of colchicum, flour of crocus, powdered galangal, and ziraund, among others, by making dust from these drugs.
Sprinkle the wound with these dry powders, and in general, use a remedy that clarifies without burning. Strive during treatment to keep the wound clean, free from fat or dirt, and never allow pus to accumulate.
Because when pus collects on the brain membrane, it corrupts and putrefies it, causing great harm to the patient; and it may occur in the cranial sac when the bone becomes exposed, especially if treatment is neglected.
If you observe a blackening on its surface, and the patient exhibits the symptoms we mentioned, know that death is inevitable. If the blackening occurred due to a treatment, that treatment was potent enough to cause this blackening.
One should take a portion of honey and three portions of rose fat, mix them well, apply the mixture to a cloth, place it on the cranial sac, and then treat the patient with all the necessary remedies until they recover.
Chapter III
In the treatment of a broken nose.
Know that the nose is broken when both upper fissures (or one of them) are affected, as they are bone; but the lower part, being cartilage, does not break, rather it may be bent, deformed, or compressed.
If one fissure is broken, you should insert your little finger into the nasal cavity and straighten the break from inside with your index and thumb fingers from outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. This should be done gently, taking care not to cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is at the tip of the nose and no finger has entered it, it should be adjusted with a rod slightly thicker on one side, and if the fracture is on both sides, do the same. It is necessary to hurry to fix it on the first day of the fracture.
If possible, and otherwise after the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling subsides, then insert a thread of cotton cloth into the nasal hole for a single fracture, or two threads for both sides.
The threads should be thick enough to fill the nasal hole, and some of the first surgeons recommended greasing them with lard and changing them every day, but I do not agree with this. Instead, I suggest soaking the threads in a mixture of egg white and powder from the mill.
Then, leave the threads until the bone heals and the cartilage hardens. You can insert tubes made of duck feathers into the nose where the threads are placed, wrapped in soft cloth, which will help keep the fracture in place and prevent the patient from having difficulty breathing.
This is not strictly necessary, but if you wish to create the threads, you can do so. If a hot swelling appears during your work, apply a poultice or a cotton ball soaked in vinegar and oil of rose or any diachylon paste to the nose.
If a hot tumor does not appear, it should be bandaged from the outside with fine flour and ground asafoetida mixed with egg white, then a soft compress is placed on top. Do not tie the nose to anything at all; if the nasal bones break, they should be set in small pieces.
If the tumor is fractured, it should be punctured and the fragments removed with the appropriate instrument, then the wound is stitched and treated with suitable ointments for healing. If there is an injury inside the nose, it should be treated with fusing and lead pipes. so that it heals.
Chapter IV.
In the case of broken lower jawbones.
If the lower jawbone breaks without a wound, and it is not broken in two places, but rather bends inward, its position can be easily corrected.
If the break is on the right side, insert the index finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth, and similarly, if the break is on the left side, insert the index finger of the right hand and gently lift the broken piece from inside to outside.
With your other hand, control the bone's alignment from outside, if a fracture of the jaw has split into two parts, it should be used with pressure from both sides to achieve straightness for its adjustment. If it occurs in the teeth, they will sprout again.
Or, if they separate, tighten what you hope to keep with a gold or silver thread or linen, then place on the broken beard a layer of asphaltum, followed by a folded cloth and, over that, a large, tight splint or a piece of leather from a shoe, equal in length to the beard. Then, secure it from above in a manner that suits you, ensuring it aligns with the healing process, until it does not unravel. Command the patient to rest and keep them calm, providing soft porridge as food. If you notice any change in the shape, even the slightest, in any way, be quick to undo the binding on the third day.
Then, secure it from above in a manner that suits you, ensuring it aligns with the healing process, until it does not unravel. Command the patient to rest and keep them calm, providing soft porridge as food. If you notice any change in the shape, even the slightest, in any way, be quick to undo the binding on the third day. Then, prepare a new dressing with powdered stone (presumably lime) and egg white or semolina flour, after removing the asphaltum. Place a soft, flexible bandage over it. As long as this new bandage adheres well and does not change the bone's position, leave it in place.
Do not remove it until the bone has healed and the fracture has strengthened, for this fracture often takes three weeks to fully heal. If a hot swelling appears during this time, apply the remedies we have mentioned repeatedly to soothe it until the swelling subsides.
And if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, you should inspect it to see if any fragment(s) of bone remain. If so, carefully remove these fragments using the appropriate instrument, and if the opening of the wound is narrow, widen it with a scalpel as needed.
After removing the fragments, if none remain, then outline the wound edge if it is wide, and otherwise, apply one of the suitable ointments to it. And stitch the wound until it heals.
Chapter V.
On reducing the shoulder blade (if fractured).
The clavicle (shoulder blade) most commonly fractures near its end at the shoulder joint, and it can break in one of three ways: either splitting into two pieces without any fragments, which is the easiest to set; or with one or more fragments forming, which complicates the reduction process.
Either the fracture may occur with fragments, which is more difficult to reduce, or it might be accompanied by a wound. If there's no wound, have two assistants help you: one holds the arm adjacent to the fractured clavicle, while the other extends the patient's neck towards the opposite side.
Then, equalize the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, without any bumps or gouges. If more extension is needed, place a ball of cloth or wool under the patient's armpit.
And the bone should be manipulated as needed, extending and lifting the clavicle while pressing the ball with your hand until the fracture is aligned properly. If you cannot pull the end of the clavicle outward due to its depth, the patient should lie on their back.
And a pad should be placed under the patient's elbow, which a server will press downward to lift the clavicle that is deeply embedded upwards. At this point, you may correct and align the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the clavicle has broken off and is mobile, you should probe it and gently extract the fragment. If the fragment is trapped within the bone, use one of the cutting tools prepared for this purpose to dislodge it. After the fracture is reduced, place under the clavicle a splint that secures the skin (psa) – an instrument made of wood or iron, as depicted below:
It resembles a spoon without jagged edges, with its width adjusted according to the size of the bone and its length suitable for your work, one end wider than the other as you can see.
If the wound laceration that you have made in removing a fragment of bone is wide and you have secured the hot tumor, then close the wound with stitches. If the laceration is slight or you fear the hot tumor, then enlarge the wound with additional incisions and staples according to the extent of the cut.
If there is a hot tumor, moisten the incision with rose oil, vinegar, or drink, and apply it. For the fixation of a bone without any wound or cut, use a plaster made from powder from a millstone mixed with egg white. Place a soft bandage on it, then place a ball under his armpit if necessary, then take a long cloth, about an inch wide, and wrap it around the bandage and padding, and finally, take a splint made from a thin board. Make it three fingers wide and long, then insert it into a cloth, wrap the cloth with the splint in place, and secure the bandage tightly around the fracture as you would a circle. Apply it to his neck, the correct armpit, and the affected armpit, pulling it back and forth on both sides. As you can see, the tension secures the fracture firmly, which is evident.
And understand that the splint should not be removed from the broken bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient every day. As soon as the bandage relaxes and the splint is removed, correct it and tighten the bandage. Then make the patient sleep on their back and place a small cushion under their armpit when they sleep at night.
So that the arm may be raised from the side, thus raising the fracture of the clavicle with the movement of the shoulder or by binding the arm to the neck, and do not loosen the bandage unless there is a change in the condition after twelve days due to itching or swelling.
Then renew the bandage if you see that it is necessary. It should be pulled and left until the fracture of the clavicle heals and hardens, becoming stronger and more robust within twenty-eight days. It may be the case for some people in less than that time.
Chapter VI.
In the healing of the shoulder fracture, it is rare that the shoulder joint itself fractures, but rather the bones' ends or somewhere in the middle. One can determine this by touch. The shape of the fracture determines the treatment.
Restoring it to its natural shape using any method possible, then apply a mixture of mortar, egg white, and soft lint to the area. Place a pad of folded cloth on top, followed by a broad splint made from a board.
It should be cut as thin as possible, or slightly wider, covering the entire shoulder or its joints. If there is a depression under the splint, fill it with soft lint until the splint lies flat, then secure it with a long bandage.
Tightly secure and ensure the splint remains in place, checking the wrap daily. If it loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint. The patient should lie on their healthy side, and the shoulder will heal in approximately 20 days.
After 20 or 25 days, remove the wrap securely, as it is safe to do so. If any bone fragments protrude and cause discomfort under the skin, make a cut and extract them. Follow the same procedure for treating a fracture of the clavicle, including addressing any hot swelling.
Chapter VII
In the Treatment of Chest Fractures.
The chest may be fractured in its middle, but this is rare, while its extremities are more prone to fracture and dislocation. Among its symptoms, if the fracture occurs in the middle, it tends to tilt downwards, causing severe pain, difficulty breathing, and coughing.
And it may ooze blood, revealing a depression in the broken bone, which is not hidden from the senses. To set it, the patient should lie on his back, placing a pillow between his shoulders, then pressing his elbows and gathering the ribs with hands on both sides, gently smoothing it out as much as possible.
And it should be wrapped with bandages and a splint, placing a light wooden board or similar material on top, after wrapping it in a cloth. Then, gently tie it to the broken bone to prevent shifting, wrapping the bandage around the back in a circular motion and tightening it firmly. Check the bandage periodically, tightening it as needed, especially after any relaxation.
If, during the healing process, any discomfort, pain, or swelling occurs at the site, dissolve the bandage, treat the problem, and then reapply it if necessary. Face and bind it tightly until it heals.
Chapter VIII
In the case of a broken rib, know that the fracture occurs in the stiff portions adjacent to the back or at the ends from the front, due to pressure. This is because they are cartilaginous, and this fact is easily discernible through tactile inspection.
Correct the fracture by aligning the broken bone with the fingers on the firmest part, until the shape is even as it should be. Then bandage and secure the broken bone with a splint, if necessary. If the ribs are fractured inward, it will cause severe pain for the patient.
And puncture, like the puncture that affects those with a chest illness, as the bone punctures the pleura, and it also causes difficulty breathing, coughing, and spitting up large amounts of blood, which is difficult to treat, and the ancients used many tricks for it.
Some said that the patient's diet should include foods that produce gas and wind, to cause the stomach to swell and expand, pushing the fracture outward. We disapprove of this, as it may confirm the presence of a hot tumor, if one is not already present. If a tumor has occurred, this treatment would only increase and confirm it.
Some say that a cup should be applied to the site and then sucked out with force, similar to measurement, except that there is a fear that the cup may attract curiosity to the weak site. Some say that the site should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
The dressing should be placed between the ribs until it fills, making the bandage even when wrapped around in a circle, then treat the patient with treatment for bruises using food and medicine. If the patient is severely exhausted, it is something they cannot endure. The bone was penetrating the peritoneum painfully, and we were concerned for the patient. Therefore, we should make an incision at the site, expose the broken rib, and place under it the device that preserves the peritoneum described earlier, then cut the bone gently and remove it.
Then, if the wound is large, sew its edges together and treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot lump appears during this time, act quickly by soaking the dressing in rose oil and applying it to the site, also treating the patient internally to soothe the lump.
He should lie on the side that is most comfortable for sleep until he heals.
Chapter IX
In Treating Herniated Discs in the Back and Neck.
However, if the bones of the neck are fractured and such an injury is rare, it is often due to impact or pressure. Similarly, for the vertebrae in the back, if a person experiences such an injury and you wish to determine whether they will recover fully or not. Examine, if you see that his hands have become limp, numb, and paralyzed, and he is unable to move them or grip them, and even pricking or poking them with a needle causes no sensation or pain, then know that recovery is unlikely in most cases, for it is often fatal.
If he can move them and feels the pricking or poking, then know that the spinal cord is unharmed and the patient will recover with treatment. If a herniated disc in the back presents similarly, you may determine whether recovery is probable by applying these same principles.
Examine his legs; if they have become weak and show similar symptoms to the hands, then when he lies down on his back, gas and feces escape involuntarily, and when he lies on his stomach, urine escapes without control. If he lies on his back and wants to urinate but cannot, then know that he is most likely dead. Do not bother with treatment, for if none of the above symptoms occur, the condition is less severe. The treatment for the swelling and heat is to apply a cold compress.
By applying rose fat alone or with fried egg whites to the diseased fissure three times a day, until the hot tumor subsides, then apply one of the strengthening, drying bandages and secure it with a bandage, ordering rest and immobility.
He should not sleep on the affected side until the pain disappears, and if a fracture or something that has healed away is present in the bone, it should be cut through with a scalpel and the bone extracted. Then, if the wound is large, sew the edges together and treat it with sealing ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx (the tailbone) breaks, insert the thumb from the left hand into the anus.
Adjust the broken bone with the other hand as best you can, then apply a bandage and a splint if necessary, and secure it tightly.
If you feel a broken shard inside, cut through it with a scalpel and extract it, then treat the wound as previously described until it heals.
Chapter Ten
In setting a broken hip.
If the bones of the hip do not break, or if they do, it is as if they crumble at their ends, split lengthwise, and tilt inward, causing pain in the affected area for the patient, numbness, and paralysis of the leg from which the break occurred.
If you need to reduce the fracture, use your hand to manipulate it until you stand on the break and assess its shape. If the fracture is only at the ends, straighten it as best suits you, ensuring it resembles its natural shape.
If the fracture is lengthwise or has tilted inward, lay the patient on their stomach to reduce it, then dress the wound and apply a splint made of wood or leather, securing it tightly.
Do not worry about the fracture shifting or the splint coming loose; adjust the depression in the hips to fill any gaps, ensuring even pressure. Order the patient to sleep on their back or on the healthy side, and if a hot swelling appears, treat it accordingly.
Cease to extend or reduce the fracture until the hot swelling subsides, applying remedies as mentioned. Then, return to setting and securing the splint as appropriate. If any fragments or chips appear at the ends of the bone, they should not be removed.
It should not be touched from within, but only smoothed from without, and its tension should be left until it heals.
Chapter XI.
In the treatment of a broken arm.
The arm, from the elbow to the shoulder joint, if broken, should be treated by using a curved and smooth stick of moderate thickness, as shown in this figure.
Tie two bands at the ends, then hang it from a high place and have the patient sit on a chair. Then, place the broken arm on the stick so that its armpit is aligned with the middle of the curve, and hang a heavy object from above.
Or, a servant can extend it downwards, and the physician should straighten the break with both hands until it is in the correct position, as shown in the other method: have the patient lie on their back and hang their hand from their neck with a band.
Then, command two servants: one to hold the upper part of the break with their hands, and the other to hold the lower part. If you wish for a stronger pull, tighten the band under the break and above it, and have each servant pull in their respective directions.
If the fracture is near the side of the shoulder joint, the middle of the ligature should be under the armpit and the other end under the fracture towards the elbow; likewise, if the fracture is near the elbow, the ligature should be placed at that point and on the elbow itself.
Then, the fracture should be gently adjusted without force until it is in the correct position and well aligned. If there is no sign of a hot swelling, tighten it as necessary. If a hot swelling appears, do not tighten it until the seventh day, applying a compress soaked with vinegar and rose fat to it.
Once the swelling subsides, then tighten it. The procedure for tightening it is to place a bandage over the fracture, then wrap a new cloth around it, and finally gather the arm at the elbow and place the hand open on the shoulder.
Carry the cloth and tighten it on the arm and forearm so that the arm acts as a splint if there is no impediment and no change in the fractured bone. If you fear that something may change, use splints instead.
It involves placing a splint directly on the fracture, wider and stronger than other splints, with a space of one finger width between each splint, and ensuring their length covers the fracture with an additional three fingers from both sides.
Then, apply the compression as mentioned in the introduction of this book.
And it should be that the compression on the fracture site is stronger.
And the less distance between the fracture, the greater the compression should be; if you observe the application of bandages and compression as we mentioned in your time of setting the member, then do so.
And if you fear the development of a hot swelling, then leave off compression and bandages until the seventh day, as we mentioned; then inspect the binding every three days to ensure no itching, bulging, or blockage of nourishment to the member due to excessive tension, so that all these things may be remedied as we have stated.
If you are certain that none of these things will occur, then do not loosen the binding for a long time; let the patient lie on his back, with his hand on his stomach, and place under the arm a padded cushion filled with wool, moderate in thickness.
Inspect at all times, day or night, to ensure that the shape of the fractured member does not change or that the binding does not relax; rectify all these things with your best effort. Make the patient's food on a gentle diet first, so that when the bone begins to harden, it will be easier to maintain.
It is necessary to thicken his food, for the arm and leg usually gain strength within forty days, at which point they should be relaxed, and a bath taken with suitable ointments applied. If the fracture is severe and dislocated.
Do not remove the bandage from him for fifty days or two months.
Chapter XII.
In setting a broken arm.
The arm consists of two bones, called the humerus, one smaller than the other, with the smaller one attached to the thumb, and the larger one located below it. It is possible for either the larger humerus or the smaller one to break, or both may be fractured simultaneously.
When the smaller upper humerus breaks, its healing is easier and quicker. In contrast, when the lower humerus fractures, the recovery process is more difficult and prolonged. The worst case scenario occurs when both bones break simultaneously, with the healing being the most challenging and time-consuming.
When a doctor sets a broken arm, they should make the healing process as gentle as possible to align the bones correctly. If the larger humerus is fractured, the stretch should be more severe. However, if both humeri are broken, the stretch should be significantly stronger.
The hand should be positioned when setting and extending it on a cushion, with the thumb above all the fingers and the little finger below all the others, while the patient is in a seated position, square on their own body.
And let the cushion be at his side in terms of height so that the patient does not exert themselves.
Then, the attendant extends the arm from below with either their hand or a bandage, and another attendant extends it from above. The physician then adjusts the bone until it is in the best possible shape, and if there are fragments in the broken bone, they are reduced.
Adjust each fragment to its place with all your effort. If a fragment has separated and is causing the skin to be pinched, making it impossible to set, cut it off along the line of the fracture, as we have mentioned previously. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, we will discuss it in a separate chapter.
If, during your initial setting of the fracture, a hot swelling appears, apply a compress with a cloth soaked in a mixture of rose oil and white wax, which should be neither too thick nor too thin, and secure it gently. Once the swelling subsides, release the compression.
Remove the compress and apply a new one made from powdered chalk on a fine linen cloth, mixed with egg white. Then take up the splints. In most cases, there will be six splints: three for each arm. Ensure that the splint placed directly over the fracture is slightly wider and stronger than the others.
When the fracture is in one bone or in both bones, then make your traction stronger and more forceful on the site of the fracture. As you rotate the traction upwards or downwards, slightly relax the tension as mentioned earlier at the beginning of this chapter.
And let the bandage wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, not too stiff. The thread used to tighten it should be of a special kind of canvas, intermediate between softness and hardness, as we described. Check the member and ligament after several days.
If something requires repair, such as itching in the member, it should be soaked in warm water until the itching subsides. Leave the member unstressed for a night to rest, then continue with the traction.
If the traction has relaxed and the bone has shifted or similar, correct all of it to the best of your ability. Also, check if food is unable to reach the member due to excessive tension; in this case, slightly loosen the traction and leave it for several days until blood flow resumes, then tighten it again.
If the patient does not exhibit any of what we mentioned, no treatment should be initiated for 20 days or so. Then, hang the patient's hand from their neck and ensure their arm is relaxed. The patient should conserve their energy and avoid abrupt movements, keeping their sleep on their back.
Know that this fracture of the arm will heal in thirty days, or in thirty-two, and perhaps even in twenty-eight, all depending on the conditions of the humours and the strength.
Chapter XIII.
In setting a fracture of the hand and fingers.
The metacarpal bone of the hand and the phalanges of the fingers are rarely fractured, but they are frequently subjected to contusions. If a fracture or contusion occurs in the hand, it is necessary to treat it accordingly.
The patient should sit cross-legged, with a chair placed before him at the same level.
Then he places his hand over it, extends the broken bone's assistant, and the doctor aligns it until it heals properly. If no swelling occurs, then a splint should be applied, followed by a cast above, according to the location.
It should be wrapped in a soft cloth, and if the fracture is towards the palm, make a semi-sphere with a cloth and instruct the patient to grasp it with his broken hand, then secure it with a long strip of cloth.
The splint should be made of soft leather that conforms to the curvature of the hand and secures it as it should, if the fracture is protruding, a splint should be placed above and another below on the hand so that the hand remains open and straight.
Then, the tension should be carried as the hand rotates, and the fingers should be interwoven with bindings, if the fracture affects one of the phalanges, the thumb should be adjusted as it should be, then secured with the hand, and if desired, a small upright splint can be made to stabilize the fracture.
If it does not move, if the fracture affects any of the other fingers, such as the middle, index, ring, or pinky finger, they should be aligned and secured with the adjacent healthy finger, or all tied together in order, which is better, or a small upright splint can be placed on them.
As we mentioned regarding the thumb, during the splinting process, check for any swelling and, after it subsides, apply the appropriate treatment as described previously, when any of these conditions occur.
Chapter XIV
In the splinting of a thigh fracture.
The thigh bone is often broken and is easily felt because it can move forward and backward. Its splinting involves tying a band above the fracture and another below, while the patient lies on their face.
Then, each servant tie should be extended to its side in moderation, if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. But if it is near the origin of the thigh, a soft tie should be tightened, either from wool or similar material.
If the fracture is at the origin of the thigh, extending towards the groin, one tie should be above and another below the fracture; similarly, if it is near the knee, the tie should be close to the knee, allowing for extension downwards. Then, the physician should adjust the bone with both hands until it returns to its natural shape.
The bone will heal well when, in the absence of swelling or heat, it should be bandaged and secured. If a hot swell occurs, leave it for several days until the heat subsides, then proceed with treatment.
As for securing it, a wide, hard bandage should be wrapped around the fracture twice or three times, leaving enough space. Then, the leg should be twisted until the heel is at the origin of the thigh, and a long thread should be inserted between the thigh and the leg near the knee from below, with the ends of the thread brought up from above on both sides.
Then, the remaining bandage should be wrapped around the leg and thigh, placing splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture. A single brace should then be made on the femur, and the space between the thigh and leg filled with soft cloth to ensure even tension.
Then begin by tightly binding the middle portion around the fracture site, three or four times, and as you move further away from the fracture with the bandage, make your tension less, softer, and looser.
Then proceed to the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and the leg, and tie it to the subsequent bandages above, then pass the ends of the thread downwards until it ends at the heel of the man, and also tighten the ends of the bandages on the other side with it.
To prevent the bandage from slipping out of place, keep tightening it as long as there is no pain, swelling, or any other signs of damage to the member. If anything like that occurs, hurry to loosen it and rectify all such occurrences as we have mentioned repeatedly.
If there is a splinter in the bone that protrudes, you should smooth it out if possible, otherwise make a cut and remove it, treating the wound as previously mentioned until it heals. This fracture of the thigh may be secured without adding the leg with bandages.
As we mentioned regarding the arm and forearm, except that its fracture should not cause any permanent disability or limp if treated this way, but if only the upper arm is bound without adding the leg, it is inevitable that the patient will always walk with a limp.
Know that the thigh (muscle) strengthens in approximately fifty days or slightly more, or slightly less, all depending on the variations of temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter XV.
On the fracture of the knee joint.
The knee joint is rarely subjected to fracture, but it may be bruised extensively. If a fracture does occur, it is either a tear or fragmentation of its parts, which can happen with or without an accompanying wound.
You should assess all of this by touch, and realign the detached parts with your fingers until they come together and heal, to the best of your ability, gentleness, and skill. Then, you may apply a bandage and use a rounded splint if needed.
You should secure it with a suitable strap, and then monitor all the conditions described for other fractures, such as heat swelling and similar complications, addressing each occurrence with the appropriate treatment until healing is complete.
Chapter XVI.
On setting a broken leg.
The leg has two bones: one thick, called the tibia, and another thin, called the fibula, which can both suffer fractures similar to those of the arm's bones. Therefore, its treatment is the same as that of the arm, and the procedure is identical. If both bones are fractured, the leg will turn in all directions. If only the thinner bone breaks, the leg will turn forward. If the thicker bone breaks lower down, it is self-evident.
One should use traction, compression, and splints accordingly, but if the leg fracture is severe with many fragments, the traction should be less and lighter, and one must exert gentle force when treating it.
In the leg, there is an additional step: after adjusting the splints and completing all your work, take two strips of pinewood, used for smoothing out floors placed between board gaps, or palm leaves, or similar materials, to fill any gaps.
Select strips that are slightly thick, not too thin, and make them the length of the leg from the knee to the ankle. Then, wrap each one with two turns of cloth, placing one around the leg and the other on the opposite side.
They should be from the knee to below the foot, and then tie the strips in three places on each side and in the middle. With this strapping, the leg is prevented from tilting to the right or left and is shaped well. A wooden splint, lengthwise along the leg, can also be used.
It is placed there to preserve it from movement, and this should be done most especially in the case of a fracture accompanied by a particular wound. Then, inspect the leg every day, attending carefully to any swelling or puffiness or other issues. If such a thing occurs, treat it as necessary until it heals, and the leg bone will mend in about thirty days or so.
Chapter XVII
On fractures of the leg bones and fingers.
As for the heel, it never suffers a fracture, while the leg bones may indeed fracture. The fingers also rarely suffer fractures; rather, they are most likely to be dislocated in most cases.
If a leg bone fractures and you observe that the bones have shifted, the patient should place their foot on the ground in an upright position as if walking. Then, you should place your foot on the raised portion of the bones and tread down and even them out.
Until they return to their proper positions, then take the bandage and splint, place a small board with two flat ends beneath the foot, and after wrapping it with cloth and using other necessary materials, tightly secure the foot.
If three or four days have passed, then release the bandage, for you will find the bones either straightened after having been broken or dislocated, and treatment should be as you observe. If some of the fingers are broken, set them and straighten them as described in the treatment of hand fractures.
Then make a splint for the broken finger, extending along the finger and slightly wider, and under the foot place the board as described and secure it tightly. If two or three or more bones are broken.
Make a splint for each finger, graded in size, using a soft cloth, and secure the board under the foot, with each end of the board's head projecting outside the sole of the foot to ensure proper support.
You should not forget to follow all that has been mentioned for various fractures, and address each symptom as it arises, using the appropriate treatment for each case.
Chapter XVIII.
In this chapter, the text discusses fractures specific to women (fractures of the hip bone and pubic bone) and men (mentioning a specific male fracture).
When the vagina of a woman is fractured, have her sit in a squatting position, then gently bend her towards her back, and have a midwife grip the posterior portion of her vagina. The midwife should then stuff cotton into the vagina until it is filled and becomes firm, resembling a ball.
Then, the woman should be shaken and her body raised slightly; thus, the cotton will emerge until it becomes like a ball at the entrance of the vagina, and the bone fracture will heal. Then, a poultice should be placed on her back. Whenever she wishes to urinate, the cotton should be gently removed so that she can do so.
You should return to the initial procedure, then go back to the first poultice, repeating this process for about seven days, and it will heal. If you wish, you can use a goat's bladder, attach a tube to its opening, and insert the entire bladder into her vagina.
Then, blow into the tube with force until the bladder inside the vagina is inflated. This will help the bone fracture heal. Afterwards, stuff it with cotton and maintain this for several days as described until it heals. For a broken pubic bone in men or women, this method can also be used for healing.
This should be done as described for the hip bone, and the correct approach is evident to anyone familiar with this rare type of fracture. Especially for those with experience and understanding of these texts, the nature of the fracture itself often provides a clear indication.
Proceed with the correct method for its repair and stabilization. If a man's phallus breaks, insert a section of an ostrich's throat, then cover it with a piece of cloth and wrap it tightly. Leave it in this state for about three days or so until it heals.
Chapter XIX.
In the treatment of fractures with associated wounds.
One who presents with a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if the bone is large such as the femur or humerus or similar, should immediately proceed to reduce it if the conditions for reduction are met as we have previously mentioned.
If the wound is bleeding, one should immediately apply crushed glass (or similar material) to stop the bleeding, and then proceed with reducing the fracture on that same day, without delay.
If a hot swelling does not occur, but a warm swelling does, leave the reduction until the ninth day, until the warm swelling subsides, and do not attempt to reduce it on the third or fourth day, as it may result in severe complications.
If the broken bone exposes itself through the skin, one should attempt to reduce and set it with their hands gently and a slight pull, but if this is not possible, use a device made of iron, about seven or eight fingers long, to achieve reduction. And position it according to the extent of the wound.
Therefore, the physician should choose three or four, according to what is needed for treatment in each type of fracture, and they should be rounded with a slight thickness so that they do not bend when pressed upon during surgery.
They should be pointed at the end, with a bend at that tip, and the upper part should be thick while the lower half is very thin, and this is its form: .
They are called in Greek "perim" and refer to a small lever; it should be used by pressing its sharp, bent end onto the edge of the bone fragment, pushing once to align the bones slightly.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grip it well, cut off that part of the bone so that the instrument can reach it; if the bone cannot be aligned as described above, cut it with a similar-shaped incision as mentioned.
Or use a saw to cut it as best you can, then remove any remaining roughness and thin scales from the bone; if, after aligning the bone, the patient feels severe, painful discomfort, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you can return it to its natural position, do so, as this will greatly benefit the patient; if the bone healing is complete, soak a cloth in a contracting, black, special solution, especially in summer, and do not place any bandages or oily substances on the wound.
To prevent putrefaction and decay, then use bandages when you have finished setting the bone, and leave the wound uncovered by making a hole in the bandages with scissors to match the size of the wound.
Be extremely careful not to tighten the wound with the fracture, as this has often been done by ignorant physicians and has caused either death, gangrene, or a severe cough in their patients. Let your tightening be gentle and relaxing, contrary to the tightness of other fractures.
If the wound is bad or it is a large wound and you fear some of the ill symptoms we described, and there is pain in the area that is disturbing, then do not put bandages on it, and make for it wraps from strong cloth in the place where the bandages would be, and tighten it with these.
If, after a day or two, you see that pus has begun to form in the wound, then remove the cloth you put on it with a drink, and then use the twists and ointments that we usually use to treat wounds, such as the four-fold ointment and similar ones. You should also loosen the bandage.
Examine the wound every morning and evening until it heals, and you should position the limb so that pus can easily drain downward. If several days pass and the wound does not close and pus continues to flow, then know that there are small pieces of bone embedded in it.
It is necessary to examine the wound with a probe. If any of the shards have come out, extract and remove them. If some are not coming out and are causing pain by pricking the member, cut and pull them off in every way possible.
If the wound develops a cold, or an ulcer, or any other form of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary to apply the corresponding treatment described in its chapter.
And among what you should pay attention to and keep in mind: if a large bone breaks and is pressing on a member, such as the femur or upper arm, or any other large member, do not attempt to pull it out or remove it. Often, this will lead to death. Leave it until it putrefies; perhaps it will fall off on its own after twenty or thirty days.
Then, treat the wound if you see a place for treatment, otherwise leave it be.
Chapter Twenty.
On the treatment of complications that occur as a result of certain fractures.
This complication often occurs after the healing of a fracture, especially those near joints, disfiguring the member and possibly preventing it from performing its natural function.
If the complication is soft, use drugs that contract such as gum arabic, frankincense, myrrh, wormwood, and other similar substances, taking some or all of them and mixing them with a contracting solution or with egg whites or vinegar.
Carry the complication through compression, applying firm pressure and leaving it for a significant number of days without releasing it, then relaxing it and repeating the process until the complication disappears, or use a tight plate of lead as an alternative.
Lead has the property of dissolving all substances formed in the body. If the complication has hardened, intensified, and necessitates its removal, make a cut from above and remove the resulting fragment or scrape it with a scalpel until it disappears, then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In the treatment of a fracture when it heals and the member remains soft, deviating from its original state.
If a bone fracture heals while the member remains soft, weak, it is due to many causes. One of the causes is either the excessive untying and tying of ligatures in an improper manner, or the extreme tightening of ligatures to the extent that blood flow to the member is blocked, or excessive and unnecessary stretching, or excessive movements at the wrong time, or insufficient blood in the body of the patient.
To treat this, one must nourish the patient and enrich their body to increase blood flow, use baths, and bring joy and happiness, then apply pitch to the member, which will attract a great deal of nourishment to it or maintain its stretching with warm water. until blood flow is established and it returns to its natural state.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In the treatment of broken bones that are bent out of shape and prevent proper function.
When a member that has been set after healing from a broken bone shows signs of bending, or the fractured bone has healed in an awkward position, or it becomes deformed and ugly as a result. Unless the member still cannot perform its natural function, one should not accept claims that it is necessary to break the bone again from the top, as many ignorant physicians and splint-makers have done in our country.
This action is very much condemned and leads to great uncertainty, the least of which is injury. However, if the deformity and complexity are fresh, it should be washed with water that has been used to cook herbs such as plantain leaves, its root, laurel crown, and so on.
It is bandaged with relaxing compresses such as well-made diachيلون or the sap of plantain roots is taken, mixed with chicken fat and goat butter, and used as a compress, or thick figs are taken, ground with pigeon droppings and other medications called "nucile emollients.
The deformity dissolves with constant, gentle rubbing with the hands, using movements of the affected member at all times. If the deviation is old, severe, and calcified, and needs to be treated with iron.
It should be incised above, releasing the bone connection and cutting what remains of the deformity or bone with delicate cuts, using gentleness and care in this process. Then, treat the wound as mentioned earlier until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
The jaw, when dislocated, obstructs movement, deforming the member and causing severe pain and discomfort to the patient. Whenever either of the jaws is dislocated, it should be immediately attended to for its reduction.
Do not delay it at all, for if it delays the swelling of the area, making the jaw difficult to move, it should not be delayed or moved during its swelling, as it often causes the patient pain and severe discomfort.
However, if this occurs, one should promptly drain the patient and then leave it until the swelling subsides slightly, then apply hot water and fat to the member and gently return it to its place, treating each member as mentioned in its respective section.
The chapters on the jaw have also been arranged according to what was previously discussed on fractures, from the upper part of the body to its lower part.
Chapter XXIV
In the treatment of the lower jaw (fek al-laha al-sufla)
It is rare that the jaws move, and their extraction occurs in one of two ways: either they slip slightly from their positions, relaxing somewhat, or they are completely and fully dislodged, also resulting in relaxation. It is directed towards the chest, causing the patient to drool and unable to hold it, or to close their jaw, and their tongue stumbles over words. If the dislocation is slight, in most cases, it may return on its own with minimal effort.
And if the dislocation is complete, it should be reduced swiftly without delay, and this is achieved by a servant gripping the head of the patient, while the physician inserts his thumb into the origin of the jaw within the mouth, on one side.
Or, if the dislocation affects both sides of the jaw, he should insert both thumbs and all other fingers from outside to adjust it, and command the patient to relax his jaws and let them move freely in all directions, while the physician realigns the jaw and pushes it back to its proper position.
If returning the jaw is difficult, especially if both jaws are involved, use a hot water compress and fat until they become easier to manipulate, without delaying their reduction as we have mentioned. Once both jaws return to their proper position and the patient's mouth is closed and relaxed, then apply strips of cloth with beeswax and rose oil.
Then, gently bind it with a loose bandage, and have the patient sleep on his back with his head elevated on two pillows to prevent any movement to the right or left, and avoid any effort in chewing, ensuring his food is soft.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw heals, he may eat as he pleases but should do so gently, avoiding any strain on opening his mouth while eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw fully recovers.
If the jaws cannot be restored to their original position after detaching, leading to persistent inflammation, constant headaches, abdominal distension, or frequent vomiting, the patient is considered compromised.
And often, those affected will die within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Fifth.
In the reduction of the jaw, shoulder, and acromion.
As for the clavicle, it does not detach from the inside due to its connection to the chest, but it can detach externally, which is apparent to the touch. It must be set while the patient lies on their back and extends their arms.
Then, you press the site with your hand firmly, and it will return. Next, apply a bandage and splints and tighten them. The end of the clavicle adjacent to the shoulder and connected to it rarely detaches, but if it does, it should be reduced and aligned as mentioned above.
Then, apply a bandage, splints, and tension, and instruct the patient to remain motionless and at rest until they recover. With this very treatment, the shoulder joint can also be reduced if it has detached.
Chapter XXVI.
In the reduction of the shoulder dislocation.
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three ways: downwards towards the armpit, towards the chest, or, less commonly, upwards over the acromion.
It does not dislocate behind the shoulder or in front of the nerve, but most commonly it comes out downwards towards the armpit, especially in those with thin flesh, as it exits and enters quickly.
As for those with thick flesh, the opposite occurs; it exits with difficulty and enters with difficulty. Sometimes a person might suffer a blow or fall, causing the shoulder to swell and feel hot, leading one to think it is dislocated; therefore, it should be examined to confirm its condition.
Then, you should proceed with the treatment, and identify the dislocation by comparing the injured shoulder with the healthy one; you will find a clear difference, and upon examining the area near the armpit, the head of the shoulder bone will feel depressed, like an egg when pressed.
The patient is unable to raise their hand to their ear or move it through all its movements; likewise, if it dislocates towards the chest or over the acromion, this will be clearly visible to anyone.
And if the jaw is soft or the patient is a child, then it can be reduced by having a servant raise his hand above, and you place your thumbs under the armpit and lift the joint forcefully back to its position, while the servant raises his hand and extends it above.
Then, by gently lowering it, it will quickly return. If it does not return as mentioned and the jaw has been dislocated for a long time, the patient should take a hot bath and use relaxants such as cooking roots of fennel, fenugreek, and royal crown in water.
Then the patient lies on his back, and a woolen ball is placed under the armpit, neither too soft nor too hard. The doctor then places his hand on the ball and takes the patient's hand in his own, extending it downward.
He pushes the ball with his foot, and it returns to its original position. Alternatively, you can reduce it by bringing in a taller assistant who stands on one side of the patient, places his elbow under the patient's armpit, and raises the armpit upwards, suspending the patient in the air. Another servant pulls the patient's hand towards his lower abdomen. If the patient is lightweight, something heavier should be hung to make him heavier, and the jaw will return on its own. It may also be reduced in a different manner.
It is to focus on the ground a long wooden rod with a rounded head like the pestle, neither thick nor thin, and place it under the armpit of the patient after placing a soft cloth over the head of the rod while the patient stands along the length of the rod.
Then he extends his hand downward on the other side and also extends his body from the other side with force, and the joint will quickly return to its place. If it is difficult to restore it using all that has been mentioned, use this treatment: take a wooden rod about two arms' length. It should be four fingers in width and two fingers in thickness, with a rounded head to easily enter the armpit as shown.
Then, wrap a soft cloth around the rounded head to prevent damage to the rod. Position it under the armpit and extend the entire hand and arm downward on the rod, then secure the rod to the upper arm, forearm, and wrist.
Then, place the arm on a support. Pull the hand downward while keeping the rest of the body suspended from the other side; the joint will naturally enter its place. Once it is in place, one should put a moderately sized ball of wool under the armpit.
Then, take the prepared bandage made from flour, frankincense, and egg white, and apply it to the entire shoulder. Tighten the ball under the armpit securely and wrap the bandage around it from above, then hang the patient's hand on their neck.
He should rest his hand for seven days, and the patient's diet should be light to strengthen the member, thus aiding its recovery. After seven or five days, the limb can be moved; if it holds firm and does not weaken, then it is healed.
If the joint dislocates repeatedly due to moisture or another condition, then the three-fold burn should be applied as mentioned in the chapter on burns. If you do all this and after seven days the joint does not hold firm.
If you have re-dressed and bandaged it multiple times, but the joint does not hold, becomes weak, and cannot be raised, then know that the nerve at the top of the shoulder socket is either severed, stretched, or relaxed; in which case the joint will never stay in place.
As for the jaw that is positioned towards the chest and breasts, and which can be moved by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns, all forms of tension and treatment should be applied to it until it heals. If, after healing, a sensation persists in the member and its movement slows down.
The patient should frequently use the bath until the sensation in the member softens and returns to its original state.
Chapter 27
In the treatment of the dislocated shoulder.
The shoulder joint dislocates with difficulty and returns with difficulty, moving in all directions, particularly forward or backward. Its dislocation is evident to the eye and can be felt by touch, revealing its shape.
If you compare the dislocated shoulder with the healthy one, you will notice a distinct indentation in the joint, preventing the arm from bending and the shoulder from moving freely, as if there is no contact with the acromion (the bony ridge at the top of the shoulder). It is imperative to act swiftly and reduce the dislocation before a hot swelling develops.
If a hot swelling occurs, it makes the reduction of the dislocation difficult, and in some cases, it may never heal completely, especially if the dislocation is backward. This type of dislocation is the most severe and painful, often leading to death.
If reduction is possible, the assistant should extend both hands and arms fully, while the surgeon applies pressure from above and below the dislocation using the tips of his fingers or the palm of his hand, until the joint returns to its proper position.
If the dislocation is forward, it can be reduced by bending the hand once and striking the acromion with the palm of the hand, or by having two assistants stretch the arm strongly until the joint returns to its place. If this does not work, a stronger force may be applied by having two assistants stretch the arm vigorously.
The patient also has two assistants so that he does not slip at the time of stretching, then the arm is turned in every direction after it is wrapped around his hand with a folded cloth or a wide bandage, and if the doctor begins to treat the joint with his hands, he cleans them with oil to facilitate the movement of the joint.
Then the joint is pushed firmly until it returns, and after its return, one should apply a bandage with compression and drying, along with egg white, and tighten it tightly and suspend the arm from the patient's neck and leave it for several days.
Then, if the joint remains in place, release the bandage and leave it, but if you see that the joint has not strengthened, reapply the bandage and bandage again and leave it for several days until it strengthens, then release it. If any pain occurs after the joint returns and its release, treat it accordingly.
If there is difficulty in movement, use moistening in the bath and gentle massage and squeezing until it softens, or place a fat goat's skin over the joint and bind it, leaving it on for a day and night, then remove it and immerse it in a bath, and if it sweats, rub the joint gently.
Then apply the skin over the joint again, once, twice, and a third time, while immersing it in the bath until it softens. If you prefer to place wet, thin cow manure with butter on it and tighten it, do this several times, for it will soften and return to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight
In the treatment of wrist dislocation.
The wrist joint is often dislocated and its reduction is easier than that of other joints, except that it should be reduced immediately upon dislocation to prevent the site from becoming stiff or developing a hot swelling.
To reduce the dislocation, place the patient's wrist on a table, have a helper extend the patient's hand, and the physician places his hand on the joint, pushing it back until it is reduced, but ensure that the dislocation has returned to the palm side of the hand.
The patient should place the palm of their hand on the board during extension and flexion, if the dislocation is visible on the back side of the hand, they should place the inside of their hand on the board so that the physician's hand can be placed on the same joint. If it reduces immediately, wrap it tightly with compressive bandaging to prevent re-dislocation.
Do not attempt to reduce it again, as it will not yield and you will not be able to do so after several days have passed. If the joint remains dislocated but the patient is not experiencing pain or cannot flex their hand, there is no harm done.
When it is discovered that a nerve has been severed or damaged, there is no other course of action but to cauterize it. Sometimes this can be effective, sometimes not. Once the wrist is healed, apply the dressing as described and secure it, leaving it for five days.
Then the hand should be moved and exercised. If its movement is difficult or there is any hardness, it should be softened with warm water and frictioned repeatedly until it becomes flexible.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
In the treatment of dislocated fingers.
If a finger becomes dislocated and turns in any direction, such that it points to the palm or the back of the hand, stretch the finger and push the joint with your thumb until it returns to its proper position. Then bind the top of the finger and hang it towards the side it had turned to.
Leave it for two days, then release it and stretch it until it stands upright. If it is night, tie it as described previously. Continue to loosen it during the day, move it, and re-tie it at night, doing this for several days until it strengthens.
Similarly, if a finger becomes dislocated and turns towards the palm, tie it to point in that same direction and perform your initial procedure until it heals. Do the same for any other direction it may turn.
Chapter Thirty
In the treatment of a spinal vertebrae dislocation.
When a spinal bead becomes completely dislocated or numerous beads have moved from their positions, treatment is not possible as death approaches the patient rapidly.
A sign of this is that the patient's stool exits without control, unable to hold it, and often some of their members relax, either both legs or arms, or one of them, and if only one bead moves from its position, it often detaches.
It can detach in four directions, if it moves towards the back, it is called a hunch. Its treatment involves examination; if the hunch occurred during childhood, there is no cure and it will never improve. However, if it resulted from a fall or blow, etc.,...
He has mentioned these treatments in detail in various ways, but much of it is not beneficial. I have condensed the essential points from his explanations and descriptions, presenting a simplified version of the techniques.
I say that the hunch occurring at the front of the chest, or on both sides, is incurable and beyond any remedy. However, the one that appears in the back can be treated by placing the patient face down.
On a bed placed near a wall, the patient lies with their chest supported by a moist pad to prevent injury, then a vertical wooden pole is inserted into a hole in the ground at one end of the bed nearest the head, and another wood at the opposite end towards the feet on the other side of the bed.
And a server holds the pole, not fixed in the hole, and another server holds the other in the same manner, then they wrap around the patient's chest and under their armpits with a tight, soft cloth, and extend the cloth's end to the pole at the head and tie it there.
Then, another cloth is tensioned over their hips and above their knees and at their ankles, and all the bindings are gathered and tied to the other pole at their feet, then each server extends their pole with the binding towards their respective side without moving the poles from their fixed positions.
Except that they are not fixed as we said, and the physician places their hands on the poles with force until they return, or places a board on top of them and leans on it with his legs until they return. If this treatment does not work, take a board that is about three arms long.
A space is dug in the wall, as we mentioned, close to the patient, into which one end of the board is inserted. Then, the middle of the board is placed on the hump, and the physician places his legs on the other end, tightening it well to compress the pole.
Return it to its place, or if you prefer, make it with a spiral twisted by hand; this involves driving into the ground at the head of the patient on one end of the bed two wooden poles of a length equal to a arm, and at his feet on the other end of the bed also two wooden poles.
And after that, between each pole, a space of one span should be left, and in each pole a hole made, through which the spiral is passed and all the poles are firmly fixed in the ground so as not to move at all; then a round stick, which is the spiral, is inserted into the holes of both poles.
At the end of it, a hole is made in which a stick one span long is inserted and twisted; likewise, in the other two poles. Then the bandage that was tied on the chest of the patient is tightened around the spiral at his head, and the bands that were tied on his legs are tightened around the spirals at his feet.
Then, at each spiral, a servant stands and twists his hand with the tool that twists the spiral, while the doctor adjusts the curve as previously described. And this is a picture of the spiral, the bed, and the patient: .
Then, after the spine is in place and the position is even, the dried bandage made from egg whites should be applied, followed by a splint; then, a board of approximately three fingers width and length enough to cover the hump should be placed on top of the bandage.
And then, after some correct stitching, it should be bound with the appropriate bandage and the patient should be given soft food until they recover. If there remains some scar tissue at the site even after recovery, medications that relax and soften should be used along with the board which we have described in detail before.
A lead plate may also be used for this purpose, and sometimes a scar tissue can remain after the last stitches of the back, leading one to suspect a dislocation, which could be an excess bone that has formed, so do not attempt this treatment, as it might result in death.
Chapter Thirty-One
In the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the elbow joint only become dislocated, not subjected to the slight displacement and deformity as other joints do; and the hip joint can dislocate in four ways.
This is because the hip joint can dislocate towards the inside, outside, front, or back; however, it most commonly dislocates inward, and seldomly towards the front or back. A sign that the joint has dislocated inward is when, by comparing the healthy leg to the affected leg, the latter appears shorter.
The knee is bent more than it should be, and the patient cannot bend their leg at the thigh. The area following the thigh is clearly swollen, as the head of the femur has moved there.
When the jaw deviates outward, the symptoms are opposite to these, and when it deviates forward, it extends the leg completely, except that he cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he attempts to walk, he is unable to do so forward.
It causes urinary retention, swelling in the thigh, and when walking, he bears weight on his heels. When the jaw deviates backward, it does not extend the knee or bend it without first bending the thigh, and his leg is also shorter than the other.
The thigh is relaxed, and the head of the femur is clearly in the position of the hip belt. As for the correction of this type of jaw, if it has been old and attached to its owner and has not attempted to return to its proper place, remaining in its condition, then there is no treatment at all.
One should not attempt to treat it, but if the jaw is recently dislocated and of any of the four types, then hurry to twist the joint, stretch it inward and outward, and move it right and left. Perhaps...
Return and do not require other treatment, if not, then you should consider a strong aid to extend his leg from below, either by hand or by a cloth bound above the knee on his leg, and another helper extends him from above.
He should insert his hands from under his armpits, then tighten a soft cloth at the base of the thigh, and another helper, a third, holds the end of the cloth, either extending it from the front, from the groin to the shoulder, or from behind, towards the back.
They should all be extended at once until the patient raises his body from the ground and remains suspended, for this type of extension is common to all four cases. If the jaw returns as we have said, otherwise each case requires the specific treatment I have mentioned.
As for its specific return, if the jaw is drawn in, the patient should lie on his right side, then a cloth should be wrapped at the base of the thigh between the head of the femur and the place below the groin.
Then the strap is extended above, from the groin to the top of the body, towards the shoulder, and another strong helper uses both his arms to embrace the thick part of the thigh of the patient and extends it outward with a strong pull, returning it to its place.
This is the easiest type of treatment among all other treatments that aim to restore this member, if it is impossible for you or it does not respond to this type of treatment at all, then both legs of the patient should be bound with a strong yet soft bandage around the ankles and knees.
It should be positioned so that each one is separated by the width of four fingers, and the affected leg should be extended further than the other by two fingers. Then the patient should be suspended from a beam in the house, at a distance of two arms' length from the ground.
Then you command a strong boy to embrace the thigh, while another boy holds onto the patient, and the latter pushes the former with force. The joint will quickly return to its place. If it dislocates completely, a special effort will be required to reduce it.
The patient should lie on the bed, as described for the hunchback, with a bandage tightly wrapped around his affected leg and chest. Two beams are placed, one at each end of the bed, with an additional beam across the middle, all secured firmly.
A damp cloth should be wrapped around them to prevent any harm, with a beam placed between his thighs to prevent him from sinking downward during the procedure. Each assistant should then pull towards their respective side, while the doctor adjusts the hip with his hands, and if it returns to its place.
And if it is decided to dislocate it forward, the patient's leg should be extended once while he lies on his healthy side, as mentioned regarding the humped rib.
The surgeon places the palm of his right hand on the affected thigh and presses it with his left hand, but the pressure should be extended downwards towards the knee, if the dislocation is to the rear, the patient should not extend downward.
He should be raised above the ground, rather, he should be placed on a solid surface, and as mentioned, his hip joint should be dislocated outward while he lies face down on the bench with his ligaments tightened.
As mentioned earlier, the dislocation should be corrected by using a board on the area where the joint has moved, once the hip joint returns to its proper place, the sign of its return will be clear, which is when the patient's leg extends.
If you see both legs level and the patient is able to contract and extend their leg without difficulty, then know that the joint has returned to its proper place. At this point, align the femurs, secure the bandage, and tightly wrap a compress around it so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient should rest for three or four days, then loosen the bandage and check the leg. If they are equal in size, know that the fracture is stable, release the patient to walk, but if you see any signs of relaxation, be cautious.
Then, dress and bind it as you initially tightened it, leaving it for three more days, then loosen it and let the patient walk slowly on it for several days until it strengthens, God willing.
Chapter II and III.
On the treatment of knee dislocation.
The knee dislocates in three ways: inward, outward, and downward (i.e., backward), but never forward. The indication of its dislocation is to order the patient to draw their leg towards their thigh.
If it does not adhere to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated. To reduce it, sit the patient down with their legs extended if they can do so, and have a servant sit behind them, grasping their midsection and gently leaning them backward slightly, then you will sit on their thighs.
Press your back against his, placing the patient's leg between your legs, then grasp their knee with both hands and interlace your fingers around it. Press firmly with your hands while another assistant extends the patient's leg until the knee returns to its socket.
The sign of its healing is when the leg adheres to the thigh in a soft, non-forced manner, then bandage it and adhere the leg to the thigh, then tie them all together with a bandage for three or four days, then release them, and do not use walking except for a few days until it strengthens.
If it is not possible for her to return as described, otherwise use the strong traction with bandages that we have described in the treatment of the hip until she returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
In the treatment of the ankle joint.
The ankle joint may come loose partially or completely, and its dislocation can be either inward or outward. The sign of its dislocation is that you will see the ankle bone protruding and prominent towards the side it has moved to.
As for its treatment, it is easy to reduce it by gently pulling with the hands and aligning it until it returns. However, if it dislocates completely, the patient should sit down, and a strong servant should hold him from behind, in the middle of his back, to help reduce the joint.
Then, hold his foot with your right hand at the top and your left hand at the bottom in the area of the ankle. Pull the foot towards you with your right hand twice, then extend it with your left hand, gently pushing the foot's front towards the thigh without force.
Make these two movements as described, and then in the third instance, push the foot towards the leg while pulling on the heel. If it returns once or twice after following this method and you observe the foot to be level, then repeat the procedure; otherwise, begin again.
If the foot does not return as described, place the patient on their back on the ground and insert a peg firmly into the earth between their thighs, wrapping it with cloth to prevent injury, then have an assistant secure the patient's thigh.
Then, have a second assistant extend the patient's leg either by hand or with a strap tied around the patient's neck, extending it against the first assistant's pull while the peg remains between the patient's thighs to prevent the body from tilting downward. The physician then adjusts the jaw with their hands.
Have a third assistant hold the healthy leg downward, and if the jaw returns quickly, indicating its proper alignment, then secure the bandage and splint tightly with straps, binding the foot to the leg below the ankle, while taking care to avoid the nerve located behind the heel.
To prevent the bandage from being too tight and causing injury, leave it for two or three days. If the bandage relaxes, tighten it again, then release it on the fourth day or after thirty days, after which the patient should refrain from walking for forty days.
If one attempts to walk before this period has passed, there is a risk that the jaw will dislocate and become damaged, and it will not respond to treatment afterwards. If a hot swelling appears, one should use what we have described in our treatments and bandages elsewhere until it disappears.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
In the treatment of a man's finger dislocation.
One should straighten what has come apart with a slight stretch, without force, which is easy to do. If the dislocation is in one of the toes, the patient should place their foot on a level surface.
Or, while he stands on a board, you should place your foot on the joint and press down firmly with the other foot until it aligns and appears straightened without any bumps.
Then, place a board under the sole of his foot, covering the entire foot, with two heads, and secure it tightly for three days. After that, release it and protect his foot from walking for many days until it strengthens and you are confident that it will not relapse, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
In Types of Jaw That Are Affected by Injury or Fracture or Both.
When such conditions occur, whether due to injury, fracture, or both, their treatment and reduction must be handled with care, as they often lead to death.
Therefore, one should not undertake such treatment unless they are skilled in the craft, experienced, gentle, compassionate, deliberate, and cautious, and begin with drugs that soothe hot swelling only and carefully observe the patient.
O God, save us from harm except as we hope for safety with a mild illness and some hope appears to You therein; then reduce it at once in Your time before the hot swelling occurs.
If the member returns as intended, use measures to soothe hot swelling and treat the wound with suitable drying ointments. If the jaw is fractured and there are protruding fragments of bone, then extract them. Follow what we have mentioned for simple diseases, as outlined in their respective sections.
Exert your utmost effort and purify yourself from venturing into risky paths, as I have advised you; this will preserve your honor and protect your reputation, God willing.
